Defending the Correct Interpretation of the Matn: Paternity of a Widow's Child
Super-commentator refutes a critique on paternity conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two alternative conditions for establishing paternity of a widow's child and explain why the matn's wording does not require change by refuting the opposing interpretation.

Core Idea
One of two conditions suffices: birth within two years or heirs' acknowledgment; the matn's wording stands without change.

This lesson examines a refined intra-Hanafi dispute about the correct reading of the matn in Al-Wiqayah regarding paternity of a widow's child. The commentator had stated that paternity is established either by birth within two years of the husband's death or by the heirs' acknowledgment. A later glossator, 'Isam al-Din, argued that the matn's expression should be changed because he misinterpreted the phrase 'and a woman in 'iddah' as requiring a different syntactical role. The super-commentator of 'Umdat al-Ri'aya refutes this critique, showing that the original interpretation is coherent and that the conditions are specific to a widow, not a divorcée. By working through the dispute, you will sharpen your ability to resolve apparent textual contradictions within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the substantive ruling
State the two paths that suffice to establish paternity for a widow's child according to the commentator: (1) birth within two years of death, or (2) acknowledgment by the heirs. Note that only one is needed; both are not required.
This is the agreed-upon ruling within the Hanafi school.
2
Understand 'Isam al-Din's critique
Examine 'Isam's claim that the commentator misread the matn by treating 'and a woman in 'iddah' as a conjunct to 'a woman in revocable divorce', requiring a different predicate structure. 'Isam argued that the matn's expression must be changed.
'Isam proposed that 'a woman in 'iddah whose pregnancy has appeared' is the subject and 'for if he denies' is the predicate, applying to divorcées and married women.
3
Trace the super-commentator's first objection
The super-commentator argues that 'Isam's interpretation is forced and contradicts the context (siyaq wa sabaq). The term 'woman in 'iddah' in the matn must refer to a widow because the conditions of two years and heirs' acknowledgment are specific to widows, not divorcées.
A divorcée's paternity rules differ: revocable divorce accepts paternity even after two years as long as 'iddah is not acknowledged to have ended.
4
Trace the second objection
Even if one tried to use the rhetorical device of istikhdam (double reference) to make 'woman in 'iddah' apply to both a divorcée and a widow for different parts of the sentence, the reference to 'he denies' in the predicate refers to the husband, which cannot occur when the husband is dead. This disproves the connection.
The husband's denial is impossible for a widow, so the predicate cannot apply to her, leaving the matn's original interpretation intact.
5
Conclude the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical ruling remains the same: paternity is established by either condition. The dispute is purely about the correct grammatical reading of the matn. The super-commentator's defense upholds the standard Hanafi position without changing the text.
Thus, the matn's wording is fine as is, and no alteration is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشارح: واعلم أن ثبوت النسب لولد المعتدة المتوفى عنها زوجها يكون بأحد أمرين: إما بأن تلد لأقل من سنتين من يوم الوفاة، وإما بإقرار الورثة. ثم قال: فإن لم يعلم المدة ولا الإقرار، فإن أقر الورثة يعتبر. فقلنا: أحدهما كاف: أي المدة أو الإقرار. فحينئذ إن أقر الورثة يعتبر، فيجب أن تغير عبارة «الوقاية».
قوله: يثبت النسب؛ ولا يشترط فيه إقرار الورثة.
صور:
[۲] قوله: وإن لم يعلم...الخ؛ هاهنا أحدها: أن يعلم أنها ولدت لأقل من سنتين، وحينئذ لا حاجة إلى إقرار الورثة. وثانيها: أن يعلم أنها ولدت لسنتين أو أكثر، وحينئذ لا يثبت النسب من المتوفى وإن أقر الورثة؛ لظهور بطلانه. وثالثها: أن لا يعلم هذا ولا ذاك، وحينئذ يعتبر إقرار الورثة، فعُلِمَ من هاهنا أنه لا يشترط في ثبوت النسب وجود الأمرين؛ أي الولادة لأقل من سنتين وإقرار الورثة.
[٣] قوله: فيجب؛ تفريع على ما أثبته سابقاً من كفاية أحد الأمرين لثبوتِ نسب ولد المعتدة التي توفى عنها زوجها؛ أي أقرّ الورثة، أو ثبوت كون الولادة لأقل من سنتين.
وقال الفاضل عصام الدين الاسفرائيني  في «حواشيه»: «جعل الشارح له قوله: «ومعتدة»، عطفاً على قوله: «معتدة الرجعي»، على أنها مضاف إليه للولد، فزل قدماه، ولم يقدر على النهوض، وظنّ أن بيان المتن هو المنقوص، وحكم بوجوب تغيير عبارته، ولم يتأمل حتى لا يقدم على جسارته. ولو كان الأمر كذلك لا تصح عبارة المتن بما أتى به من التغيير؛ إذ قوله: ومعتدة لا يصلح مقابلاً لما عطفت عليه؛ إذ هذه المعتدة واحدة من هؤلاء المذكورات، وكان ينبغي أن يقول: وثبوت نسب هؤلاء إذا أنكر الزوج أو غيره بأحد هذه الأمور. فنقول: ومن الله ترجى الوقاية، قوله: ومعتدة ظهر حبلها»، مبتدأ خبره قوله: «فإن جحد»، والفاء في الخبر؛ لأنها نكرة موصوفة بفعل، ومعنى هذه المسألة: إنّ المعتدة الموصوفة بإحدى هذه الصفاتِ من ظهور الحبل أو إقرار الزوج بالحبل، أو ثبوتِ ولادتها بحجّة تامة، بأن تقوم الحجّة التامة على أنها ولدت أو ولدت لأقل من سنتين، وعلم تلك الولادة بإقرار الورثة. والمنكوحة التي أتت به لستة أشهر؛ أي التي هي غير مطلقة بل نكاحها قائم، فإن جحد ولادتها لهذا المولود يثبت تعينها بشهادة امرأة واحدة من غير اشتراط هذه الصفات». انتهى.
أقول: قد سكت أكثر الناظرين في هذا المقام عن الحل والتنقيح، ولم يأتوا بما يفيد التوضيح، والعصام وإن أتى بما خلت عنه أكثر الحواشي، لكنّه لم يعصم عن زلّة الأقدام، فإن كلامه مخدوش عندي بوجوه:
أحدها: إنّ فيما اختاره تكلّفاً واضحاً يأبى عنه السياق والسباق.
وثانيها: إنّ المراد بلفظ المعتدة الذي جعله مبتدأ لا يخلو إما أن يكون المراد به معتدة الطلاق فقط أو أعم منها ومن معتدة الوفاة، فإن أريد الأول فمع قطع النظر عن كون هذا التخصيص بلا مخصص لا يصح قوله: أو ولدت لأقل من سنتين وأقر الورثة بها؛ لأنّ هذه المدة إنما تعتبر في المتوفى عنها زوجها لا في المطلقة، فإن في معتدة الرجعي يثبت النسب وإن جاءت به لأكثر من سنتين ما لم تقرّ بانقضاء العدة، وفي المبتوتة يثبت وإن جاءت لتمام سنتين على ما مر تفصيله. وأيضاً لا يصح قوله: وأقرّ الورثة، فإنّ إقرار الورثة إنما يحتاج إليه في معتدة الوفاة، وأين الورثة من حياة الزوج.
فإن قلت: المراد بالمعتدة في ذلك القول هو معتدّة الطلاق، والمراد بضمير ولدت: معتدة الوفاة، بطريق صنعة الاستخدام.
قلت: هذا مع كونه لا يتبادر الفهم إليه يبطلُ بما يبطل به الشق الثاني؛ أي المدة الأعم من لفظ المعتدة، وهو إن قوله الآتي: فإن جحد ولادتها، ضمير الجحود فيه راجع إلى الزوج، بقرينة قوله بعده، فيلا عن إن نفاه، وبشهادة قوله السابق: أقرّ به الزوج أو سكت.
وثالثها: إنه ظاهر كلامه الآتي سياقاً وسباقاً كما بيناه آنفاً يشهد بأن قوله: فإن جحد ... الخ بيان لإنكار الزوج، ولا يتصوّر هذا في صورة وفاته، فلا يرتبط الخبر بالمبتدأ.
The commentator said: Know that the establishment of paternity for the child of a widow in ‘iddah is achieved by one of two matters: either that she gives birth less than two years from the day of death, or by the acknowledgment of the heirs. Then he said: If the period and the acknowledgment are not known, then if the heirs acknowledge, it is considered. So we said: One of the two is sufficient: i.e., the period or the acknowledgment. Then if the heirs acknowledge, it is considered; therefore the expression of Al-Wiqayah must be changed. His statement: Paternity is established; and the acknowledgment of the heirs is not a condition for it. Cases: [2] His statement: And if it is not known... etc.; here, first: that it is known that she gave birth in less than two years, in which case there is no need for the heirs' acknowledgment. Second: that it is known that she gave birth after two years or more, in which case paternity is not established from the deceased even if the heirs acknowledge, because its falsehood is apparent. Third: that neither this nor that is known, in which case the heirs' acknowledgment is considered. So it is understood from here that the existence of both matters—i.e., birth within less than two years and the heirs' acknowledgment—is not required for establishing paternity. [3] His statement: Therefore it is necessary; it is a branching from what he previously established of the sufficiency of one of the two matters for establishing the paternity of the child of a widow in ‘iddah; i.e., either the heirs acknowledge, or it is established that the birth was within less than two years. And the excellent scholar ‘Isam al-Din al-Isfarayini said in his glosses: "The commentator made his statement ‘and a woman in ‘iddah’ as a conjunct to his statement ‘a woman in revocable divorce’, considering it a possessive construction for the child, so his feet slipped, and he was unable to get up, and he thought that the clarification of the text was deficient, and he ruled that its expression must be changed. And if it were so, the expression of the text would not be valid with the change he brought; for his statement ‘and a woman in ‘iddah’ is not suitable as a counterpart to what it was conjoined to; for this woman in ‘iddah is one of those mentioned, and he should have said: and the establishment of the paternity of these when the husband or others denies by one of these matters. So we say: From Allah we hope for protection. His statement ‘and a woman in ‘iddah whose pregnancy has appeared’ is a subject whose predicate is his statement ‘for if he denies’, and the ‘fa’ is in the predicate because it is an indefinite noun qualified by a verb, and the meaning of this issue is: The woman in ‘iddah described by one of these descriptions—appearance of pregnancy, or the husband's acknowledgment of the pregnancy, or the establishment of her giving birth by a complete proof—in that a complete proof establishes that she gave birth or gave birth within less than two years, and that birth is known by the heirs' acknowledgment. And the woman married (non-divorced) who brings a child after six months—i.e., she is not divorced but her marriage is intact—then if he denies her giving birth to this child, its establishment is confirmed by the testimony of one woman without requiring these descriptions." End of his words. I say: Most of the viewers in this place have remained silent about the solution and the refinement, and they have not brought what benefits clarification; and ‘Isam, although he brought what most glosses lack, was not protected from slipping of the feet, for his speech is flawed in my view in several respects: First: What he chose involves an obvious affectation that the context and preceding discourse reject. Second: The meaning of the word ‘woman in ‘iddah’ which he made a subject is either limited to a woman in ‘iddah of divorce or includes both her and a widow in ‘iddah. If the first is intended, then aside from the fact that this specification is without a specifier, his statement ‘or she gave birth within less than two years and the heirs acknowledged it’ is invalid, because this period is only considered for the widow, not the divorcée; for in the case of revocable divorce, paternity is established even if she brings the child after more than two years as long as she does not acknowledge the expiration of the ‘iddah, and in the case of irrevocable divorce, it is established even if she brings it after exactly two years as per the detailed explanation given earlier. Also his statement ‘and the heirs acknowledged’ is invalid, because the heirs' acknowledgment is only needed for the case of a widow; where are the heirs when the husband is alive? If you say: What is meant by ‘woman in ‘iddah’ in that statement is the divorcée, and what is meant by the pronoun ‘she gave birth’ is the widow, by way of the rhetorical device of ‘istikhdām’ (double reference). I say: This, besides not being readily understood, is invalidated by what invalidates the second aspect—i.e., the period being more general than the word ‘woman in ‘iddah’—and that is that his subsequent statement ‘for if he denies her giving birth’, the pronoun of denial refers back to the husband, as indicated by his later statement ‘or if he denies it’, and by his earlier statement ‘the husband acknowledged it or remained silent’. Third: It is apparent from his later statement contextually and sequentially, as we explained above, that his statement ‘for if he denies...’ is an explanation of the husband's denial, and this cannot be conceived in the case of his death, so the predicate is not connected to the subject.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiyah) within the Super-commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'aya. The author, after establishing that for a widow's child in ‘iddah, paternity is established either by birth within two years of the husband's death or by the heirs' acknowledgment, addresses a critique from Fadil 'Isam al-Din al-Isfarayini. 'Isam argued that the commentator (the author of Sharh al-Wiqayah) misconstrued the matn of Al-Wiqayah by treating 'and a woman in ‘iddah' as a conjunct to 'a woman in revocable divorce', which would make it a possessive construction referring to the child, and thus the matn's expression needed change. 'Isam proposed that the correct interpretation is that 'a woman in ‘iddah whose pregnancy has appeared' is the subject (mubtada') and 'for if he denies' is the predicate (khabar), applying to a woman in divorce or a married woman who gives birth after six months.

The author refutes 'Isam with three objections: (1) 'Isam's interpretation is forced and contradicts the context. (2) If 'woman in ‘iddah' means only a divorcée, then the conditions of birth within two years and heirs' acknowledgment are irrelevant for divorcées, as those rules apply only to widows. The period of two years is specific to a widow, and heirs' acknowledgment only applies when the husband is dead. So 'Isam's interpretation conflates two different legal scenarios. (3) The subsequent phrase 'for if he denies' clearly refers to the husband's denial, which cannot happen if the husband is dead, so the predicate would not connect to a widow. Therefore, the original interpretation of the commentator is correct: the passage deals with a widow, and the matn's wording is fine as is. The author thus defends the standard Hanafi position that one of two conditions suffices, without requiring both, and clarifies that the acknowledgment of heirs is only relevant when the birth time is unknown.

Key Terms
معتدة المتوفى عنها زوجها (mu‘taddat al-mutawaffā ‘anhā zawjuhā): A widow observing the ‘iddah period after her husband's death.
إقرار الورثة (iqrār al-wurathā’): The acknowledgment by the deceased's heirs that the child is from the deceased.
أقل من سنتين (aqall min sanatayn): A period of less than two years from the husband's death; the maximum duration of pregnancy according to Hanafi view.
استخدام (istikhdām): A rhetorical device where a word is used in two different senses in the same context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
For a widow's child, paternity is established by either birth within two years of the husband's death or the heirs' acknowledgment; the matn's wording does not require change.

Maintenance Obligation and the Effect of Nushuz
Defining nushuz and explaining its effect on maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on maintenance obligation from the marriage contract and identify the conditions under which maintenance is dropped due to nushuz.

Core Idea
Maintenance is wajib from the contract because it compensates the husband's right of confinement; nushuz cancels past prescribed maintenance.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on spousal maintenance (nafaqa). The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies that maintenance becomes obligatory immediately upon the marriage contract, even if the wife remains at her father's house, because maintenance is compensation (jaza') for the husband's right to confine his wife. However, this obligation is suspended if the wife becomes nashiza (rebellious) by leaving the house without right. We will explore the definition of nushuz, its legal effect, and an important exception for court-ordered debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage states that maintenance is due even if the wife is in her father's house or ill. Note the principle: maintenance is compensation for the right of confinement (ihtibas).
This principle is the key proof for the ruling.
2
Define nushuz
Understand the linguistic and legal meaning: a woman who leaves her husband's house without permission and without legal right.
Leaving includes actual departure and constructive refusal to move or allow entry.
3
Determine the effect of nushuz on past maintenance
Past prescribed maintenance (e.g., monthly amounts) is cancelled, but not maintenance that the wife has borrowed by court order.
This distinction is critical for practical application.
4
Distinguish between actual and constructive leaving
Leaving includes both physical departure and constructive acts like refusing to move to the husband's house or preventing him from entering.
Constructive nushuz is treated like actual nushuz.
5
Note the intra-madhhab difference
Abu Yusuf held that maintenance is not due until the wife moves to the husband's house, but the fatwa is on the first position.
The relied-upon view is that maintenance starts from the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو هي في بيت أبيها ، أو مرضت في بيت الزوج. لا لناشزة خرجت من بيته بغير حق.
[١] قوله : أو مرضت ؛ يعني إذا مرضت في بيت زوجها ابتداءً بعد ما سلمت نفسها تجب النفقة، وكذا إذا مرضت في بيتها ثم انتقلت إلى بيت الزوج أو بقيت في منزلها ولم تمنع نفسها عن النقلة ؛ وذلك لأنَّ النفقة جزاء الاحتباس، وهو موجود في جميع الصور. كذا في «الفتح»(۳).
[٢] قوله : لا لناشزة ؛ شروعٌ في بيان من لا تجب نفقته أو تسقط.
والنشور : لغة العصيان والمخالفة، وشرعاً : الناشزة المرأة التي تخرج من بيت زوجها بدون إذنه بغير حق شرعي، فلا تجب نفقتها إلى أن تعود وتترك النشوز وتسقط بالنشوز النفقة المفروضة الماضية لا المستدانة، يعني إذا كان لها عليه نفقة أشهر مفروضة ثم نشزت سقطت تلك النفقة ، بخلاف ما إذا أمرها القاضي بالاستدانة فاستدانت عليه، فإنّها لا تسقط. كذا في «الذخيرة» وغيرها. وقد ذكر في بعض الفتاوى سقوط المهر أيضاً بالنشوز، ولا عبرة به، فإنّه خلافُ الرواية والدراية.
[٣] قوله : خرجت من بيته ؛ الخروج أعم من أن يكون حقيقياً أو حكمياً، فيدخل فيه ما لو طلب الزوج نقلتها من بيت أبيها وامتنعت منها، وكذا ما لو كان المنزل لها فمنعته من الدخول عليها ، بخلاف ما إذا خرجت من بيت الغصب أو أبت المقام معه في
And if she is in her father's house, or she falls ill in the husband's house. Not for a rebellious one who left his house without right. [1] His statement: 'or she falls ill' means if she falls ill in her husband's house initially after she surrendered herself, maintenance is obligatory. Similarly, if she falls ill in her house then moves to the husband's house or remains in her house and does not prevent herself from moving; because maintenance is the compensation for confinement, which exists in all these cases. As in al-Fath (3). [2] His statement: 'not for a rebellious one' begins explanation of whom maintenance is not obligatory or is dropped. Nushuz linguistically means disobedience and opposition. Legally, the rebellious woman is the one who leaves her husband's house without his permission without a legal right. So her maintenance is not obligatory until she returns and abandons rebellion. And past prescribed maintenance is dropped by rebellion, but not the borrowed one. Meaning, if she had prescribed maintenance for months then rebelled, that maintenance is dropped. But if the judge ordered her to borrow and she borrowed against him, it is not dropped. As in al-Dhakhira and others. And it is mentioned in some fatwas that the dower is also dropped by rebellion, but no attention is paid to that, for it is contrary to transmitted reports and discernment. [3] His statement: 'left his house' — leaving is broader, whether actual or constructive, so it includes the case where the husband asks her to move from her father's house and she refuses, as well as the case where the house is hers and she prevents him from entering it. Unlike if she leaves from a usurped house or she refuses to reside with him in...

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement defining when maintenance is obligatory and when it is removed due to nushuz (rebellion). The matn states that maintenance is due from the moment of the marriage contract even if the wife remains in her father's house, and even if she falls ill in the husband's house. The exception is a nashiza woman who leaves without right. The commentator expands: maintenance is compensation for the husband's right to confine his wife (ihtibas). This right exists from the contract, so maintenance is due immediately unless the wife prevents it by nushuz. Nushuz is defined as leaving the husband's house without permission and without legal cause. The effect of nushuz is that it cancels past prescribed maintenance that was already owed (such as monthly installments), but not maintenance that she has already taken by court-ordered borrowing. The commentator also notes that some incorrectly extend the effect to the dower, but that is rejected. The definition of 'leaving' includes both physical departure and constructive departure (e.g., refusing to move to his house or preventing him from entering).

Key Terms
ناشزة (nashiza): A woman who leaves her husband's house without his permission without a legitimate legal right.
جزاء الاحتباس (jaza' al-ihtibas): Legal principle that maintenance is the compensation for the husband's right to confine his wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to past prescribed maintenance if the wife becomes nashiza?
A) It remains owed.
B) It is cancelled.
C) It is cancelled only if she leaves the house permanently.
D) It is reduced by half.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, maintenance is wajib from the marriage contract; nushuz cancels past prescribed maintenance but not court-ordered debt.

Master's Obligation of Nafaqah for Slave and Its Enforcement
Ruling on master's nafaqah duty towards slave and enforcement measures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a master's obligation to provide nafaqah for a slave and explain the enforcement mechanism when the master fails.

Core Idea
Nafaqah of a slave is wajib; if the master refuses, the slave earns or the court forces sale.

This passage from Sharh al-Wiqayah establishes the master's duty to maintain his slave and outlines what happens if he fails. The ruling is grounded in a hadith from Bukhari and Muslim, and the text distinguishes the slave's legal entitlement from animals and spouses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
Determine the act (providing nafaqah) and its hukm level (wajib). Note that it applies to the master (owner or possessor).
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the hadith cited and classify it as ahad. Identify the imperative phrasing that indicates obligation.
Hadith: 'Your brothers are your dependents...'
3
Trace the enforcement hierarchy
If master refuses: (1) slave earns if able; (2) if unable, court orders master to sell; (3) if master refuses, judge sells.
The judge has general authority over wealth.
4
Compare with adjacent cases
Understand why the same enforcement is not applied to spouses (wife's nafaqah becomes debt) or animals (diyanatan only).
Slaves are ahl al-istihqaq (entitled to rights).
5
Extract the scope of nafaqah
The nafaqah must cover sufficient food, clothing, and lodging per local custom; cannot be limited to covering private parts nor extravagant.
Recommended to treat slaves equally; female slave gets extra for enjoyment.

ومن له أب حاضر ، ومَن ليس له أب حاضر، والأمة المتزوجة ما لم يبوئها إلى منزل الزوج. كذا في «البحر» وغيره.
وذُكِرَ في «الفتاوى»: إن نفقة المملوكِ بقدر كفايته من غالب قوتِ البلد وإدامه، وكذا الكسوة، ولا يجوز الاقتصار فيها على ستر العورة، ولا يلزم السيد أن يعطيه زيادةً على الحاجة، ويستحب أن يسوي بين العبيد والجواري، ويزيد جاريته الاستمتاع في الكسوة للعرف. والأصل في هذا الباب حديث: «إخوانكم خولكم جعلهم الله تحت أيديكم ، فمن كان أخوه تحت يده فليطعمه مما يأكل ويلبس مما يلبس)، أخرجه البخاري ومسلم.
وفي «الموطأ»: عن عثمان الله : لا تكلّفوا الصغير الكسب فيسرق، ولا الأمة غير ذات الصنعة فتكتسب الفرجها، وفي الباب أخبار أخر أيضاً.
[١]قوله : على سيده ؛ أي المالك له أو من هو في يده، فنفقة العبد المغصوب على الغاصب، ونفقة العبد المرهون على الراهن، ونفقة العبد المبيع على البائع ما دام في يده، هو الصحيح، ذكره في «القنية»: وقيل : على المشتري، ونفقة المبيع بشرط الخيار على مَن له الملك في العبدِ وقت الوجوب، وقيل: على البائع.
[٢]قوله : فإن أبى ؛ أي إن امتنع السيّد من الإنفاق على العبد، كسب العبد وأنفق منه على نفسه، فإن كان عارفاً بصناعتِهِ يكسب به ، وإلا فيؤجر نفسه من صناع.
[٣] قوله : وإن عجز ؛ أي المملوك عن الكسب، بأن كان جارية عاجزة عن الكسب أو عبداً زمناً عاجزاً. أمر ببيعه.
[١] قوله : أمر ؛ بصيغة المجهول، والضمير إلى السيد ؛ أي أمره القاضي جبراً ؛ أي بإخراجه عن ملكه ، وإن لم يكن بيعاً بل هبة ، فإن امتنع حبسه القاضي حتى ينفق عليه أو يبيعه، فإن لم يفد ذلك باعه القاضي ؛ لأنّ القاضي له ولاية عامة، فيتولى إصلاح أموالهم، ويهتم بعدم إضاعتها.

قلت : لا ضرورة هناك إلى التفريق ؛ لأنّ نفقة الزوجة تصير ديـنـاً عليه، ولا كذلك المملوك، فإنّه لا يثبت له دين على مولاه، فيحتاج إلى أن يأمره القاضي بالبيع ، أو يبعه بنفسه عند ظهور تمرد المولى.

Upon his master. If he refuses, the slave earns and spends [on himself]. And if he is unable, [the master] is ordered to sell him. And [the same applies to] one who has a present father, and one who does not have a present father, and the married female slave as long as he has not prepared a home for her in the husband's house. Thus in 'al-Bahr' and others. And it is mentioned in 'al-Fatawa': The maintenance of a slave is according to his sufficiency from the predominant food of the country and its condiment, and similarly clothing. It is not permissible to restrict it to covering the private parts, nor is the master obliged to give him more than needed. It is recommended to treat male and female slaves equally, and to increase the clothing of his female slave for enjoyment due to custom. The basis of this chapter is the hadith: 'Your brothers are your dependents; Allah has placed them under your authority. So whoever has his brother under his authority, let him feed him from what he eats and clothe him from what he wears' – related by Bukhari and Muslim. And in 'al-Muwatta': from Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him): 'Do not burden a young one to earn, lest he steal; nor a female slave without a skill, lest she earn through her private parts.' And there are other reports in this chapter. [1] His saying: 'upon his master' – meaning the owner or the one in whose possession he is. So the maintenance of a usurped slave is upon the usurper; maintenance of a pledged slave is upon the pledgor; maintenance of a sold slave is upon the seller as long as it remains in his possession – that is the correct view, mentioned in 'al-Qunyah'. And it is said: upon the buyer. And maintenance of a slave sold with a condition of option is upon the one who has ownership at the time of obligation; and it is said: upon the seller. [2] His saying: 'if he refuses' – meaning if the master refrains from spending on the slave, the slave earns and spends from it on himself. If he knows a craft, he earns by it; otherwise, he hires himself out to a craftsman. [3] His saying: 'and if he is unable' – meaning the slave is unable to earn, such as a female slave incapable of earning or a chronically ill male slave unable [to earn]; [the master] is ordered to sell him. [1] His saying: 'ordered' – in the passive voice, and the pronoun refers to the master; meaning the judge orders him compulsorily, i.e., to remove him from his ownership, even if it is not a sale but a gift. If he refuses, the judge imprisons him until he spends on him or sells him. If that does not avail, the judge sells him, because the judge has general authority; he undertakes the rectification of their wealth and is concerned to avoid wasting it. If you say: Why is separation not made between spouses when he is unable to spend, just as a slave is sold when the master does not spend? I say: There is no need for separation there because the wife's maintenance becomes a debt upon him, and not so with the slave, for he has no debt established against his master. Therefore, the judge needs to order him to sell, or to sell himself when the master's recalcitrance appears. If you say: What about animals? Why is not their owner ordered to sell them when he does not spend on them, given the hadith: 'Do not torture Allah's creation' related by Abu Dawood, and the hadith: 'Allah has forbidden you to waste wealth' related by Bukhari? I say: Because maintenance of other animals is obligatory only as a matter of religious duty (diyanatan), not as a legal obligation (qadaan); for they are not entitled to rights, unlike the male and female slave, for they are entitled to rights.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the master's obligation to provide maintenance (nafaqah) for his slave, including a discussion of enforcement mechanisms and comparisons with other dependents. The ruling states: it is obligatory upon the master to provide for his slave. If the master refuses, the slave is permitted to earn for himself and spend on his own needs. If the slave is unable to earn (e.g., a female slave with no skill or a chronically ill male slave), then the master is ordered by the court to sell the slave. The passage then expands on various scenarios: the maintenance of a slave in different situations (usurped, pledged, sold with or without option) and the extent of maintenance – sufficient food and clothing according to local custom, not merely covering the private parts, with a recommended increase for female slaves' clothing due to enjoyment. The authority is based on the hadith from Bukhari and Muslim about treating slaves and serving, and the athar from Uthman ibn Affan in al-Muwatta. The commentary resolves potential objections: first, why not order separation between spouses when maintenance fails? The answer: because a wife's maintenance becomes a debt on the husband, enforceable after divorce; a slave cannot have a debt on the master, so sale is the remedy. Second, why are animals not sold when neglected? Because maintenance of animals is a religious duty (diyanatan) not legally enforceable (qadaan), since animals are not legal persons capable of entitlement. The judge's authority to force the sale stems from his general guardianship over wealth. The passage thus clarifies the unique nature of the master-slave relationship in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance including food, clothing, and lodging sufficient for the slave's needs according to local custom.
مملوك (mamluk): A male or female slave owned by a master; in this context, entitled to maintenance from the master.
ديانة (diyanatan): Religiously obligatory but not enforceable by a court; used here to describe maintenance of animals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what happens if a master refuses to provide maintenance and the slave is unable to earn?
A) The slave is set free immediately.
B) The master is imprisoned until he sells the slave.
C) The slave is given a loan from the public treasury.
D) The court orders the master to sell the slave, and if he refuses, the judge sells him.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely enforces the master's nafaqah for slaves through sale if necessary, based on the slave's legal capacity to be owed rights.

Conditions and Words of Emancipation
Ruling with proof and expansion on words of emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for valid emancipation (free, legally responsible owner, owned slave) and distinguish between explicit words (no intent needed judicially) and implicit words (intent required).

Core Idea
Emancipation is valid from a free, legally responsible owner; explicit words effect freedom without need for intent.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah opens the Book of Emancipation. It establishes that emancipation is a recommended act with great reward, then sets the conditions for its validity: the manumitter must be free, legally responsible (sane, adult), and the slave must be his property. The passage then divides the words of emancipation into explicit (صريح) and implicit (كناية), with a third annexed category. Understanding this division is crucial because the ruling on whether intent is required differs fundamentally between the types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for valid emancipation
From the passage, list the three conditions: the manumitter must be free, legally responsible (sane and adult), and the slave must be owned by him. Note the proofs: hadith and Quran for recommendation, and hadith 'No emancipation for what one does not own' for the ownership condition.
The condition of legal responsibility excludes the insane and minors; guardians cannot emancipate on their behalf.
2
Understand the explicit words (sarih) and their ruling
Explicit words are those coined for emancipation, like 'you are free', 'I emancipate you', etc. They effect emancipation even without intent judicially. If the speaker intended something else (e.g., 'free from work'), he is not believed in court but is believed between himself and Allah.
Examples from text: 'You are free', 'You are manumitted', 'You are emancipated', 'I have emancipated you'.
3
Distinguish implicit words (kinayah) and annexed category
Implicit words require intent to effect emancipation; without intent, no emancipation occurs. The annexed category (mulhaq bil-sarih) includes phrases like 'I have given you yourself' which are not coined for emancipation but, by usage, do not require intent.
The text cites three categories in some sources: sarih, mulhaq, and kinayah.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a statement like 'O free one!' (explicit), the slave is freed even if the speaker meant to address someone else. Given 'You are free from work' (explicit word with qualifying intent), the slave is freed judicially because the word 'free' is explicit; the qualification is ignored in court.
This illustrates the principle that explicit words operate by their apparent meaning.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the intent requirement
The difference between explicit and implicit words is whether the court accepts a claim of different intent. For explicit words, the court does not accept such a claim; for implicit words, the court requires proof of intent.
This has implications for litigation involving emancipation claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتاق

[١]قوله : كتاب العتاق ؛ مناسبته ببحث الطلاق لكون كلّ منها إسقاطاً وتصرّفاً لازماً، ولما كان الطلاق من متعلقات النكاح ذكر بحثه مع ما يتعلق به بعد بحث النكاح، ثمّ عقبه بذكر مثيله، وعبر بالعتاق دون الإعتاق ؛ ليشمل نحو عتقه بدخوله في ملك ذي رحم محرم منه. ثم العتق والعتاق والعتاقة : بفتح العين، فهنّ مصادر عتق يعتق، والعتق بالكسرِ اسم منه، والعتق في الأصل القوة ، يقال : عتق الطير؛ إذا قوي وطار عن وكره. وفي الشرع : هو عبارة عن قوة حكميّة يصيرُ المرء بها أهلاً للشهادة والقضاء وغيرهما، وبهذا ظهر أنّ العتاق لازم ويتعدّى بالهمزة فيقال : أعتقته فهو معتق وعتيق، ولا يجوز معتوق، فالثلاثي لازم ، والرباعي متعد. كذا في «المصباح المنير، وغيره.

بصريح لفظه بلا نية : كأنتَ حُرّ، أو معتق، أو عتيق، أو أعتقتك لفظه بلا نية : كانت حُرّ، أو معتق ، أو عتيق" ، أو أعتقتك (4) بصريح والعبد لا يملك شيئاً، وأما اشتراط كونه مكلفا ؛ أي عاقلاً بالغاً فلأن المجنون ليس من أهل التصرف، والصبي ليس أهلاً للتصرّف الذي هو ضرر ظاهر، كالاعتاق والطلاق ؛ ولهذا لا يملكه المولى عليه والوصي. كذا في «الهداية» و«البناية"
[١]قوله : بصريح كناية، على ما في «النهاية» و«فتاوى قاضي خان»، فالصريح ما لا يحتاج إلى النية، والكناية ما يحتاج إليها. وثلث القسمة في «جامع المضمرات»، ووجهه : إنّ اللفظ إن وضع لإثباتِ العتق لفظه؛ قال في «كشف الوقاية»: له ألفاظ، فهي إما صريحة وإمّا فهو الصريح وإن لم يوضع، فإن شرط فيه النية فهو الكناية، وإن لم يشترط فهو الملحق بالصريح؛ لعدم اشتراط النية، نحو : وهبتُ لك نفسك. كذا في «شرح أبي المكارم». وفي «الفتاوى» ناقلاً عن «الحاوي القدسي»: أما ألفاظه فثلاثة أنواع : صريح، وملحق به، وكناية، فالصريح : كلفظ الحرية والعتق والولاء وما اشتق منهما، وأنه لا يفتقر إلى النية وصفه به أو أخبر أو نادى؛ كقوله لعبده أو أمته : أنت حر أو معتق أو عتيق أو محرّر، إنّه حرّرتك، أو أعتقتك، أو يا حرّ، أو يا عتيق، أو يا مولى، أو هذا مولاي، ولو نوى بهذه الألفاظ غير العتق لا يصدق قضاء. انتهى.
[٢] قوله : بلا نية؛ أي بلا توقف على نيته، فيعتق بهذه الألفاظ وإن لم ينو عتقه، وكذا لو نوى غير العتق نحو أنت حرّ من العمل لا يصدق قضاء، ويصدق فيما بينه وبين الله، فلا يقع، كما لو قال : عنيتُ بالمولى الناصر. كذا في «الفتح»).
[٣] قوله : أو عتيق؛ ومثله : العتاقُ عليك، أو عتقك علي، فيعتق بلا نية، ولو زاد واجب لم يعتق الجواز وجوبه لكفّارة. كذا في «الظهيرية».
[٤] قوله : أعتقتك؛ ومثله : أعتقك الله؛ لأنّ المعنى أعتقك الله، لا في أعتقك. كذا في «الظهيرية».
Book of Emancipation. (It is valid from a free, legally responsible person.) His saying: Book of Emancipation; its appropriateness with the section on divorce is because each of them is a revocation and a binding disposition. Since divorce is related to marriage, its section was mentioned with what pertains to it after the section on marriage, then he followed it by mentioning its equivalent. He used 'al-ataq' instead of 'al-i'taq' to include the case of one becoming free by entering into the ownership of a blood relative who is prohibited to him. Then al-atuq, al-ataq, and al-ataqah: with fatha on the 'ayn, they are verbal nouns of atuqa ya'tiqu, and al-'itq with kasra is a noun from it. Al-'itq originally means strength; it is said: atuqa al-tayr when it becomes strong and flies from its nest. In sacred law: it is an expression for a legal strength by which a person becomes qualified for testimony, judgeship, and other things. By this it becomes clear that al-ataq is intransitive and becomes transitive with the hamza, so it is said: a'taqtuhu so he is mu'taq and 'atiq, and ma'tuq is not permissible; thus the triliteral is intransitive and the quadriliteral is transitive. Thus in al-Misbah al-Munir and others. His saying: 'it is valid'; i.e., al-ataq is valid when the manumitter is free, sane, and adult. Reports have come about its recommendation, like the hadith: 'Any Muslim who emancipates a believing slave, Allah will emancipate for every limb of his a limb from the Fire,' narrated by the six imams with similar wordings. The Book (Quran) indicates it, namely His saying: 'So contract with them if you know in them good' (24:33), and the Almighty said: 'freeing a neck' (90:13). His saying: 'from a free, legally responsible person'; the condition that the manumitter be free is because emancipation only takes effect on one's own slave; therefore it is not permissible for a man to emancipate another's slave due to the hadith: 'No emancipation for what the son of Adam does not own,' narrated by Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud. With an explicit word without intent: like 'you are free', or 'manumitted', or 'emancipated', or 'I have emancipated you' explicitly and the slave owns nothing. As for the condition that he be legally responsible, i.e., sane and adult, it is because the insane person is not qualified for transactions, and the minor is not qualified for a transaction that is manifest harm, like emancipation and divorce; therefore the guardian and executor do not have this authority. Thus in al-Hidayah and al-Binayah. His saying: 'explicitly' and 'implicitly', according to what is in al-Nihayah and Fatawa Qadikhan, so the explicit is what does not require intent, and the implicit requires it. And a third division in Jami' al-Mudmarat, and its explanation: if the word is coined to establish emancipation, it is explicit; if not, then if intent is required, it is implicit; and if intent is not required, it is annexed to the explicit due to the absence of requiring intent, like 'I have given you yourself.' Thus in Sharh Abi al-Makarim. In al-Fatawa, quoting from al-Hawi al-Qudsi: its words are three types: explicit, annexed to it, and implicit. The explicit: like the words of freedom, emancipation, allegiance, and their derivatives, and it does not require intent whether describing, informing, or calling; like saying to his slave or slave-girl: 'you are free' or 'manumitted' or 'emancipated' or 'freed', or 'I have freed you', or 'I have emancipated you', or 'O free', or 'O emancipated', or 'O client', or 'this is my client'; and if he intends other than emancipation with these words, he is not believed judicially. End. His saying: 'without intent'; i.e., without depending on his intent; so he becomes free with these words even if he did not intend emancipation. Similarly, if he intended other than emancipation, like 'you are free from work', he is not believed judicially, but he is believed between himself and Allah, so it does not occur, as if he said: 'I meant by al-mawla the helper.' Thus in al-Fath. His saying: 'or emancipated'; and its like: 'al-ataq is upon you', or 'your emancipation is upon me', so he becomes free without intent; and if he added 'obligatory', he does not become free due to the permissibility of its being obligatory for expiation. Thus in al-Dhahiriyyah. His saying: 'I have emancipated you'; and its like: 'may Allah emancipate you'; because the meaning is 'may Allah emancipate you', not 'I emancipate you'. Thus in al-Dhahiriyyah.
Source pages p. 589

Commentary
This passage opens Kitab al-Itaq (Book of Emancipation) from the commentary on al-Wiqayah. The commentator first explains why this book follows divorce: both are revocations and binding dispositions. Then he provides linguistic and legal definitions: 'ataq' (emancipation) is a legal strength that makes one qualified for testimony and judgeship. The matn states that emancipation is valid from a free, legally responsible person. The commentary elaborates with proofs: the hadith about the reward for emancipating a believing slave, and Quranic verses (24:33 and 90:13) showing its recommendation. The condition of the manumitter being free is based on the principle that one can only dispose of one's own property; a hadith prohibits emancipating another's slave. The condition of legal responsibility (sane, adult) excludes the insane and minor because they lack capacity for harmful dispositions; guardians do not have this authority. The passage then discusses the words of emancipation. It divides them into explicit (صريح) and implicit (كناية), with some adding a third category 'annexed to explicit' (ملحق بالصريح). Explicit words like 'you are free' or 'I have emancipated you' effect emancipation without intent, even if the speaker intended something else judicially (though he is believed between himself and Allah). Implicit words require intent. The annexed category, like 'I have given you yourself', works without intent but is not originally coined for emancipation. The commentary provides examples and rulings, emphasizing that the judgment is based on the apparent meaning of the words.

Key Terms
العتاق (al-ataq): Emancipation; a verbal noun meaning the act of freeing a slave. Also used for the legal effect of becoming free.
الصريح (al-sarih): Explicit words of emancipation that do not require intent; they effect emancipation by their apparent meaning.
الكناية (al-kinayah): Implicit words that require intent to effect emancipation; without intent, they do not emancipate.
مكلف (mukallaf): Legally responsible person: one who is sane and has reached puberty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for the validity of emancipation?
A) The emancipator must be free.
B) The emancipator must be legally responsible (sane and adult).
C) The emancipator must intend to free the slave.
D) The slave must be owned by the emancipator.

Key Takeaway
Emancipation by explicit words is effective even without intent judicially, but implicit words require intent; always check the conditions of the manumitter (free, legally responsible, owner) first.

The Word 'Mawla' and Emancipation: Explicit Expression
Ruling on 'mawla' as sarih for emancipation with intra-Hanafi and inter-sectarian disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that 'anta mawlay' is sarih for emancipation without intention, identify the contextual specification that makes it explicit, and distinguish the practical consequences for dinan and qada'.

Core Idea
Context (idafa to slave) specifies homonymous 'mawla' to 'mu'tiq', making it sarih without intention.

The word 'mawla' carries many meanings, from helper to patron. In the Hanafi school, when a master addresses his own slave with 'anta mawlay' or 'ya mawlay', this word becomes unambiguous and frees the slave immediately, even without the master intending emancipation. This lesson examines the linguistic and legal reasoning behind this ruling, including an intra-Hanafi objection by al-Atqani and the refutation of a Shi'a counterargument.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the main ruling: 'anta mawlay' and 'ya mawlay' are explicit (sarih) expressions for emancipation and require no intention (niyyah). Emancipation is effective immediately.
This is the majority Hanafi view, relied upon for fatwa.
2
Identify the opposing view within the school
Al-Atqani (a Hanafi scholar) argued that because 'mawla' is homonymous (mushtarak), it cannot be explicit without intention. He would require niyyah for emancipation with this word.
This is a minority view within the Hanafi school.
3
Analyse the opponent's proof
Al-Atqani's dalil is the homonymy: a word with many meanings cannot be clear without a contextual indicator. Thus, intention is needed to specify which meaning is intended.
This is a valid usul concern, but the Hanafi response shows it is answered by the context.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The commentator refutes al-Atqani by pointing out that the addition (idafa) of 'mawla' to the slave ('al-'abd') serves as a contextual indicator (qarina) that restricts the meaning to 'mu'tiq' (emancipator). Other meanings (helper, cousin, etc.) do not fit a master-slave relationship.
This is an example of takhsis al-amm by qarina.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under the majority view, if a master says 'anta mawlay' without intention, emancipation occurs. Under al-Atqani's view, emancipation would not occur without explicit intention. In court (qada'), the master's claim of intending 'helper' is not accepted; only in religious matters (dinan) is it believed.
The practice follows the majority view; judges rule that the slave is free.

Book of Emancipation: Its Expressions. Or 'muharrar', or 'harrartuka', or 'hadha mawlay', or 'ya mawlay'. [The word] 'mawla' is homonymous, one of its meanings is emancipator (mu'tiq), and in a slave it only befits this meaning, so he is emancipated without intention. [1] His statement: 'or ya mawlay'; the reason for emancipation by it is that calling is to summon the addressee, so when he calls him with a description that he can create (insha'), it becomes a realization of that description, like 'ya hurr' and 'ya 'atiq', or 'ya mawlay', or 'ya mawlati', contrary to if he said to his slave 'ya sayyidi' or 'ya maliki', for he is not emancipated except with intention, because he might intend honor by it. As in 'al-Bahr'. [2] His statement: 'the word mawla...'; know that 'mawla' is used for many meanings, Ibn al-Athir al-Jazari enumerated over twenty, among them: the helper, the supporter, the beloved, the cousin, the emancipator (with kasr the one who emancipates), the emancipated (with fath), and others. So wherever 'mawla' occurs, a contextual indication (qarina) whether situational (haliyya) or textual (maqaliyya) preceding or following is necessary to determine one of its meanings. With this, the Shi'a's inference for establishing the caliphate of 'Ali al-Murtada based on the hadith 'Whosoever's mawla I am, 'Ali is his mawla' is invalidated. For interpreting 'mawla' as 'caliph' without an external contextual indication is a claim without proof. Rather, in the mentioned hadith it means the helper or the beloved or similar, as in his saying to some of his companions: 'You are our mawla' (narrated by al-Bukhari), and the saying of Allah in Surah al-Tahrim: 'For Allah is his mawla, and Jibril, and the righteous believers.' If this is established, then know that there is an objection to what they mentioned that the statement of the master to his slave 'anta mawlay' or 'ya mawlay' is explicit (sarih) in emancipation and does not need intention: how can that be correct given that it is homonymous among multiple meanings? Rather, it should not emancipate by it except with intention, as chosen by al-Atqani in 'Ghayat al-Bayan'. [3] The commentator (sharh) indicated its answer, the gist of which is: although it is homonymous, its addition to the slave specifies the intention of one of its meanings, which is 'emancipator', so it does not need intention after that. However, if he says 'I intended the meaning of helper and the like', he is believed in matters of religion (dinan) but not in judicial (qada').
Source pages p. 591

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqayah, addressing the expressions (alfaz) used for emancipation. The text begins by listing explicit words: 'muharrar' (freed), 'harrartuka' (I have freed you), 'hadha mawlay' (this is my master), and 'ya mawlay' (O my master). The key ruling is that the word 'mawla' is homonymous (mushtarak) with many meanings, but when addressed to a slave, the only suitable meaning is 'mu'tiq' (emancipator), so emancipation is effected without intention (niyyah). The commentator explains that calling someone 'ya mawlay' is like calling him 'ya hurr' (O free) because the act of naming with a description that can be created (insha') actualizes that description. However, if the master says 'ya sayyidi' (O my master) or 'ya maliki' (O my owner), emancipation requires intention because the master may intend honor. This distinction is from al-Bahr. The commentator then notes the many meanings of 'mawla' as listed by Ibn al-Athir, including helper, supporter, etc. He uses this to refute the Shi'a argument that the hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu fa 'Aliyyun mawlahu' proves 'Ali's caliphate, since 'mawla' in that context means helper or beloved, not caliph, as shown by other texts. The passage then presents an objection from al-Atqani, a Hanafi scholar, who argues that because 'mawla' is homonymous, saying 'anta mawlay' should require intention. The commentator responds that although 'mawla' has multiple meanings, its addition (idafa) to the slave (al-'abd) specifies the intended meaning of 'emancipator', so no intention is needed. However, if the master claims he meant 'helper', it is accepted in religious matters (dinan) but not in judicial rulings (qada'). This is an intra-Hanafi dispute resolved by affirming the explicit nature of the expression.

Key Terms
المولى (al-mawla): A homonymous word with many meanings; in the context of emancipation, it means 'mu'tiq' (emancipator) when added to the slave.
صريح (sarih): An unambiguous expression that effects emancipation without intention.
نية (niyyah): Intent; in this passage, it refers to the intention required for kinayah expressions to effect emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave is not emancipated because 'mawla' is ambiguous and requires intention.
B) The slave is emancipated because the context specifies the meaning of 'emancipator'.
C) The slave is emancipated only if the master intended emancipation, otherwise not.
D) The slave becomes his own master and is automatically free.

Key Takeaway
When a master says 'anta mawlay' to his slave, the contextual addition to the slave specifies the meaning of emancipator, making it explicit and effective without intention.

Adding Freedom to Body Parts: Common Fractions and Specific Parts
Rulings on body-part emancipation and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify body-part expressions and state the resulting emancipation ruling for each category according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Emancipation extent depends on whether the body part is a common fraction, a specific part denoting the whole, or a specific part not denoting the whole.

This lesson focuses on the Hanafi rulings when a master says 'your head is free', 'half of you is free', or similar expressions. The passage explains three scenarios: common parts (like half), specific parts that denote the whole (like head/neck), and specific parts that do not (like nose). An intra-Hanafi dispute exists for common parts over whether emancipation is divisible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of body part
Determine whether the expression uses a common fraction (juz' sha'i') like 'half' or 'third', or a specific part (juz' mu'ayyan) like 'head' or 'nose'.
Common fractions are fractions of the whole slave; specific parts are named body parts.
2
Classify specific parts
For a specific part, decide if it typically denotes the whole person (like head or neck) or not (like nose). This classification is crucial for the ruling.
Parts that denote the whole are those that can stand for the entire slave in common usage.
3
Apply the ruling
Use the rules: For common fractions – dispute between Abu Hanifa (only that fraction free) and Sahibayn (whole free). For specific parts denoting the whole – whole free by agreement. For specific parts not denoting the whole – nothing free by agreement.
The passage also mentions phrases like 'ra'suka ra'su hurr' (simile, no effect) vs 'ra'sun hurr' (description, emancipation).
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
The core difference is over tajzi' al-'itq (divisibility of emancipation). Abu Hanifa considers it divisible; the Sahibayn do not. The mufta bihi is the view of the Sahibayn.
This dispute affects only common fractions; other cases are unanimous.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah)
If a master says 'half of you is free', under Abu Hanifa the slave becomes half-free, under the Sahibayn the whole slave is free. The latter is the operative position for fatwa.
The difference has real impact on the slave's status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٢] قوله : وبكنايته ؛ عطف على قوله : «بصريح لفظه»، كما أن قوله الآتي بعد ذكر أمثلة الكناية ، وبهذا ابني معطوف عليه. [٣] قوله : إن نوى ؛ إنّما احتيج إلى النيّة ؛ لأنّ ألفاظ الكناية لم توضع للعتق بل تحتمله، وتحتمل غيره، فلا بد لتعيين أحد محتملاته من النية، وتقوم مقامها دلالة الحال.
Or 'your head is free' and similar expressions that refer to the body. And by its kinayah (indirect expression) if he intends: like 'no ownership of me over you', 'no way', 'no slavery'. [1] His statement: 'or your head is free'; the examples that have passed had the addition of freedom to the essence of the owned person, and these examples involve its addition to his parts. The detail is according to what has passed in the discussion of divorce: the addition to its parts is either to a common part (juz' sha'i') or a specific part (juz' mu'ayyan). In the second case, either that part is one that denotes the whole (like the head and neck) or not (like the nose). So if he adds it to a common part like one-third, half, or quarter, then that amount is emancipated according to Abu Hanifa, and the whole according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because according to Abu Hanifa emancipation is divisible, not according to them, as will come in detail if Allah wills. And if he adds it to a specific part that does not denote the person, nothing is emancipated by agreement. And if he adds it to a part that denotes the person, the whole is emancipated according to all of them. So if he says: 'your head is free', he is emancipated without intention. However, if he says: 'your head is (like) the head of a free person' with idafa (genitive construction), he is not emancipated because it is a simile. And if he says: 'a free head' with tanwin on 'ra's', he is emancipated because it is a description. [2] His statement: 'and by its kinayah' is conjuncted to his statement: 'with his explicit word', just as his statement following after mentioning examples of kinayah, and with this my son is conjuncted to it. [3] His statement: 'if he intends'; intention is needed because the words of kinayah are not placed for emancipation but may indicate it or something else, so to determine one of its probabilities, intention is necessary, and contextual indication can take its place.
Source pages p. 592

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the expressions of emancipation, now focusing on two categories: (1) body-part expressions like 'ra'suka hurr' (your head is free) and (2) kinayah (indirect) expressions like 'la mulka li 'alayka' (no ownership over you). The commentator first explains the ruling on adding 'freedom' to a part of the slave's body. He distinguishes between a common part (like a fraction) and a specific part. For a common part (e.g., one-third of the slave), Abu Hanifa rules that only that fraction is emancipated because he considers emancipation divisible, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule that the whole is emancipated because they view emancipation as indivisible. For a specific part that does not denote the whole (e.g., nose), nothing is emancipated by consensus. For a specific part that denotes the whole (e.g., head, neck), the whole slave is emancipated according to all. The commentator also clarifies that saying 'ra'suka hurr' (without idafa) causes emancipation without intention, but saying 'ra'suka ra'su hurr' (your head is the head of a free person) is a simile and does not emancipate. Saying 'ra'sun hurr' (a free head) with tanwin is descriptive and does emancipate. The second part discusses kinayah. The commentator notes that words like 'la mulka li 'alayka' are not explicitly placed for emancipation but can imply it. Therefore, intention (niyyah) is required to specify that emancipation is meant. Contextual indication (dalalat al-hal) can substitute for intention.

Key Terms
جزء شائع (juz' sha'i'): A common part or fraction (like half, third) of the slave's body; when freedom is added to it, the ruling differs between Abu Hanifa and the two.
جزء معين (juz' mu'ayyan): A specific named body part like head, neck, nose; the ruling depends on whether it customarily denotes the whole person.
تجزي العتق (tajzi' al-'itq): Divisibility of emancipation; the principle that emancipation can be partial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master tells his slave, 'I free one-quarter of you.' According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what is the ruling?
A) Only one-quarter of the slave is freed.
B) The entire slave is freed.
C) Nothing is freed because the fraction is not a body part.
D) The slave is not freed because intention is lacking.

Key Takeaway
When freedom is added to a body part, the extent of emancipation depends on the type of part; common fractions trigger an intra-Hanafi dispute with the mufta bihi being full emancipation.

Kinayah Expressions in Emancipation: The Requirement of Intention
The requirement of intention for kinayah emancipation expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify kinayah (indirect) expressions for emancipation and explain the role of intention (niyyah) and contextual indication.

Core Idea
Indirect emancipation expressions require intention; without it, no emancipation occurs.

This lesson examines the second part of the passage focusing on kinayah—indirect phrases like 'لا ملك لي عليك' (no ownership over you). Unlike explicit words (sarih), kinayah words are not exclusively used for emancipation, so the master's intention is essential. The commentator explains that intention can be explicit or inferred from context.

Guided Path
1
Identify kinayah words
Recognize that expressions like 'la mulka li 'alayka', 'la sabil', 'la raqqa' are indirect; they can imply emancipation but also other meanings (e.g., disowning, ending a contract).
These are not the same as explicit words like 'anta hurr'.
2
Understand the need for intention
Because kinayah words are ambiguous, the master's intention (niyyah) is required to specify that emancipation is meant. Without intention, the expression is legally ineffective for emancipation.
Intention can be explicit (in the heart) or inferred from context (dalalat al-hal).
3
Apply contextual indication
If the context clearly shows the master intends emancipation (e.g., he says it during a dispute about slavery), that can substitute for explicit intention.
Context must be strong enough to remove ambiguity.
4
Distinguish from sarih (explicit)
Explicit words like 'hurr' or 'free' do not require intention because they are unambiguous in meaning. Kinayah requires intention precisely because of ambiguity.
This distinction is consistent across Hanafi fiqh.

[٢] قوله : وبكنايته ؛ عطف على قوله : «بصريح لفظه»، كما أن قوله الآتي بعد ذكر أمثلة الكناية ، وبهذا ابني معطوف عليه. [٣] قوله : إن نوى ؛ إنّما احتيج إلى النيّة ؛ لأنّ ألفاظ الكناية لم توضع للعتق بل تحتمله، وتحتمل غيره، فلا بد لتعيين أحد محتملاته من النية، وتقوم مقامها دلالة الحال.
Or 'your head is free' and similar expressions that refer to the body. And by its kinayah (indirect expression) if he intends: like 'no ownership of me over you', 'no way', 'no slavery'. [1] His statement: 'or your head is free'; the examples that have passed had the addition of freedom to the essence of the owned person, and these examples involve its addition to his parts. The detail is according to what has passed in the discussion of divorce: the addition to its parts is either to a common part (juz' sha'i') or a specific part (juz' mu'ayyan). In the second case, either that part is one that denotes the whole (like the head and neck) or not (like the nose). So if he adds it to a common part like one-third, half, or quarter, then that amount is emancipated according to Abu Hanifa, and the whole according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because according to Abu Hanifa emancipation is divisible, not according to them, as will come in detail if Allah wills. And if he adds it to a specific part that does not denote the person, nothing is emancipated by agreement. And if he adds it to a part that denotes the person, the whole is emancipated according to all of them. So if he says: 'your head is free', he is emancipated without intention. However, if he says: 'your head is (like) the head of a free person' with idafa (genitive construction), he is not emancipated because it is a simile. And if he says: 'a free head' with tanwin on 'ra's', he is emancipated because it is a description. [2] His statement: 'and by its kinayah' is conjuncted to his statement: 'with his explicit word', just as his statement following after mentioning examples of kinayah, and with this my son is conjuncted to it. [3] His statement: 'if he intends'; intention is needed because the words of kinayah are not placed for emancipation but may indicate it or something else, so to determine one of its probabilities, intention is necessary, and contextual indication can take its place.
Source pages p. 592

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the expressions of emancipation, now focusing on two categories: (1) body-part expressions like 'ra'suka hurr' (your head is free) and (2) kinayah (indirect) expressions like 'la mulka li 'alayka' (no ownership over you). The commentator first explains the ruling on adding 'freedom' to a part of the slave's body. He distinguishes between a common part (like a fraction) and a specific part. For a common part (e.g., one-third of the slave), Abu Hanifa rules that only that fraction is emancipated because he considers emancipation divisible, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule that the whole is emancipated because they view emancipation as indivisible. For a specific part that does not denote the whole (e.g., nose), nothing is emancipated by consensus. For a specific part that denotes the whole (e.g., head, neck), the whole slave is emancipated according to all. The commentator also clarifies that saying 'ra'suka hurr' (without idafa) causes emancipation without intention, but saying 'ra'suka ra'su hurr' (your head is the head of a free person) is a simile and does not emancipate. Saying 'ra'sun hurr' (a free head) with tanwin is descriptive and does emancipate. The second part discusses kinayah. The commentator notes that words like 'la mulka li 'alayka' are not explicitly placed for emancipation but can imply it. Therefore, intention (niyyah) is required to specify that emancipation is meant. Contextual indication (dalalat al-hal) can substitute for intention.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): An indirect expression that can imply emancipation but is not explicit; requires intention to be effective.
دلالة الحال (dalalat al-hal): Contextual indication that substitutes for explicit intention when the circumstances clearly show the master's intent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is intention needed for kinayah expressions in emancipation?
A) Because the words are not in Arabic.
B) Because the words are ambiguous and not exclusively used for emancipation.
C) Because the master must say the words loudly.
D) Because the slave must consent.

Key Takeaway
Kinayah expressions for emancipation require intention (or contextual indication) because they are ambiguous; without it, no emancipation takes place.

Sarih and Kinayah in Emancipation: Intention and Divisibility
Explaining the rules for صريح and كناية in العتاق based on the matn of الوقاية

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sarih and kinayah expressions for emancipation, identify when intention is required, and explain the Hanafi dispute regarding emancipation of fractional parts of a slave.

Core Idea
Sarih words free without intention; kinayah needs it. Emancipation divides according to Abu Hanifa, not by the Sahibayn.

This lesson explores the critical distinction between explicit and metaphorical wording in the act of emancipation (itq). In Hanafi law, the validity of freeing a slave hinges on whether the words used are unambiguous (sarih) or ambiguous (kinayah). The passage also addresses a key intra-madhhab disagreement: if someone frees a fractional part of a slave, does only that part become free, or the entire slave? Understanding these rules is essential for correctly applying the law of manumission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of emancipation expressions
Determine whether a phrase is sarih (explicit) or kinayah (metaphorical). Sarih words like 'you are free' or 'your head is free' directly indicate freedom. Kinayah phrases like 'I have no ownership over you' carry multiple meanings.
The passage gives 'head free' as sarih and 'no ownership' as kinayah.
2
Determine the role of intention (niyyah)
For sarih, no intention is needed because the meaning is clear. For kinayah, intention is necessary to specify emancipation among possible meanings. Context (dilalat al-hal) can substitute for explicit intention.
Intention is a condition for validity of kinayah but not for sarih.
3
Analyze addition of freedom to a part of the slave
When the master says 'half of you is free' or 'your head is free', the ruling depends on whether the part is fractional, a part that symbolizes the whole, or a part that does not.
If a fractional part (e.g., half) -> dispute. If a part symbolizing the whole (e.g., head) -> whole free. If a part not symbolizing the whole (e.g., hand) -> nothing free.
4
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute on divisibility
Abu Hanifa holds emancipation is divisible (qiyas on sale), so only the stated fraction is freed. The Sahibayn hold emancipation is indivisible (istihsan), so the whole slave is freed. The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view.
This dispute applies only to fractional parts; for parts symbolizing the whole, all agree the whole is freed.
5
Apply the rules to the phrase 'no slavery over you'
The passage clarifies that 'la riqqa li 'alayka' is interpreted as 'no ownership' because slavery is a cause of ownership. Thus it is kinayah requiring intention.
The phrase is not literally about ownership; the interpretation uses the causal link between slavery and ownership.

Source pages p. 592

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah deals with the wording of emancipation (عتق). It distinguishes between explicit (صريح) and metaphorical (كناية) expressions. For explicit words like 'head free' which refer to a part symbolizing the whole, no intention is needed because the meaning is clear. For metaphorical phrases like 'I have no ownership over you', intention is required because they could mean sale or other things. The passage details the ruling when adding freedom to a part of the slave: if to a fractional part (e.g., half), Abu Hanifa rules that only that part is freed (since emancipation is divisible in his view), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule the whole slave is freed. If to a specific part that does not symbolize the whole (e.g., hand), nothing is freed. If to a part that does symbolize the whole (e.g., head), the whole is freed. The need for intention in metaphorical expressions is due to their ambiguity. The text also addresses the phrase 'no slavery over you', explaining that slavery is not something the master possesses, so it is interpreted as 'no ownership', requiring intention.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): An explicit word that clearly indicates emancipation without need for intention, e.g., 'you are free' or 'your head is free'.
كناية (kinayah): A metaphorically used word that implies emancipation but also bears other meanings, e.g., 'I have no ownership over you'. It requires intention to effect emancipation.
نية (niyyah): Intention in the heart to emancipate the slave, necessary for metaphorical expressions to be effective.
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave, releasing him from bondage. The legal act being discussed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a master says to his slave 'I have no ownership over you' with the intention of sale, does the slave become free?
A) Yes, because the words are kinayah and any intention suffices.
B) No, because the master's intention was sale, not emancipation, so the words do not effect emancipation.
C) Yes, because intention is not required for kinayah.
D) No, because the words are explicit.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, explicit expressions (sarih) for emancipation require no intention, while metaphorical expressions (kinayah) require intention; additionally, the issue of partial emancipation divides the school.
