Definitions of Riq and Milk in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining the legal concepts of riq and milk and their relationship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the Hanafi legal terms riq and milk, explain their relationship, and identify cases where each exists without the other.

Core Idea
Riq is a legal incapacity due to original disbelief; milk is absolute ownership. Every slave is owned, but not every owned thing is a slave.

In Hanafi fiqh, the concepts of slavery (riq) and ownership (milk) are distinct yet interrelated. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya defines each term precisely and clarifies their relationship. Understanding these foundational definitions is essential for grasping many rulings in transactions, family law, and the law of war. We will dissect the definitions step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two central terms
Locate the definitions of riq and milk in the Arabic text. Note that riq is defined as a legal incapacity established as an effect of original disbelief, and it is a right of Allah. Milk is defined as a legal connection giving absolute disposition and barring others.
2
Analyse the components of each definition
Break down the definition of riq into its elements: 'shar'i' (legal), 'yuthbatu fi al-insan atharan li al-kufr' (establishes in a human an effect of disbelief), 'huwa haqq allah' (right of Allah). For milk: 'ittisal shar'i' (legal connection), 'bayna insan wa bayna shay' (between a human and a thing), 'mutlaqan li tasarrufihi fihi' (absolute for his disposition), 'hajizan 'an tasarruf al-ghayr' (barrier to others).
The commentary expands on 'shar'i' as an incapacity decreed by the Law.
3
Understand the relationship between mamluk and marqooq
The text states: 'fa al-shay' yakunu mamlukan wa la yakunu marqooqan illa wa an yakuna mamlukan' – a thing is owned and is not a slave unless it is owned. This establishes that every marqooq is mamluk, but not every mamluk is marqooq. The commentary adds that this is a general-specific relationship (umum wa khusus mutlaq).
There is a reference to Ghayat al-Bayan suggesting the relationship might be from one aspect (min wajh).
4
Examine the exceptions: riq without milk and milk without riq
The text gives examples: a harbi disbeliever in dar al-harb or a musta'min have riq (as recompense for disbelief) but no one owns them (riq without milk). Commodities and animals have ownership but no riq (milk without riq). A purchased slave has both (riq and milk combined).
Riq is specific to humans, while milk applies to both humans and property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرعي يُثبت في الإنسان أثراً للكفر ، وهو حق الله تعالى، وأما الملك : فهو اتصال شرعي بين الإنسان وبين شيء يكون مطلقا لتصرفه فيه ، وحاجزاً عن تصرف الغير فيه ، فالشَّيء يكون مملوكاً ، ولا يكون مرقوقاً إلا وأن يكون مملوكاً، فالرق في الابتداء يكون سبباً للملك ، فقولُه : لا رق لي عليك، أطلق الرق، وأراد به الملك للكفر الأصلي، والملك عبارةٌ عن المطلق للتصرّف لمن قام به المانع عن التصرف لغير من قام به. وقد يوجد الرق ولا ملك ثمه كما في الكافر الحربي في دار الحرب، والمستأمن في دار الإسلام، فإنّهم خلقوا أرقاء جزاءً لكفرهم، ولكن لا ملك لأحد عليهم ، وقد يوجد الملك ولا رق، كما في العروض والبهائم ؛ لأنّ الرق مختص ببني آدم، وقد يجتمعان كالعبد المشترى. [1]قوله : شرعي ؛ أي عجز حكم به الشرع بثبوته في الإنسان أثراً لكفره، فالمسلم الأصلي لا يوجد فيه الرق أصلا، والكافر ابتداء يثبت فيه الرق، ويدوم به وإن أسلم، ولا ينفك عنه إلا بإزالته من مولاه. (۲) قوله : وهو حق الله ؛ لأنه ثبت شرعاً جزاء للكفر الأصلي. [۳] قوله : وبين شيء ؛ أشار بإطلاقه إلى أنّ المملوك لا يلزم أن يكون إنساناً. [٤] قوله : يكون مطلقاً ؛ اسم فاعل من الإطلاق ؛ أي مبيحاً، ومجوزاً لتصرف الإنسان في ذلك الشيء. [0]قوله : وحاجزاً ؛ أي مانعاً عن تصرف الغير الذي لم يوجد بينه وبين ذلك الشيء . ء ذلك الاتصال عن التصرّف فيه والانتفاع إلا بإذن مالكه. [٦] قوله : فالشيء...الخ ؛ بيان للنسبة بين المملوكِ وبين المرقوق، بعد بيان معناهما، وظاهره أن النسبة بينهما عموم وخصوص مطلقاً، فكل مرقوق مملوك، ولا عكس، وظاهرُ «غاية البيان على ما مرّ نقل عبارته أنّ بينهما نسبة العموم والخصوص من وجه [ ] قوله : أطلق ؛ بصيغة المجهول من الإطلاق، أو بصيغة المعروف، والضمير إلى قائل : لا رق لي عليك.
Legal: it establishes in a human an effect of disbelief, and it is a right of Allah. As for ownership: it is a legal connection between a human and a thing that is absolute for his disposition of it, and a barrier to the disposition of others in it. So a thing is owned, and is not a slave unless it is owned. So slavery initially becomes a cause for ownership. So his statement: 'there is no slavery for me over you'—he used slavery to mean ownership. Slavery is for original disbelief, and ownership is an expression of absolute disposition for one who has the capacity, with a barrier to disposition for others. And slavery may exist without ownership, as in the case of a warring disbeliever in dar al-harb, or a musta'min in dar al-Islam, for they were created as slaves as a recompense for their disbelief, but no one has ownership over them. And ownership may exist without slavery, as in commodities and animals, because slavery is specific to human beings. And both may combine, as in a purchased slave. [1] His statement: 'Legal' means an incapacity decreed by the Law, established in a human as an effect of his disbelief. So an original Muslim has no slavery at all. In the disbeliever, slavery is initially established and continues even if he accepts Islam, and is not removed except by the master's removal. (2) His statement: 'It is a right of Allah' because it is legally established as recompense for original disbelief. [3] His statement: 'and a thing'—his generality indicates that the owned thing need not be a human. [4] His statement: 'is absolute'—a participle from 'itlaq', meaning permissible and authorized for the person's disposition of that thing. [0] His statement: 'and a barrier'—meaning a preventer of disposition of others who have no connection with that thing. That connection prevents disposition and benefit except by the permission of its owner. [6] His statement: 'So a thing...'—an explanation of the relationship between the owned and the slave, after explaining their meanings. Apparently, the relationship is general and specific in an absolute sense: every slave is owned, but not vice versa. And the apparent meaning of Ghayat al-Bayan, as quoted, is that the relationship is general and specific from one aspect. [ ] His statement: 'He used'—in the passive form of 'itlaq' or the active form; the pronoun refers to the speaker: 'there is no slavery for me over you'.
Source pages p. 594

Commentary
This passage is a definition and commentary on two foundational legal concepts in Hanafi fiqh: riq (slavery) and milk (ownership). The author begins by defining riq as a legal incapacity that is established in a human being as an effect of original disbelief, and it is a right of Allah. Milk is defined as a legal connection between a person and a thing that gives the person absolute authority to dispose of it and bars others from disposing of it. The passage then clarifies the relationship between the terms: every marqooq (slave) is mamluk (owned), but not every mamluk is marqooq, because ownership can apply to non-human things like commodities and animals, while riq is specific to humans. The commentary expands on each phrase: it explains that 'shar'i' means an incapacity decreed by the Law; that riq is a right of Allah because it is recompense for original disbelief; that mamluk can be a non-human; that 'mutlaq' means permissible and authorized; that 'hajiz' means a barrier; and that the relationship between mamluk and marqooq is one of general and specific in an absolute sense (every marqooq is mamluk, not vice versa). The passage also notes that there can be riq without milk (e.g., a harbi in dar al-harb) and milk without riq (e.g., animals).

Key Terms
رق (riq): A legal incapacity established in a human as a consequence of original disbelief, considered a right of Allah.
ملك (milk): A legal connection between a person and a thing that gives the person absolute authority to dispose of it and bars others from disposition.
مرقوق (marqooq): A human being subject to riq (a slave).
مملوك (mamluk): Anything that is owned, including both humans (slaves) and non-humans (commodities, animals).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes a 'مرقوق' from a 'مملوك' according to this passage?
A) All مرقوق are مملوك but not vice versa.
B) All مملوك are مرقوق but not vice versa.
C) They are completely synonymous.
D) They are opposites.

Key Takeaway
Riq and milk are distinct legal concepts: riq is a status attached to humans as a recompense for original disbelief, while milk is the broad right of ownership over any thing, including humans.

Kinaya and Sarih Expressions in Manumission
Explaining rulings on specific lafz for manumission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish kinaya from sarih expressions for manumission and apply the rule that kinaya requires intention while sarih does not.

Core Idea
Kinaya needs intention; sarih does not. 'بهذا ابني' is sarih; 'أخي' is not.

This passage from the Book of Manumission clarifies which words free a slave and which do not, depending on whether the expression is explicit (sarih) or implicit (kinaya). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying manumission rulings. We'll explore specific phrases and the role of intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Expressions
List the phrases mentioned: 'خرجت من ملكي' (you have left my ownership), 'خليت سبيلك' (I have freed your way), 'قد أطلقتك' (I have released you), and 'بهذا ابني' (by this, my son). Also note the exceptions 'أخي' and 'أختي'.
These are all ways a master might speak to a slave.
2
Classify Each Expression
Determine whether each phrase is kinaya (implicit) or sarih (explicit). According to the text, 'خرجت من ملكي', 'خليت سبيلك', and 'قد أطلقتك' are kinaya. 'بهذا ابني' is sarih. 'أخي' and 'أختي' are kinaya and do not free in the apparent narration.
Kinaya admits multiple interpretations; sarih is unambiguous.
3
Apply the Rule of Intention
For kinaya, manumission only occurs if the master intends it. For sarih, no intention is needed. Thus, saying 'بهذا ابني' frees the slave regardless of intent, while 'خرجت من ملكي' requires intent.
The exception for 'أخي' and 'أختي' is based on weak analogy to true siblings.
4
Understand the Grammatical Clue
The letter 'ba' in 'بهذا ابني' indicates it is not conjoined to the kinaya examples, reinforcing its sarih status. Without 'ba', it might be misclassified.
This shows how Arabic grammar affects legal rulings.
5
Note the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
The passage notes that in zahir al-riwaya, 'أخي' and 'أختي' do not free, but some later commentaries may differ. The relied-upon position is that they do not free.
This is an example of tarjih within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتاق الفاظه
وخرجت من ملكي، وخليت سبيلك ، ولأمته : قد أطلقتك. وبهذا ابني للأصغر والأكبر [1]- وخرجت من ملكي، وخليت سبيلك ، ولأمته : قد أطلقتُك " [2] (۱) (۲) وبهذا ابني" للأصغر والأكبر) ؛ وإنما جاءَ بلفظ الباء في قوله : وبهذا ابني ؛ ليُعْلَمَ أَنه عطف على قوله : وبكنايته ، ولو لم يذكر حرف الباء ، أوهم أَنَّهُ عطف على أمثلة الكناية نحو : لا ملك لي عليك ... إلى آخره، فيلزم حينئذٍ أنه كناية، وليس كذلك. ١ ] قوله : وخرجت ؛ - بفتح التاء - خطاباً إلى المملوك، فهذا اللفظ وكذا : خليت سبيلك - بضم التاء - يحتمل أن يكون المراد به الخروج أو التخليه بسبب آخر ، ويحتمل أن يكون المراد به إنّ الخروج والتخليوة بالعتق ، فإذا نواه عتق. [٢] قوله : ولأمته قد أطلقتك من الإطلاق بمعنى الإرسال ؛ لكونه مثل التخلية، محتملاً لصور، فلا تعتق إلا بالنيّة ، بخلاف ما إذا قال لأمته : طلقتك أو أنت طالق ، فإنّه من الألفاظ الصريحة للطلاق الذي هو من آثار النكاح ، فلا يقع به العتق مطلقاً. (۳) قوله : وبهذا ابني ؛ الأصل فيه أن من وصف مملوكه بصيغة من يعتق عليه إذا ملكه بالقرابة المحرمة للنكاح عتق عليه ، كهذه بنتي ، وهذه أمي، وهذا أبي، أو عمي، أو خالي أو جدي، إلا في أخي وأختي ؛ فإنّه لا يعتق بهما في ظاهر الرواية. كذا في شروح «الهداية». [٤] قوله : للأصغر ؛ أي لمملوك هو أصغر سنّاً من المالك أو أكبر، ومثله المساوي، ولم يذكره لظهور من ذكر الأكبر. [0] قوله : أوهم.... الخ ؛ حاصله أنه لو قال : وهذا ابني بدون الباء توهم أنه معطوف على أمثلة الكناية التي ذكرها بقوله : كلامك لي عليك داخل تحت الكناية التي يحتاجُ العتق بها إلى النية وليس كذلك، فإنّ مثل هذا اللفظ يلحق بالصريح في عدمِ الاحتياج إلى النية. (۱) لأن كل لفظ من هذا يحتمل وجهين فقوله : خرجت من ملكي ؛ يحتمل بالبيع وبالعتق، ولا سبيل لي عليك لأنك وفيت بالخدمة فلا سبيل لي عليك باللوم والعقوبة، ويحتمل لأنك معتق، وكذا إذا قال لأمته : قد أطلقتك ونوى العتق عتقت ؛ لأن الإطلاق يقتضي زوال اليد وقد نزل يده عنها بالعتق وغيره وهو مثل خليت سبيلك . ينظر : «الجوهرة النيرة (٢) : ٩٦).
Book of Manumission: Its Wordings. 'You have left my ownership', 'I have freed your way', and to his female slave: 'I have released you'. And by this: 'my son' for the younger and the older. [1]- 'You have left my ownership', 'I have freed your way', and to his female slave: 'I have released you.' [2] (1)(2) And by this: 'my son' for the younger and the older. And it is only that he came with the letter 'ba' in his statement: 'and by this: my son' to make known that it is conjoined to his statement: 'and by his kinaya'. If he had not mentioned the letter 'ba', it would have been understood that it is conjoined to the examples of kinaya like 'no ownership for me over you'..., so then it would be considered kinaya, but it is not so. [1] His statement: 'You have left'—with fatha on the 'ta'—addressing the slave. So this wording, and similarly 'I have freed your way'—with dhamma on the 'ta'—admits the possibility that the intended meaning is leaving or freeing due to some other cause, and admits the possibility that the intended meaning is that the leaving and freeing is by manumission. So if he intends it, he becomes free. [2] His statement: 'and to his female slave: I have released you'—from 'itlaq' meaning sending; because it is like freeing, it is subject to various possibilities, so she does not become free except with intention. Unlike if he says to his female slave: 'I have divorced you' or 'you are divorced', for those are explicit phrases for divorce which is an effect of marriage, so manumission does not occur by them at all. (3) His statement: 'and by this: my son'—the basis of this is that whoever describes his slave with a form that would cause the slave to be free upon acquisition due to prohibited blood relationship for marriage, then he becomes free. Like 'this is my daughter', 'this is my mother', 'this is my father', 'my uncle', 'my maternal uncle', or 'my grandfather', except for 'my brother' and 'my sister', for they do not cause freedom by these terms in the apparent transmission. Thus in the commentaries of Al-Hidayah. [4] His statement: 'for the younger'—meaning a slave who is younger in age than the owner, or older, and similarly equal; and he did not mention it due to the obviousness of mentioning the older. [0] His statement: 'he would have misunderstood...'—the summary is that if he said 'and this is my son' without the 'ba', it would be understood that it is conjoined to the examples of kinaya that he mentioned in his statement: 'your speech over me...' included under kinaya that requires intention for manumission, and that is not the case. Indeed such a wording is attached to the explicit in not needing intention. (1) Because every wording of this type admits two aspects: his statement 'You have left my ownership' admits sale and manumission; and 'there is no way for me over you' because you have fulfilled service, so there is no way for me over you in blame and punishment, and it admits that you are freed. Similarly, if he says to his female slave: 'I have released you' and intends manumission, she becomes free, because release necessitates removal of hand, and he has removed his hand from her by manumission or otherwise, and it is like 'I have freed your way'. Refer to Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah (2:96).
Source pages p. 595

Commentary
This passage is from the Book of Manumission (Kitab al-'Itaq) and discusses the wording used to free a slave. It lists several phrases: 'You have left my ownership', 'I have freed your way', and 'I have released you' addressed to a female slave, and 'by this, my son' (for a younger or older slave). The commentary explains that the first three phrases are kinaaya (implicit expressions) that require intention (niyya) to effect manumission, because they can also be interpreted in other ways (e.g., sale, fulfillment of service, etc.). If the master says these words without intending manumission, the slave does not become free. In contrast, if he says explicit words like 'You are free' (أنت حر), that is sarih and requires no intention. The passage also discusses the phrase 'بهذا ابني' (by this, my son): this is considered sarih (explicit) for manumission because calling a slave 'my son' implies a relationship that would necessitate freedom if the slave were actually a son. However, the commentary notes that 'my brother' and 'my sister' are exceptions and do not cause freedom in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya). The inclusion of the letter 'ba' in 'بهذا ابني' is to clarify that it is not a mere example of kinaaya. The passage then clarifies that 'for the younger and the older' covers all ages. The overall ruling: these phrases are kinaaya except for 'بهذا ابني' which is treated as sarih in the school.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): Manumission; the act of freeing a slave.
كناية (kinayah): An expression that is ambiguous and requires intention (niyya) to effect a legal consequence.
صريح (sarih): An explicit expression that effects a legal consequence without need for intention.
نية (niyyah): Intent; the mental determination to achieve a specific legal result, e.g., manumission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 'خليت سبيلك'
C) 'بهذا ابني'
D) 'قد أطلقتك'

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi manumission law, kinaya expressions require intention, while sarih expressions like 'بهذا ابني' do not; however, 'أخي' and 'أختي' are exceptions that do not free even with intention.

Ambiguous Emancipation Phrases Requiring Intent
Commentary on ambiguous emancipation phrases and the necessity of intent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify ambiguous emancipation phrases (kinayah), state that they require intent (niyyah) to effect emancipation, and distinguish them from explicit divorce phrases.

Core Idea
Kinayah requires intent; without it, no emancipation occurs.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains three phrases a master might use that are ambiguous (kinayah) for emancipation. Unlike explicit words like 'أنت حر', these phrases can have other meanings, so the master's intent (niyyah) determines whether the slave becomes free. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of emancipation in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrases
List the three ambiguous phrases mentioned: 'خرجت من ملكي', 'خليت سبيلك', and 'أطلقتك'.
2
Classify the phrase type
Recognize that these are kinayah (ambiguous) because they can be used in multiple contexts (e.g., sale, freeing).
Contrast with sarih (explicit) phrases like 'أنت حر'.
3
Apply the rule
Since the phrases are kinayah, emancipation occurs only if the master intends emancipation at the time of utterance.
4
Distinguish from divorce phrases
Note that 'طلقتك' or 'أنت طالق' are explicit for divorce and cannot effect emancipation, even with intent.
This is because they are reserved for the marriage context.
5
Derive the proof
The proof (talil) is the ambiguity of the words; they are not exclusively for emancipation, so intent specifies the meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتاق الفاظه
وخرجت من ملكي، وخليت سبيلك ، ولأمته : قد أطلقتك.
[1] قوله : وخرجت ؛ - بفتح التاء - خطاباً إلى المملوك، فهذا اللفظ وكذا : خليت سبيلك - بضم التاء - يحتمل أن يكون المراد به الخروج أو التخليه بسبب آخر ، ويحتمل أن يكون المراد به إنّ الخروج والتخليّة بالعتق ، فإذا نواه عتق.
[٢] قوله : ولأمته قد أطلقتك من الإطلاق بمعنى الإرسال ؛ لكونه مثل التخلية، محتملاً لصور، فلا تعتق إلا بالنيّة ، بخلاف ما إذا قال لأمته : طلقتك أو أنت طالق ، فإنّه من الألفاظ الصريحة للطلاق الذي هو من آثار النكاح ، فلا يقع به العتق مطلقاً.
Book of Emancipation: Its Wording. [Matn:] 'You have left my ownership, and I have freed you, and to his female slave: I have released you.' [Commentary 1] His statement: 'you have left' – with fatha on the ta – addressing the slave. This phrase, and likewise 'I have freed you' – with dhamma on the ta – can mean exiting or freeing due to another reason, and can mean that exiting and freeing are by emancipation. So if he intends emancipation, he is emancipated. [Commentary 2] His statement: 'and to his female slave: I have released you' – from 'release' meaning 'sending forth'; because it is like freeing, it can be interpreted in multiple ways, so she is not emancipated except with intent. Unlike if he says to her 'I have divorced you' or 'you are divorced,' for that is an explicit expression for divorce, which is an effect of marriage, so emancipation does not occur by it at all.
Source pages p. 595

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn from the book of emancipation dealing with wording. The author explains three ambiguous phrases a master might use: 'خرجت من ملكي' (you have left my ownership), 'خليت سبيلك' (I have freed you), and 'أطلقتك' (I have released you). The first two are addressed to a male slave, the third to a female slave. These phrases are not explicit (sarih) for emancipation because they can also be used in other contexts, such as selling or forgiving service. Therefore, they require the master's intent (niyyah) to effect emancipation. If he intends emancipation when uttering them, the slave becomes free. The commentary contrasts this with the phrase 'طلقتك' or 'أنت طالق' addressed to a female slave, which are explicit for divorce and cannot effect emancipation even if intended, as they are strictly for marriage dissolution. The reasoning is based on the principle that ambiguous words (kinayah) require intent, while explicit words (sarih) do not, and these phrases fall under kinayah in the context of emancipation.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): A word that is ambiguous and can be interpreted in multiple ways; for emancipation, kinayah requires intent (niyyah) to be effective.
صريح (sarih): An explicit word that has a clear legal meaning and does not require intent to effect the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Conditions and Dispute Over Master's Acknowledgment of Paternity
Explaining conditions for a master's acknowledgment of paternity to free a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a master's acknowledgment of paternity to free a slave and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding slaves of known lineage.

Core Idea
A master's 'this is my son' frees the slave only if lineage can be established — except Abu Hanifa frees even without lineage.

When a master says 'this is my son' to his slave, does the slave become free? The Hanafi school conditions this on the possibility of paternity and unknown lineage. But what if the slave's father is known? Here, Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, diverge. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the practical outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
The master's statement 'هذا ابني' is an acknowledgment of paternity (iqrar bil-bunuwah). It is not kinayah but explicit (sarih) in establishing lineage.
2
List the two conditions for establishing lineage
(a) The slave must be of such age that a person like him could be born from a person like the master (يولد مثله لمثله). (b) The slave must be of unknown lineage (مجهول النسب).
These conditions ensure plausibility.
3
State the consensus ruling
When both conditions are met, lineage is established by consensus, and the slave becomes free automatically, even without the master's intention.
This is an agreed position.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on known lineage
If the slave is of known lineage (e.g., his father is known), the situation splits: Abu Hanifa holds that the slave is still freed; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold that he is not freed.
The text records this as 'فكذلك عنده، وعندهما لا يعتق'.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Although the text does not explicitly state which view is relied upon, later Hanafi practice and the exception for brother/sister ('لا يعتق بهما في ظاهر الرواية') suggest that the Sahibayn's position is the operative one.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا ابني للأصغر والأكبر)
وإنما جاءَ بلفظ الباء في قوله : وبهذا ابني ؛ ليُعْلَمَ أَنه عطف على قوله : وبكنايته ، ولو لم يذكر حرف الباء ، أوهم أَنَّهُ عطف على أمثلة الكناية نحو : لا ملك لي عليك ... إلى آخره، فيلزم حينئذٍ أنه كناية، وليس كذلك.
[3] قوله : وبهذا ابني ؛ الأصل فيه أن من وصف مملوكه بصيغة من يعتق عليه إذا ملكه بالقرابة المحرمة للنكاح عتق عليه ، كهذه بنتي ، وهذه أمي، وهذا أبي، أو عمي، أو خالي أو جدي، إلا في أخي وأختي ؛ فإنّه لا يعتق بهما في ظاهر الرواية. كذا في شروح «الهداية».
[٤] قوله : للأصغر ؛ أي لمملوك هو أصغر سنّاً من المالك أو أكبر، ومثله المساوي، ولم يذكره لظهور من ذكر الأكبر.
[0] قوله : أوهم.... الخ ؛ حاصله أنه لو قال : وهذا ابني بدون الباء توهم أنه معطوف على أمثلة الكناية التي ذكرها بقوله : كلامك لي عليك داخل تحت الكناية التي يحتاجُ العتق بها إلى النية وليس كذلك، فإنّ مثل هذا اللفظ يلحق بالصريح في عدمِ الاحتياج إلى النية.

فإنَّ الْمُقِرَّ له إن كان يولد مثلُهُ لمثله " ، وهو " مجهول النسب يثبت نسبه منه ،
ويكون حراً، وإن لم ينو، وإن لم يكن كذلك
[0].
[١] قوله : فإن المقر له ... الخ ؛ تفصيله : إن العبد المقر له ؛ أي الذي أقر له مولاه بالبنوة بقوله : هذا ابني لا يخلو إما أن يكون صالحاً لأن يكون ابنا له ، بأن كان مقدار سنه بحيث يمكن أن يكون ابناً له ، أو لا يكون صالحاً، وكلّ منهما لا يخلو من أن يكون مجهول النسب أو لا.
فإن صلح وهو مجهول النسب ثبت نسبه منه وعتق إجماعاً.
وإن كان معروف النسب لا يثبت نسبه منه، لكن يعتق وإن لم يصلح ولداً له، فكذلك عنده، وعندهما لا يعتق، وكذلك الكلام في هذا أبي وهذه أمي، وهذه بنتي.
(1)
كذا في «البحر» [٢] قوله : إن كان يولد مثله لمثله ؛ بأن كان التفاوتُ بين سن المولى المقر وبين سنّ العبد المقر له بحيث يمكن كونه مولوداً منه ، ويكون مثلُ المُقَرِّ له سنّاً ولداً لمثلِ الْمُقِرّ سنّاً. [٣]قوله : وهو ؛ أي والحال أن المقرّ له مجهول النسب ؛ أي لا يعرف له أب، ولا يعلم له نسب في مولده وفي مسكنه.
[٤]قوله : يثبت نسبه ؛ أي ذلك المقر له ، يعني يكون هو ابناً له شرعاً؛ لأنّ النَّسَبَ يلزم المقر بإقراره ما لم يمنع منه مانع، ككونه معروف النسب. ولما ثبت نسبه منه صارَ المُقَرّ له حراً سواءً نوى المقرّ العتق أو لم ينو ؛ لأن الحرية حينئذ من لوازم ثبوت النسب، لما تقرر أن من ملك ذا رحم محرماً منه عتق عليه بدون اختيار كما سيأتي تفصيله إن شاء الله تعالى.
[0] قوله : وإن لم يكن كذلك ؛ هذا يشتمل صورتين :
إحداهما : أن يكون المقرّ لا يولد مثله لمثله، وذلك بأن يكون أكبر سنّاً من مولاه أو مساوياً له أو أصغر منه ، لكن لا بقدر يمكن أن يكون مثله مولوداً منه، بأن يكون أصغر منه بسنتين مثلاً ، ففي هذه الصورة لا يثبت نسبه منه بقوله : «هذا ابني»، وإن كان مجهول النسب ؛ لعدم إمكان كونه ابناً له مولوداً منه.
And by this [phrase] 'my son' for the younger and the older. And indeed the letter ba' came in his statement 'and by this my son' to inform that it is coordinated with his statement 'and by its kinayah'. If he did not mention the letter ba', it would be misunderstood that it is coordinated with examples of kinayah like 'no ownership of mine over you' etc., so then it would be considered kinayah, but it is not. [Commentary 3] His statement: 'and by this my son'; the principle is that whoever describes his slave with a form of someone who is freed upon him if he owns him due to blood relationship prohibited for marriage, then he is freed, like 'this is my daughter', 'this is my mother', 'this is my father', or my uncle, or my maternal uncle, or my grandfather, except for 'my brother' and 'my sister', for he is not freed by them in the apparent narration. Thus in the commentaries of al-Hidayah. [Commentary 4] His statement: 'for the younger'; i.e., for a slave who is younger in age than the owner or older, and likewise the equal, and he did not mention it because the mention of the older makes it clear. [Commentary 0] The summary is that if he said 'and this is my son' without the ba', it would be misunderstood that it is coordinated with the examples of kinayah which he mentioned in his statement 'your speech to me is over you' and would fall under kinayah which requires intention for emancipation, but it is not like that; rather such a phrase is attached to the explicit in not needing intention. [Page 596] So the one acknowledged (the slave) if a person like him can be born from a person like him (the master), and he is of unknown lineage, his lineage is established from him, and he becomes free, even if he did not intend. And if it is not like that. [Commentary 1] The detail: the slave who is acknowledged, i.e., the one whose master acknowledged his sonship by saying 'this is my son', is either suitable to be his son, by his age being such that he could be his son, or not suitable. And each of them is either of unknown lineage or not. If he is suitable and of unknown lineage, his lineage is established and he is freed by consensus. If he is of known lineage, his lineage is not established, but he is freed according to him (Abu Hanifa) even if he is not suitable as a son, whereas according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he is not freed. And the same applies to 'this is my father', 'this is my mother', 'this is my daughter'. [Commentary 2] 'If a person like him can be born from a person like him': the difference in age between the master acknowledging and the slave acknowledged is such that it is possible for him to be born from him, and the acknowledged is of an age to be a son to someone like the acknowledger in age. [Commentary 3] 'And he is': i.e., the state that the acknowledged is of unknown lineage, i.e., no father is known for him, and no lineage is known for him in his birthplace and residence. [Commentary 4] 'His lineage is established': i.e., that acknowledged person becomes his son legally, because lineage is binding on the acknowledger by his acknowledgment as long as there is no preventing factor, such as the slave being of known lineage. When his lineage is established, the acknowledged becomes free whether the acknowledger intended emancipation or not, because freedom then is a necessary consequence of establishing lineage, based on the established principle that whoever owns a relative within a prohibited degree becomes free without choice, as will be detailed. [Commentary 0] 'And if it is not like that': this includes two cases. One: the acknowledger is not such that a person like him can be born from a person like him, e.g., he is older than his master or equal or younger but not to the extent that a person like him could be born from him, such as being younger by two years. In this case, his lineage is not established by his saying 'this is my son', even if of unknown lineage, due to the impossibility of him being his son born from him.
Source pages p. 595

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a master saying 'هذا ابني' (this is my son) to his slave. The matn states 'وبهذا ابني للأصغر والأكبر', meaning that this phrase applies whether the slave is younger or older than the master. The commentary clarifies that this expression is not kinayah but rather explicit (sarih) in establishing lineage and emancipation, as indicated by the inclusion of the letter ba' which ties it to explicit expressions. The core ruling: if a master acknowledges a slave as his son, certain conditions determine the outcome. The slave must be of such age that he could reasonably be the master's son (يولد مثله لمثله), and he must be of unknown lineage (مجهول النسب). If both conditions are met, the slave's lineage is established and he becomes free automatically, whether the master intended emancipation or not—this is by consensus. If the slave is of known lineage, his lineage is not established, but Abu Hanifa holds that the slave is still freed (due to the master's statement being akin to an explicit declaration of kinship), whereas Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that he is not freed because the statement is false in establishing lineage and does not effect emancipation. If the age condition is not met (e.g., the slave is too close in age to be the master's son), then lineage is not established and the slave is not freed even if of unknown lineage. The commentary also notes that this ruling extends to other kinship terms like 'this is my father', 'this is my mother', 'this is my daughter', except for 'brother' and 'sister' (in the apparent narration, they do not effect emancipation). The dispute is recorded in works like al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Key Terms
مجهول النسب (majhul al-nasab): A person whose lineage (father) is unknown; no one knows his father or his ancestry in his birthplace.
يولد مثله لمثله (yulad mithlu li mithli): A condition for the possibility of paternity: the age difference between the master and the slave must be such that the master could be the father (usually a difference of at least 12 years or so, though not precisely defined here).
صريح (sarih): Explicit; a statement that needs no intention for its effect. Here, 'هذا ابني' is considered sarih in establishing lineage and freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a master says 'this is my son' to a slave who is of known lineage and of suitable age, what is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The slave is not freed because lineage is not established.
B) The slave is freed even though lineage is not established.
C) The slave is freed only if the master intends emancipation.
D) The slave's lineage is established and he is freed.

Key Takeaway
The master's acknowledgment of paternity frees a slave only if the slave's lineage is unknown and age is suitable; if lineage is known, Abu Hanifa frees but the relied-upon position of the Sahibayn does not.

Metaphor and Freedom: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn on Acknowledging a Slave as Son
Intra-Hanafi dispute on acknowledging a slave as son.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a master's statement 'this is my son' frees a slave when literal paternity is impossible, and apply the underlying usul principle regarding metaphor.

Core Idea
When literal paternity is impossible, 'son' becomes a determined metaphor for freedom, freeing the slave without intention according to Abu Hanifa.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents a classic intra-Hanafi disagreement on the effect of a master's acknowledgment of paternity. The core issue is whether the word 'son' can serve as a metaphor for freedom even when literal sonship is impossible — e.g., when the slave is older than the master or of known lineage. Abu Hanifa holds that it can, because the possibility of the literal meaning is not a condition for the validity of a metaphor. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree, requiring the literal meaning to be possible for the metaphor to be valid. Understanding this dispute illuminates Hanafi usul on majaz and the difference between metaphor (majaz) and allusion (kinayah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage divides cases of a master saying 'this is my son' to a slave into three: (1) slave could be son and is of unknown lineage → lineage and freedom by consensus; (2) slave is of known lineage → lineage not established, but Abu Hanifa frees him; (3) slave cannot be son by age → lineage not established, but again Abu Hanifa frees him if unknown lineage. The dispute centers on cases (2) and (3).
Remember: the Sahibayn do not free in these two scenarios.
2
Analyse Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that 'ibn' is a metaphor for freedom because freedom is a necessary consequence of paternity. The impossibility of literal paternity does not invalidate the metaphor, as evidenced by common Arabic metaphors like 'asad' (lion) for a brave person. The mind's transfer to the metaphorical meaning requires understanding the literal, not intending it.
This is a usul argument about the nature of majaz.
3
Analyse the Sahibayn's objection
The Sahibayn hold that for a metaphor to be valid, the literal meaning must be possible to intend. If it is impossible, the speech becomes null (hader) because the metaphorical meaning depends on the literal. They view the substitution as in the ruling, not just in the speech act.
This reflects a different conception of majaz: the literal must be feasible.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, a slave of known lineage or older than the master is freed by the acknowledgment. Under the Sahibayn, such a slave is not freed. This impacts real cases in manumission law.
The author sides with Abu Hanifa, indicating the preferred fatwa.
5
Connect to usul al-fiqh
The dispute hinges on the condition for the validity of majaz: is possibility of the literal meaning required? Abu Hanifa says no, the Sahibayn say yes. This also distinguishes majaz from kinayah: majaz determined needs no intention, while kinayah requires niyyah.
This lesson bridges fiqh and usul.

For the one acknowledged to (the slave), if one like him is born to one like him (the master), and he is of unknown lineage, his lineage is established from him, and he becomes free, even if he did not intend. And if it is not so. [0] [1] His saying: 'For the one acknowledged to...' etc.; its elaboration: The slave acknowledged to, i.e., the one for whom his master acknowledged paternity by saying 'this is my son', is not free from either being suitable to be a son to him, by his age being such that he could possibly be a son to him, or not being suitable. And each of them is not free from being of unknown lineage or not. If he is suitable and of unknown lineage, his lineage is established from him and he becomes free by consensus. And if he is of known lineage, his lineage is not established from him, but he becomes free even if he is not suitable as a son; thus according to him (Abu Hanifa), but according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he does not become free. And similarly the statement in this (case of) 'this is my father' and 'this is my mother' and 'this is my daughter'. (1) Thus in 'Al-Bahr'. [2] His saying: 'if one like him is born to one like him'; meaning that the difference between the age of the master acknowledging and the age of the slave acknowledged to is such that it is possible for him to be born from him, and one like the acknowledged to in age is a son to one like the acknowledger in age. [3] His saying: 'and he is'; i.e., while the acknowledged to is of unknown lineage; i.e., no father is known for him, and no lineage is known for him in his birthplace and residence. [4] His saying: 'his lineage is established'; i.e., that acknowledged to, meaning he becomes his son legally; because lineage becomes binding on the acknowledger by his acknowledgment as long as there is no preventing impediment, such as being of known lineage. And when his lineage is established from him, the acknowledged to becomes free whether the acknowledger intended manumission or not; because freedom then is one of the necessary consequences of the establishment of lineage, based on what is established that whoever owns a mahram relative of his, he becomes free from him without choice, as will come in detail, God willing. [0] His saying: 'And if it is not so'; this includes two scenarios: One of them: that the acknowledger is not one to whom one like him is born, such as being older than his master, or equal to him, or younger but not to the extent that one like him could be born from him, such as being two years younger, for example. In this scenario, his lineage is not established from him by his saying 'this is my son', even if he is of unknown lineage, due to the impossibility of his being a son born from him. (1) 'Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq' (4:243). [Page 597] Book of Manumission: Its Expressions 597 (1) This expression becomes a metaphor for freedom, so he becomes free, even if he did not intend; because the metaphor is determined, and if it were a kinayah (allusion) it would need intention. And regarding the one older than him, there is a disagreement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. And I have elaborated on this issue in the chapter on metaphor from the book 'Al-Tanqih'. Its summary: that the possibility of the literal meaning is not a condition for the validity of the metaphor, like the application of 'lion' to a brave person; so the possibility of sonship is not a condition for the validity of the metaphor, which is freedom. The other scenario: that the slave is of known lineage, whether younger or older or equal; so his lineage is not established from him, because the establishment of his lineage from someone else is a preventing impediment. And when in these two scenarios intending the literal meaning is not possible, the metaphorical meaning is intended, which is manumission, and there is no need for intention then; because intention is only needed to specify one of multiple possible meanings of the word, as you have learned regarding words of kinayah, and here the metaphor is determined, the word does not admit anything else, so it is established necessarily. [1] His saying: 'as a metaphor'; because freedom and manumission are necessary concomitants of sonship, so it is of the type of using the name of the necessary condition (malzum) and intending the necessary consequence (lazim). [2] His saying: 'Its summary...' etc.; its clarification: that after they agreed that the metaphor is a substitute for the literal meaning, they differed concerning the aspect of substitution. According to him (Abu Hanifa), the substitution is in the aspect of speech (takallum), and according to them (Sahibayn), in the aspect of the ruling; meaning its metaphorical ruling is a substitute for its literal ruling. So when a word is uttered, it is conditional for the validity of intending its metaphorical meaning that intending the literal meaning be possible, even if it is not intended for some reason. Therefore, where it is impossible to intend its literal meaning, it is also impossible to intend its metaphorical meaning; rather the speech is null. The justification for that: in metaphor, the mind transfers from the thing named (al-mawdu' lahu) to its necessary concomitant (lazim); so the second depends on the first; so when it is absent, it is absent; so it is necessary for the first to be possible. And according to him (Abu Hanifa): the transfer of the mind to the second depends on understanding the first, not on intending it. So when a word that is sound in terms of speech is uttered, capable of conveying a sound meaning, and its literal meaning is not intended, either due to impossibility or another reason, it is interpreted as having the metaphorical meaning, and is not considered null. How many metaphors whose literal meanings are not possible are frequent in the speech of the Arabs? Do you not see that 'lion' is applied to a brave person despite the impossibility of intending the literal meaning, i.e., the specific predatory animal? And on this topic there are detailed investigations in 'Al-Tawdih' and [ ] and 'Al-Talwih'. (1) See: 'Al-Tanqih' and its commentary 'Al-Tawdih' (1:152), 'Kashf al-Asrar Sharh Usul al-Bazdawi' (2:78), 'Al-Taqrir wa al-Tahbir' (2:33), 'Hashiyat al-'Attar' (1:407), and others.
Source pages p. 596

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah deals with the legal effect of a master's acknowledgment of paternity (binuwwah) towards his slave. The issue is whether saying 'this is my son' establishes the slave's lineage and frees him. The author first presents the agreed-upon case: if the slave could reasonably be the master's son and is of unknown lineage, lineage is established and he becomes free, without the master needing to intend manumission. Then he discusses the disputed case: if the slave is of known lineage, lineage cannot be established, but Abu Hanifa still rules that the slave becomes free because the word 'son' is a metaphor for freedom. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) do not free him in this case. The dispute extends to cases where the slave is older than the master or the age difference makes literal sonship impossible. The core disagreement is about the conditions for using a metaphor (majaz). Abu Hanifa holds that the literal meaning need not be possible for the metaphor to be valid; it suffices that the word can be understood metaphorically. He compares it to calling a brave person 'lion' even though he is not a real lion. The Sahibayn require that the literal meaning be possible for the metaphorical meaning to be intended; otherwise the speech is meaningless. Abu Hanifa responds that the mind's transfer to the metaphorical meaning depends on understanding (fahm) the literal meaning, not on intending it (irada), so impossibility of intention does not prevent the metaphor. The author sides with Abu Hanifa, elaborating that when the literal meaning is impossible, the metaphorical meaning is determined and requires no additional intention. This passage illustrates a key Hanafi principle in usul al-fiqh regarding the operation of majaz.

Key Terms
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage of a word, where it is used to mean something other than its literal meaning due to a connection (alaka). In this passage, the connection is that freedom is a necessary consequence of paternity.
كناية (kinayah): An indirect expression that requires intention (niyyah) to determine the intended meaning because it has multiple possibilities.
نية (niyyah): Intention that specifies which of several possible meanings is intended. In the disputed case, if the word were kinayah, intention would be needed; but since it is majaz determined, no intention is required.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes the condition for the validity of a metaphor according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The literal meaning must be possible to intend.
B) The literal meaning must be understood, even if impossible to intend.
C) The literal meaning must be impossible.
D) The metaphor must be a kinayah that requires intention.

Key Takeaway
When literal paternity is impossible, the word 'son' becomes a determined metaphor for freedom, freeing the slave without the need for intention according to Abu Hanifa, and this is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Ruling on Using Divorce Wording for Emancipation
Dispute on whether 'talaq' effects emancipation with intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the word 'talaq' does not effect emancipation even with intention, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on the principle of metaphor (majaz) and necessary consequence (luzum).

Core Idea
A word effects its literal meaning unless a necessary relation (luzum) exists; 'talaq' lacks that for emancipation.

This lesson examines a key disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the use of divorce terminology to effect emancipation. At stake is the validity of an intended emancipation when a master says 'You are divorced' to his slave-girl. The Hanafi school, relying on principles of metaphor and linguistic necessity, denies that emancipation occurs, while the Shafi'i school permits it. Understanding the underlying reasoning will sharpen your grasp of Hanafi usul al-fiqh in practice.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Clearly identify what the Hanafi school rules: saying 'You are divorced' to a female slave with the intention of emancipation does not free her. Note that even if the master explicitly intends emancipation, the words do not effect it.
The core of the ruling: the word 'talaq' is not a valid expression for emancipation, regardless of intention.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi‘i (and the Shafi‘i school) holds that the slave-girl is emancipated because both 'talaq' and 'itq' remove a type of ownership, so each can be used metaphorically for the other.
The Shafi‘i argument is based on the conceptual overlap as removals of rights.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi‘i proof: both are removal of ownership (mulk), so they are related; metaphor is permissible between related terms.
The Shafi‘i view treats 'talaq' as a kinayah (indirect expression) for emancipation, relying on mutual connection.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response introduces a crucial condition for valid metaphor: the intended meaning must be a necessary consequence (lazim) of the literal meaning. While emancipation necessarily dissolves marriage (removing mut‘ah), divorce does not necessarily remove raqabah. Hence, the reverse metaphor fails. Additionally, linguistic roots differ: 'talaq' means release from confinement, 'itq' means strength, so they are not interchangeable.
The key concept is luzum (necessary consequence). The Hanafis use this principle to restrict metaphor application.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: under Hanafi law, the slave-girl remains a slave; under Shafi‘i law, she becomes free. This affects the legal status of subsequent acts (e.g., marriage, financial obligations).
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Ibn al-Humam equated 'talaq' to other kinayah words like 'la sabil', so he allowed emancipation with intention. However, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is the stricter view that intention does not suffice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولفظ الطلاق وكنايته مع نية العتق : فإنه إذا قال : لأمته أنتِ طالق ، ونوى به العتق ، لا تعتق عندنا . وعند الشافعي ( رحمه الله ) تعتق ؛ لأن الاعتاق، هو إزالة ملك الرقبة، والطلاق إزالة ملك المتعة، فيجوزُ إطلاق كلِّ واحدٍ منهما على الآخر مجازاً. قلنا : المجاز لفظ يُذكَرُ ويراد به لازمه ، وإزالة ملك المتعة لازم لإزالة ملك الرقبة ، فإنَّه إذا أعتق أمته يزولُ ملك المتعة، ولا لزوم على العكس. وحقق ابن الهمام في فتح القدير : إن عدم العتق بـه عند عدم النية ؛ إذ لا فرق بينه وبين لا سبيل ونحوه، فإذا نوى ثبت العتق ، ومثله في «البحر الرائق» و«النهر الفائق» وغيرهما. [وقد ذكر الشارح في التنقيح والتوضيح : أن المعنى اللغوي للطلاق ينبئ عن إزالة الحبس ورفع القيد، والمعنى اللغوي للعتاق منبئ عن القوة والغلبة، وفرعوا على هذا عدم جواز استعارة أحد اللفظين عن الآخر]
And the word of divorce and its kinayah with the intention of emancipation: if he says to his slave-girl 'You are divorced' and intends by it emancipation, she is not emancipated according to us (Hanafis). According to al-Shafi‘i (may Allah have mercy on him) she is emancipated, because emancipation is removal of ownership of the right of possession, and divorce is removal of ownership of the right of enjoyment, so it is permissible to use each of them metaphorically for the other. We said: metaphor is a word mentioned intending its necessary consequence, and removal of ownership of enjoyment is a necessary consequence of removal of ownership of the right of possession, for when he emancipates his slave-girl the ownership of enjoyment is removed, but there is no necessary consequence in the reverse. And Ibn al-Humam verified in Fath al-Qadir: that there is no emancipation by it when there is no intention, for there is no difference between it and 'la sabil' and the like; so if he intends, emancipation is established, and similar is in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and al-Nahr al-Fa'iq and others. [And the commentator mentioned in al-Tanqih and al-Tawdih: that the linguistic meaning of divorce indicates removal of confinement and lifting of restriction, and the linguistic meaning of emancipation indicates strength and dominance, and they derived from this the impermissibility of using one term metaphorically for the other.]
Source pages p. 599

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi‘i schools. It deals with the ruling when a master says to his female slave 'You are divorced' and intends emancipation. The Hanafi position is that she is not emancipated, because the word 'talaq' is specific to divorce, and can only be used metaphorically for emancipation if there is a necessary relation (luzum) between the two. Here, removing the right of enjoyment (mulk al-mut‘ah) is a necessary consequence of removing ownership of the right of possession (mulk al-raqabah) — i.e., if you free a slave, her marriage (if any) is automatically dissolved — but the reverse is not true: divorce does not necessarily remove ownership of the slave. Therefore, the metaphor only works one way. The Shafi‘i argument relies on mutual permissibility of metaphor. The Hanafi response clarifies that metaphor is only valid when the intended meaning is a necessary concomitant of the literal meaning. The text then quotes Ibn al-Humam's verification that without intention, there is no emancipation, but with intention, emancipation does occur, equating 'talaq' to other ambiguous words like 'la sabil'. However, the preferred view among Hanafis, as explained by the commentator in al-Tanqih and al-Tawdih, is that the linguistic differences between 'talaq' (divorce) and 'itq' (emancipation) are so fundamental that even with intention, one cannot be used for the other. This is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
عتق (‘itq): Emancipation of a slave, i.e., removal of the owner’s right of possession (mulk al-raqabah).
طلاق (talaq): Divorce, i.e., removal of the right of enjoyment (mulk al-mut‘ah) in marriage.
ملك الرقبة (mulk al-raqabah): Ownership of the right of possession over a slave, including the right to sell, use, etc.
ملك المتعة (mulk al-mut‘ah): Ownership of the right of enjoyment (sexual and marital benefits) over a female slave or a wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, does saying 'You are divorced' to one's female slave with the intention of emancipating her effect emancipation?
A) Yes, because the word implies release.
B) No, because the word 'talaq' is specific to divorce and cannot be used metaphorically for emancipation.
C) Yes, but only if the master explicitly states his intention.
D) No, because a slave cannot be divorced.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, using divorce words (talaq) cannot effect emancipation because the necessary relation (luzum) between the literal and intended meanings is absent; the relied-upon position is that intention does not make it valid.

8 min
Emancipation Not Effected by the Word Talaq
Inter-madhhab dispute over linguistic usage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on uttering 'talaq' to a slave and explain the Hanafi distinction between causal and non-reversible metaphors.

Core Idea
Using 'talaq' for a slave cannot effect emancipation because the causal link is one-way.

Can a master free his slave by saying 'You are divorced'? The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools disagree on this question, revealing a deeper debate about the nature of metaphorical language in Islamic law. This lesson examines the Hanafi position that the word 'talaq' is invalid for emancipation and explores the linguistic reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Recognize that the ruling concerns whether uttering 'talaq' to a slave effects emancipation. Note the two opposing positions.
The word 'talaq' (divorce) is for removing the marriage bond, not ownership.
2
Follow the Hanafi proof
Understand that the Hanafi argument rests on the nature of causality: removal of ownership (emancipation) is a cause for removal of the right to sexual enjoyment, but the reverse is not true. Thus, using 'talaq' (which removes the latter) for emancipation (which removes the former) is not valid as a metaphor (majaz).
Examples: eunuchs, mahram slaves, married slaves – where removal of ownership does not remove the right to enjoyment.
3
Examine the Shafi'i counter-proof
The Shafi'i school allows using 'talaq' for emancipation via isti'ara (metaphor based on resemblance) rather than causal majaz.
Isti'ara does not require a causal relation, only a resemblance.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rejection is that isti'ara requires a strong and distinctive resemblance (e.g., 'lion' for bravery), which is absent between 'talaq' (removing a bond) and 'itq' (granting power).
The text analogizes: one cannot intend 'earth' by saying 'sky'.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: In the Hanafi school, no emancipation occurs; in the Shafi'i school, it does.
This affects ownership status and future rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتاق الفاظه
وأما وقوع العتق بلفظ : الطلاق ؛ فيصح عند الشافعي ، ولا يصح عندنا ، وذلك لما مهدنا سابقاً أن المجاز الذي يكون بسبب علاقة اللزومِ والسببية إنما يصح من الطرفين إذا كانت السببية في معنى العلّة، وتوجد الملازمة، فيكون كل منهما أصلاً من وجه، وفرعاً من وجه، لا في السببية المحضة.
ومن المعلوم أن إزالة ملك الرقبة سبب محض لإزالة ملك المتعة، وليست علّة مؤثرة فيها، فكثيراً ما توجد إزالة ملك الرقبة من دون أن توجد هناك إزالة ملك المتعة، كما في عنق العبيد وعتق الإماء التي هي من محارم المعتق، أو الإماء المزوجة ؛ لعدم وجود ملك المتعة في هذه الصور حتى يزول بزوال ملك الرقبة.
وبالجملة : إزالة ملك الرقبة وإن كان سبباً لإزالة ملك المتعة لكنَّ الإزالة الثانية ليست بمقصودة منها ، وإزالة ملك المتعة وإن كانت لازمة لإزالة ملك الرقبة، لكن إزالة ملك الرقبة ليست لازمة لإزالة ملك المتعة، فلا يصح المجاز هاهنا من الطرف الآخر، وإنما يصح إطلاق اسم السبب وهو العتق على المسبب لا العكس، حتى يقع العتقُ بلفظ الطلاق صريحاً كان أو كناية.
فإن قلت : ينبغي أن يقع العتق بلفظ : الطلاق مع النية.
قلت : النيّة إنّما تعتبرُ بعد صحة استعمال اللفظ في المعنى المجازي، وهاهنا لا صحة لاستعمال لفظ الطلاق في العتاق لا حقيقة ولا مجازاً، فلا عبرة للنية، كما إذا نوى الأرض من لفظ السماء.
ثم اعلم أنه قد يوجه مذهب الشافعي له بأنه يمكن أن يطلق الطلاق ويراد به العتق على طريق الاستعارة المبنية على علاقة التشبيه لا على طريق المجاز المرسل بعلاقة السببية والمسببية، حتى يقال بعدم صحة المجاز من الطرفين.
وأجيب عنه : ، تارةً بأن الاستعارة لا تصح بكل وصف للقطع بامتناع استعارة السماء للأرض مع اشتراكهما في الوجود والحدوث، بل لا بد من وصف مشهور له زيادة اختصاص بالمستعار منه كشجاعة الأسد، وهذا غير متحقق في الطلاق والعتاق ؛ فإن العتاق عبارة عن إثباتِ القوّة المخصوصة، والطلاق عن إزالة القيد، فلا تشابه بين المعنيين في الوجه الذي شرعا عليه.
Book of Emancipation: Its Wording. As for the occurrence of emancipation by the word 'divorce': it is valid according to al-Shafi'i, and not valid according to us. This is due to what we established earlier: that metaphorical usage (majaz) based on a relation of necessary concomitance and causality is only valid from both sides if the causality is in the sense of an effective cause, and there exists inseparability, so each is in one respect an original and in another a derivative—not in pure causality. It is known that removal of ownership of the slave-girl (riqabah) is a pure cause for removal of the right to sexual enjoyment (mut'ah) and is not an effective cause in it. For frequently removal of ownership of the slave-girl occurs without removal of the right to sexual enjoyment, as in the case of emancipation of eunuchs and female slaves who are mahram to the master, or married female slaves, because there is no right to sexual enjoyment in these situations to be removed by removal of ownership. In summary: removal of ownership of the slave-girl, although it is a cause for removal of the right to sexual enjoyment, yet the second removal is not intended by it. And removal of the right to sexual enjoyment, although it is a necessary concomitant of removal of ownership of the slave-girl, yet removal of ownership of the slave-girl is not a necessary concomitant of removal of the right to sexual enjoyment. Hence metaphorical usage is not valid here from the other side. Only applying the name of the cause, which is 'itq, to the effect is valid, not the reverse, such that emancipation occurs by the word 'talaq' whether explicit or implicit. If you say: 'It should occur by the word 'talaq' with intention.' I say: 'Intention is only considered after the validity of using the word in its metaphorical sense, and here there is no validity in using the word 'talaq' for 'itq either literally or metaphorically, so intention is not considered, just as if one intends 'earth' from the word 'sky'.' Then know that the madhhab of al-Shafi'i may be justified by saying that one can utter 'talaq' and intend 'itq' by way of isti'ara (metaphor) based on relation of resemblance, not by way of majaz mursal with a causal relation, such that it is said that majaz from both sides is not valid. And it is answered: sometimes that isti'ara is not valid for every description, because it is certain that using 'sky' for 'earth' is prohibited despite their sharing existence and contingency. Rather, a well-known description having additional specificity to the borrowed-from is necessary, like the bravery of a lion. This is not realized in the case of talaq and 'itq, for 'itq is the establishment of a specific power, and talaq is the removal of a bond. Thus there is no resemblance between the two meanings in the aspect for which they were legislated.
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding whether a slave is emancipated by the word 'talaq' (divorce). The Hanafi position is that it does not effect emancipation. The reasoning is based on the nature of metaphorical usage (majaz). The author explains that for a metaphor based on causality to be reversible, the causal relationship must be mutual and necessary. Here, removing ownership of the slave (i.e., emancipation) is a cause for removing the right to sexual enjoyment, but the latter is not always present (e.g., in eunuchs, mahram, or married slaves). Thus, using the term for the effect (talaq) to refer to the cause (itq) is invalid because the two are not universally concomitant. Even intention does not help because the word 'talaq' has no valid usage—literal or metaphorical—for emancipation. The author then presents the Shafi'i view that this can be done via isti'ara (metaphor based on resemblance) rather than causal majaz. The Hanafi response argues that isti'ara requires a strong and specific resemblance, which is absent here since the two acts are fundamentally different. The passage concludes that the Hanafi ruling stands.

Key Terms
عتاق (itq): Emancipation of a slave; removal of ownership of the slave (milk al-riqabah).
طلاق (talaq): Divorce; removal of the marriage bond (izalat al-qayd).
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage of a word for something other than its original meaning.
استعارة (isti'ara): Metaphor based on resemblance between the original and the new meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school hold that uttering the word 'talaq' to a slave does not effect emancipation?
A) Because 'talaq' is a word specific to marriage and cannot be used for anything else.
B) Because the causal relationship between removing ownership of the slave and removing the right to sexual enjoyment is not mutual; the former does not always lead to the latter.
C) Because the slave does not understand the word 'talaq'.
D) Because the intention is not present.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the word 'talaq' cannot be used for emancipation because the causal relationship between removing ownership and removing the marriage bond is not mutual.

Rulings on Expressions Implying Freedom
Ruling on phrases that may imply emancipation of a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for three types of emancipation expressions and apply the rule that 'mithl' requires intention while the negative-exceptive construction does not.

Core Idea
Saying 'you are like a free person' frees only if intended; saying 'you are nothing but free' frees immediately.

In Hanafi law, the words a master uses to declare a slave free matter greatly. Some phrases are unambiguous, others require intention. This lesson examines three key expressions derived from the text of al-Hidayah and its commentaries, clarifying when emancipation takes effect and when it does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three expressions
The passage discusses three types of statements: 'anta mithl al-hurr', 'ma anta illa hurr', and the general phrase 'man malaka' (whoever owns). Note the different linguistic structures.
2
Analyze the effect of 'mithl'
The word 'mithl' indicates similarity, not identity, creating doubt. Therefore, emancipation does not occur unless the master intended it. Understand the reasoning from al-Hidayah.
Intention removes the doubt.
3
Analyze the negative-exceptive construction
The phrase 'ma anta illa hurr' uses exception after negation, which in Arabic affirms the predicate. Thus, it is a clear declaration of freedom and requires no intention.
Analogous to 'La ilaha illa Allah'.
4
Understand the general ownership phrase
The phrase 'man malaka' is general and applies unconditionally, even if the owner is a child, insane, or disbeliever. Emancipation is immediate.
5
Apply the rulings to examples
Work through scenarios: master says 'you are like a free person' without intending – no freedom. With intention – freedom. Master says 'you are nothing but free' – freedom regardless of intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنت مثل الحر بخلاف ما أنت إلا حرّ ، ومَن مَلَكَ
(وأنت مثل الحر ) (۱) بخلاف ما أنت إلا حرّ . ومَن مَلَكَ
وفيه بحث لما عرفت من أنّ العتاق موضوع لإزالة ملك الرقبة، لا لإثبات القوة المخصوصة، بل هو من لوازمه، وتارة بأنّ في الاستعارة يجب أن يكون المستعار منه أقوى في وجه الشبه كالأسد في الشجاعة، وهو منتف هاهنا، فإنّ إزالة ملك المتعة ليست بأقوى من إزالة ملك الرقبة، بل الأمر بالعكس، وليست إزالة ملك المتعة لازمة لها أيضاً.
(۲)
وفيه بحث، وهو أن هذا الاشتراط إنّما هو في بعض أقسام الاستعارة، وقد تكون الاستعارة مبنية على التشابه ، من دون اعتبار القوة، كاستعارة الصبح لـغـرة الفرس وبالعكس، وفي المقام أبحاث مبسوطة في «التلويح))) [١] قوله : وأنت مثلُ الحرّ ; عطف على قوله : يا ابني»، وظاهره أنه لا يعتق به مطلقاً، ووجه صاحب الهداية»(۳) بأنّ المثل يستعمل للمشاركة في بعض المعاني عرفاً، فوقع الشك في الحرية، وهذا التعليلُ يرشدك إلى وقوع العتق به إذا نوى لارتفاع الشك حينئذ، و به صرَّحَ صاحب «العناية» (٤) نقلاً عن «المبسوط» وابن الهمام) والزيلعي والأتقاني وغيرهم. [۳] قوله : ومن ملك...الخ ; أشار بإطلاقه إلى أنه يعتق عليه، وإن كان المالك صبياً أو مجنوناً أو كافراً لا يقال : كيف يعتق على الصبي والمجنون مع أنهما ليسا من أهلِ
And 'You are like a free person' — unlike 'You are nothing but free,' and 'Whoever owns...' (1) [Commentary:] 'You are like a free person' (1) unlike 'You are nothing but free.' And 'Whoever owns...' There is discussion here based on what you know: that 'itq is established for removing ownership of the slave-girl, not for establishing a specific power, rather that is a consequence. And sometimes that in isti'ara, the borrowed-from must be stronger in the aspect of resemblance, like the lion in bravery, which is absent here. For removal of the right to sexual enjoyment is not stronger than removal of ownership of the slave-girl; rather the opposite, and removal of the right to sexual enjoyment is also not necessary for it. (2) There is discussion: that this condition is only in some types of isti'ara, and isti'ara may be based on resemblance without considering strength, like using 'morning' for the horse's forehead and vice versa. There are extensive discussions on this in al-Talwih. [1] His statement: 'And you are like a free person' is a conjunction to his statement: 'O my son.' Its apparent meaning is that he is not freed by it at all. The author of al-Hidayah explained that 'mithl' is used customarily for participation in some meanings, so doubt arises about freedom. This explanation indicates that emancipation does occur if he intends, because the doubt is then removed. This was explicitly stated by the author of al-Inayah, quoting from al-Mabsut, Ibn al-Humam, al-Zayla'i, al-Atqani, and others. [2] His statement: 'unlike' means that if he says to his slave: 'You are nothing but free,' he is freed because the exception from negation is affirmation, as in 'La ilaha illa Allah.' But if he says: 'You are nothing but like a free person,' emancipation does not occur by it, as in al-Muhit. [3] His statement: 'And whoever owns...' etc. indicates by its generality that the slave is freed from him, even if the owner is a child, insane, or disbeliever. It cannot be said: 'How can the slave be freed from a child or insane person when they are not among those who...'
Source pages p. 604

Commentary
This passage presents the rulings for various expressions that could imply emancipation. The main rule: if a master says to his slave, 'You are like a free person,' emancipation does not occur unless he intended it. This is because the word 'mithl' (like) indicates participation in some attributes, creating doubt about actual freedom. If intention is present, the doubt is removed and emancipation takes effect. In contrast, if he says 'You are nothing but free' (ma anta illa hurr), emancipation occurs immediately because of the negative-exceptive construction, which affirms the predicate. The passage also mentions that if someone says 'Whoever owns...' in a general context, the slave is freed, even if the owner is a child, insane, or disbeliever. The commentary includes a digression on the definition of 'itq and isti'ara, but the core rulings are clear.

Key Terms
مثل الحر (mithl al-hurr): A phrase meaning 'like a free person'; in Hanafi law, this alone does not effect emancipation unless intended.
استثناء من النفي (istithna min al-nafi): Exception after negation is considered affirmative in Arabic grammar, e.g., 'ma anta illa hurr' means 'you are free.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says to his slave, 'You are nothing but free' (ما أنت إلا حر), what is the ruling?
A) The slave is not freed because the phrase is ambiguous.
B) The slave is freed immediately due to the negative-exceptive construction.
C) The slave is only freed if the master intended it.
D) The slave is freed only if witnessed.

Key Takeaway
The word 'mithl' requires intention for emancipation, while the negative-exceptive construction 'ma...illa' effects immediate freedom.

Rulings of Emancipatory Phrases: 'You are like a free person' vs 'You are nothing but free'
Critical analysis of two emancipatory expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for both emancipatory phrases, explain the role of intention (niyyah) in the first, and identify the linguistic principle (istithna min al-nafi) that renders the second immediately effective.

Core Idea
Intention decides freedom when words resemble; affirmation is automatic when exception follows negation.

Emancipation is a serious legal act in Hanafi fiqh, and the words used matter profoundly. This passage clarifies how two common expressions—'you are like a free person' and 'you are nothing but free'—produce different legal effects. The difference hinges on the linguistic principle that exception from negation affirms the excepted term, while the word 'like' introduces ambiguity that only the speaker's intention can resolve.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two expressions and their apparent rulings
Extract the two phrases from the passage: 'أنت مثل الحر' and 'ما أنت إلا حر'. Note that the matn states the first does not effect emancipation, while the second does.
The second phrase uses 'الاستثناء من النفي', which linguistically turns a negation into an affirmation.
2
Analyse the linguistic reasoning for each phrase
For 'أنت مثل الحر': the word 'مثل' (like) indicates similarity in some aspects, not full identity, creating doubt. For 'ما أنت إلا حر': the exception from negation ('إلا' after negation) affirms the excepted term (حر), so freedom is established.
The principle 'الاستثناء من النفي إثبات' is a core linguistic tool in Hanafi usul.
3
Understand the critical refinement regarding intention
The author of al-Hidayah held that 'أنت مثل الحر' does not emancipate at all. However, later authorities (al-Mabsut, al-Inayah, Ibn al-Humam) clarified: if the speaker intended emancipation, the doubt disappears and the slave is freed. This refinement is based on the reasoning that 'مثل' creates doubt, and intention removes that doubt.
This refinement is the 'critical refinement' of the lesson: the earlier absolute position is qualified by intention.
4
Consider the metaphorical argument and its refutation
The passage mentions a discussion about metaphors (استعارة): some argue that in a metaphor, the borrowed term must be stronger in the point of similarity (e.g., lion for courage). The refutation states that this condition applies only to some types of metaphors, and metaphors can also be based on mere resemblance without strength. This discussion is tangential but shows the depth of legal reasoning.
The main point is that the metaphor argument does not affect the ruling; the decisive factor is the customary use of 'مثل'.
5
Summarize the mufta bihi position and authority weight
The relied-upon position: 'أنت مثل الحر' does not effect emancipation unless the speaker intended it. 'ما أنت إلا حر' effects emancipation unconditionally. The base ruling from al-Hidayah (zahir riwayah) is modified by later fatwa authorities (fatawa mutakhkhirin) for the first phrase.
The authority_weight is mixed: zahir riwayah for the base, but the conditional acceptance is from later texts, making it fatawa mutakhkhirin for the conditional part.

Source Text

And you are like a free person, contrary to you are nothing but free. And whoever owns... (And you are like a free person) (1) contrary to you are nothing but free. And whoever owns... There is discussion because you know that emancipation is intended to remove ownership of the slave, not to establish a specific strength, but rather it is from its necessary consequences. And sometimes it is said that in metaphor, the borrowed-from must be stronger in the point of similarity, like the lion in courage, but this is absent here, for removing ownership of usufruct is not stronger than removing ownership of the physical body; rather the opposite is true, and removing ownership of usufruct is not necessarily entailed by it either. (2) There is discussion, namely that this condition applies only in some types of metaphor, and metaphor may be based on resemblance without consideration of strength, like the metaphor of dawn for the white blaze on a horse's forehead and vice versa. There are extensive investigations on this matter in al-Talwih. [1] His statement: 'And you are like a free person' is conjoined to his statement 'O my son', and its apparent meaning is that it does not effect emancipation at all. The author of al-Hidayah justified it by saying that 'like' is customarily used for sharing in some attributes, so doubt occurs about freedom, and this reasoning indicates that it does effect emancipation if he intended [freedom], for then doubt is removed. This was expressly stated by the author of al-Inayah, quoting from al-Mabsut, Ibn al-Humam, al-Zayla'i, al-Atqani, and others. [2] His statement: 'contrary to'; meaning if he says to his slave: 'You are nothing but free' he becomes free, because exception from negation is affirmation, as in 'There is no god but Allah'. And if he says: 'You are nothing but like a free person', it does not effect emancipation, as in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 604

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of two expressions used for emancipation. The commentary opens by explaining why 'you are like a free person' does not automatically emancipate: the word 'like' implies similarity in some aspects, not identity, so doubt remains. However, if the speaker intended full freedom, it becomes effective. In contrast, 'you are nothing but free' uses an exception from negation, which gives affirmation and thus immediate emancipation. The author engages with metaphorical reasoning and cites the views of major Hanafi scholars, highlighting the importance of intention (niyyah) in ambiguous expressions.

Key Terms
المثل (al-mithl): In this context, it means 'like' or 'similar', used in the phrase 'أنت مثل الحر' to indicate resemblance but not identity, leaving doubt about the intended meaning.
الاستثناء من النفي (al-istithna min al-nafi): The rhetorical device of making an exception from a negation, which results in affirmation of the excepted item, as in 'ما أنت إلا حر'.
النية (al-niyyah): Intention of the speaker; in this context, the intention to actually emancipate the slave when using an ambiguous expression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on saying to a slave 'أنت مثل الحر' (you are like a free person)?
A) The slave becomes free immediately.
B) The slave becomes free only if the owner intended emancipation.
C) The slave does not become free under any circumstances.
D) The slave becomes free if the owner repeats it three times.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between ambiguous and unambiguous emancipatory phrases: intention resolves ambiguity in 'like' expressions, while exception-from-negation expressions are immediately effective.
