8 min
Jointly Owned Slave with Contradictory Conditions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sa'ayah for partially freed slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions and the intra-madhhab dispute regarding sa'ayah for a jointly-owned slave with contradictory conditional manumissions, and apply the principle of certainty to resolve the dispute.

Core Idea
Certainty is not removed by doubt: half the sa'ayah is certainly dropped, so the remaining half is divided.

This lesson examines a case where two partners conditionally manumit their shares of a jointly-owned slave based on contradictory conditions, and the fulfillment of either condition is unknown. The school's Imams differ: Muhammad holds that the slave works (sa'ayah) for the full value, while Abu Hanifa holds that he works for only half, divided between them. Understanding this dispute requires grasping how the Hanafi school prioritizes certainty over doubt in legal obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two partners each conditionally manumit their share of a jointly-owned slave on contradictory events (e.g., one says 'if X enters tomorrow', the other says 'if X does not enter tomorrow'). After the appointed time, the occurrence of either condition is unknown.
2
Determine the immediate effect
Despite the unknown condition, half the slave is certainly free because one condition must have occurred. The other half remains enslaved.
3
Muhammad's view on sa'ayah
Muhammad holds that the slave must work for the full value for both partners. His reasoning: the partner whose sa'ayah is dropped is unknown, and judgment cannot be passed on an unknown.
4
Abu Hanifa's rebuttal
Abu Hanifa argues that half the sa'ayah is certainly dropped (since one condition occurred). The remaining half is disputed between the partners, so it is divided equally to avoid preference without evidence.
5
Practical difference and mufta bihi
Under Muhammad, the slave works for full value; under Abu Hanifa, for half the value divided equally. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view, as stated in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عتقه بفعل غداً، والآخر بعدمه فمضى الغد، وجهل شرط عتق نصفه، وسعى في نصفه لهما، وعند محمد ه سعى في كلّه) : لأنَّ المَقْضِي عليه بسقوطِ السعاية مجهول، فلا يمكن القضاء على المجهول. علّق عتقه بعدم ذلك الفعل كان يقول أحدهما مثلاً : إن دخل فلان هذه البلدة غداً فأنت حر، أي نصيبي منك حرّ، ويقول الآخر: إن لم يدخل فـلان هـذه البلدة غداً فأنت حر. ففي هذه الصورة إذا جاءَ الغد ومضى ، فإن علم أحد الشرطين ؛ أي دخولُ فلان هذه البلدة أو عدمه فذلك ؛ أي يعتق نصيب الذي علّق عتقه بذلك لا نصيب الآخر؛ لعدم وجودِ شرطه إن جهل ذلك ولم يعلم أنه دخل أو لم يدخل، عتق نصفه وسعى في نصفه لهما. [ ١ ] قوله : غداً ؛ هكذا في بعض النسخ ، وفي بعضها: بفعل غد بالإضافة، والمعنى واحد: أي بفعل يقع غداً، وذكر الغد على سبيل التمثيل، فإنّ الحكم منوط بتعليق العتق بفعل في وقت معين ، غداً كان أو أمس أو اليوم. [٢]قوله : سعى في كلّه ؛ أي سعى العبد في جميع قيمته لـهـمـا ، هذا إذا كانـا معسرين، وإن كانا موسرين فلا سعاية ، وإن كان أحدهما موسراً والآخر معسراً سعى في نصف قيمته للموسر ؛ لأنّ المعسر يتبرأ عن السعاية ، والموسر يدعيها، فإنّ يسار المعتق يمنع وجوب السعاية على أصله. كذا في «العناية». (۳) قوله : لأن المقضي عليه ...الخ ؛ حاصله : أنه لما جهل الشرط ولم يعلم أتحقق الشرط الذي علق عليه عتقه هذا الشريك، أو تحقق الشرط الذي علّق عليه الآخر، صار المقضي عليه بسقوط السعاية مجهولاً ، فإنّ الشريك الذي وجد شرطه لا تجب السعاية له، بل سعاية نصفه للآخر، ويسقط حق السعاية عنه، وهو غير معلوم، والقضاء على المجهول غير ممكن، فلا يحكم بسقوط السعاية لأحدهما، بل يحكم بأن يسعى في كلّه. (٤) قوله : لأن المقضي عليه ؛ اللام موصولة: أي الذي قضى عليه بسقوط السعاية له، وهو الحانث منهما. قلنا : نصف السعاية ساقط بيقين وكلُّ واحدٍ من الشريكين، يقولُ لصاحيه : إن النصف الباقي هو نصيبي ، والساقط نصيبك، فينصف بينهما.
His manumission is conditioned on an act tomorrow, and the other on its non-occurrence. Then tomorrow passes, and the condition for freeing half is unknown, so he works for half for both. According to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), he works for all of it: because the one who is judged to have the sa'ayah dropped is unknown, so judgment cannot be passed on the unknown. He conditioned his manumission on the non-occurrence of that act — for example, one says: 'If so-and-so enters this town tomorrow, you are free,' i.e., my share of you is free, and the other says: 'If so-and-so does not enter this town tomorrow, you are free.' In this scenario, when tomorrow comes and passes, if one of the two conditions is known, i.e., the entry or non-entry of so-and-so, then that is it: the share of the one whose condition is attached is freed, not the other's share, due to the absence of his condition. If that is unknown and it is not known whether he entered or not, then half of him is freed and he works for half for both. [1] His saying: 'tomorrow' — thus in some copies, and in others: 'by an act of tomorrow' as an idafa, and the meaning is the same: i.e., by an act that occurs tomorrow. Mentioning tomorrow is by way of example, for the ruling is tied to the conditional manumission by an act at a specific time, whether it be tomorrow, yesterday, or today. [2] His saying: 'he works for all of it' — i.e., the slave works for the entire value for both. This is when both are insolvent. If both are solvent, then no sa'ayah. If one is solvent and the other insolvent, he works for half his value for the solvent one, because the insolvent one is absolved of sa'ayah, and the solvent one claims it, for the wealth of the manumitter prevents the obligation of sa'ayah according to his principle. Thus in al-'Inayah. (3) His saying: 'because the one against whom judgment is passed...' — its summary: since the condition is unknown and it is not known whether the condition upon which this partner attached his manumission occurred, or the condition upon which the other attached his occurred, the one against whom sa'ayah is dropped becomes unknown. Indeed, the partner whose condition occurred does not owe sa'ayah, but rather the sa'ayah of his half is for the other, and his right to sa'ayah drops, and he is unknown. Judgment on the unknown is not possible, so it is not ruled that sa'ayah is dropped for either, but rather it is ruled that he works for all of it. (4) His saying: 'because the one against whom judgment is passed' — the lam is a relative pronoun: i.e., the one who is judged to have sa'ayah dropped for him, and he is the one whose condition is broken. We said: half of the sa'ayah is certainly dropped, and each of the two partners says to his companion: 'The remaining half is my share, and the dropped half is your share,' so it is divided in half between them.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a jointly-owned slave where each partner conditionally manumits his share based on contradictory conditions (e.g., one says 'if X enters town tomorrow, my share is free'; the other says 'if X does not enter, my share is free'). After the appointed time passes, the fulfillment of either condition is unknown. The passage first presents Imam Muhammad's view: the slave must work (sa'ayah) for the full value for both partners, because the partner whose sa'ayah is dropped (i.e., the one whose condition was fulfilled) is unknown, and judgment cannot be passed on an unknown person. Then the commentary explains the reasoning and nuances: the ruling depends on solvency of the masters; if both are insolvent the slave works for all; if both solvent no sa'ayah; if one solvent, only works for that one's share. Then Abu Hanifa's rebuttal (قلنا) is given: half of the sa'ayah is certainly dropped (since one condition must have occurred), and the remaining half is disputed; to avoid unjust preference, it is divided equally between the two partners. This is the relied-upon position. The text clarifies technical terms like 'sa'ayah' (work to earn freedom) and 'al-hanith' (the one whose condition is fulfilled).

Key Terms
السعاية (al-sa'ayah): Work performed by a partially manumitted slave to earn the remaining portion of his value to gain full freedom.
الحانث (al-hanith): The one whose conditional manumission condition has been fulfilled (i.e., the partner whose condition occurred).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, in the case of a jointly-owned slave with contradictory conditional manumissions, how much sa'ayah (work) does the slave owe?
A) He owes sa'ayah for the full value to both partners.
B) He owes sa'ayah for half his value, divided between both partners.
C) He owes no sa'ayah at all.
D) He owes sa'ayah for half his value only to the partner whose condition is known.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, when contradictory conditional manumissions are unknown, half the sa'ayah is certainly dropped, and the remaining half is divided equally between the partners, following the principle that certainty is not removed by doubt.

No Manumission for Two Separate Slaves with Contradictory Conditions
Ruling that neither slave is freed when conditions on separate slaves conflict

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two separate slaves are subject to contradictory conditional manumissions and explain why excessive ambiguity prevents manumission.

Core Idea
When two slaves are separately conditioned with opposite outcomes, neither is freed because the uncertainty is legally excessive.

This passage addresses a specific scenario of conditional manumission: two owners each have their own slave and attach contradictory conditions to the same event (e.g., one says 'if X enters, my slave is free' and the other says 'if X does not enter, my slave is free'). If the outcome is unknown, neither slave is freed. Understanding this ruling reveals how Hanafi law treats excessive ambiguity in legal acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two separate slaves owned by different individuals; each imposes a conditional manumission based on the same event but with opposite conditions. Example: one owner says 'if Zayd enters the house tomorrow, my slave is free'; the other says 'if Zayd does not enter the house tomorrow, my slave is free'.
The two slaves are distinct and owned separately.
2
State the ruling
When tomorrow passes and it is unknown whether the event occurred or not, neither slave is freed. The manumission is completely void.
The ruling is batil (invalid) due to excessive uncertainty.
3
Analyze the reason
The one who would be effectively manumitted (the slave whose condition is fulfilled) and the one entitled to the manumission (the beneficiary) are both unknown. This leads to 'fahushat al-jahalah' (excessive ignorance), which prevents legal effect.
Fahushat al-jahalah is a key concept in Hanafi fiqh meaning uncertainty so great it invalidates the action.
4
Distinguish from the single-slave case
In the single-slave case (e.g., two partners in one slave), the slave himself is known; only the partner breaking the condition is unknown. The known overrides the unknown, so half the sa'ayah is dropped. Here, both the subject and object of manumission are unknown, so no effect occurs.
This contrast shows how the number of unknowns determines validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا عتق في عبدين ) : أي إذا قال رجلٌ : إن دخل فلانٌ الدَّارَ غداً، فعبده حرّ، وقال الآخر: إن لم يدخل فلانٌ الدَّارَ غدا ، فعبدُهُ حرّ، فمضى، ولم يدر أنه دَخَلَ أو لا، لا يعتق شيء من العبدين ؛ لأنَّ المقضي عليه بالعتق والمقضي له مجهولان، ففحشت الجهالة (1) [١] قوله : في عبدين ؛ يعني إذا كان لكل منهما عبد على حدة، فقال أحدهما : إن دخل فلان الدار غـداً فـعـبدي حر، أو قال الآخر: إن لم يدخل فلان الدار غداً فعبدي حرّ، ومضى الغد ولم يعلم الدخولُ وعدمه، فلا يعتق واحد منهما. [٤] قوله : ففحشت ؛ من الفحش - بضمّ الفاء وسكون الحاء المهملة - بمعنى : التجاوز عن الحد ؛ أي كثرت الجهالة في هذه الصورة حيث لم يعلم المقضي له ولا (۱) بخلاف العبد الواحد ؛ لأن المقضي له بسقوط نصف السعاية معلوم، وهو العبد، والمقضي به وهو سقوط نصف السعاية ،معلوم والمجهول واحد وهو الحانث فغلب المعلوم المجهول. ينظر : مجمع الأنهر» (١ : ٥١٨).
And there is no manumission in two slaves: i.e., if a man says: 'If so-and-so enters the house tomorrow, his slave is free,' and another says: 'If so-and-so does not enter the house tomorrow, his slave is free,' then tomorrow passes and he does not know whether he entered or not, nothing of the two slaves is free, because the one judged to be manumitted and the one entitled to it are both unknown, so the ignorance is excessive. [1] His saying: 'in two slaves' — meaning if each of them has a separate slave, and one says: 'If so-and-so enters the house tomorrow, my slave is free,' or the other says: 'If so-and-so does not enter the house tomorrow, my slave is free,' then tomorrow passes and it is not known whether entry occurred or not, then neither of them is free. [4] His saying: 'so the ignorance is excessive' — from al-fuhsh (with damma on fa and sukun on ha), meaning exceeding the limit; i.e., the ignorance is great in this scenario where neither the one entitled to manumission nor the one judged to be free is known. (1) Unlike the case of a single slave, because the one entitled to dropping half the sa'ayah is known (the slave), and what is judged (dropping half the sa'ayah) is known, and the unknown is only one (the one whose condition is broken), so the known overrides the unknown. See: Majma' al-Anhur (1:518).
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage presents a distinct ruling: when each owner has his own separate slave, and they attach contradictory conditions (e.g., one says 'if X enters, my slave is free'; the other says 'if X does not enter, my slave is free'), and the outcome is unknown, neither slave is freed. The reason is that the one to be effectively manumitted and the one entitled to the manumission are both unknown (which slave's condition was fulfilled?). This results in excessive ambiguity (فحشت الجهالة). The commentary clarifies that the phrase 'in two slaves' refers to two separate slaves owned individually. The footnote distinguishes this from the single-slave case: there, the entitlement to half the sa'ayah is known (the slave himself is known, though the partner with the fulfilled condition is not), so the known overcomes the unknown. Here, both the subject and object of manumission are unknown, so no effect takes place.

Key Terms
فحشت الجهالة (fahushat al-jahalah): The ignorance (uncertainty) is excessive; used when ambiguity is so great that it prevents legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are neither of the two slaves freed in the case of two separate slaves with contradictory conditional manumissions?
A) Because the conditions are contradictory.
B) Because the one to be manumitted and the one entitled to it are both unknown.
C) Because the owners are insolvent.
D) Because the conditions are not fulfilled.

Key Takeaway
When two separate slaves are subject to contradictory conditional manumissions, neither is freed because the excessive uncertainty incapacitates the legal effect.

Contradictory Conditional Manumissions: Abu Hanifa's Resolution
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional manumissions with unknown outcome

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Abu Hanifa's position on contradictory conditional manumissions and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute with Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
When two contradictory conditions are equally possible, half of each slave is freed and earnings split—certainty overcomes ignorance.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi disagreement about the fate of two slaves when their owners' conditions are contradictory and the outcome unknown. While the matn declares no emancipation due to excessive ignorance, Abu Hanifa argues that half of each slave must be freed because certainty of one condition's occurrence overrides the ignorance of which one.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Two owners each make a conditional statement about their own slave: one says 'if X enters, my slave is free'; the other says 'if X does not enter, my slave is free'. The day passes and it is unknown whether X entered.
The conditions are mutually exclusive and exhaustive; one must be true.
2
Identify the matn's ruling and reasoning
The matn states 'no emancipation' because both the person entitled and the person liable for emancipation are unknown, leading to excessive ignorance (tafahush al-jahalah).
This is the apparent position of the matn.
3
Examine Abu Hanifa's counter-argument
Abu Hanifa argues that one condition definitely occurred, so half of each slave is freed. The dropping of half the earnings is certain. The ignorance of which owner is due the earnings is resolved by splitting the half equally.
Analogy: if a man emancipates one of two unspecified slaves and dies, half of each is freed.
4
Compare with Muhammad's implied position
Muhammad al-Shaybani likely held that no earnings are due or distributed differently, possibly based on the excessive ignorance. The text refutes this by showing the ignorance is not harmful.
The relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's as defended in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتاق / باب عتق البعض
ولا عتق في عبدين
قلنا : نصف السعاية ساقط بيقين وكلُّ واحدٍ من الشريكين، يقولُ لصاحيه : إن النصف الباقي هو نصيبي ، والساقط نصيبك، فينصف " بينهما. (ولا عتق في عبدين ) : أي إذا قال رجلٌ : إن دخل فلانٌ الدَّارَ غداً، فعبده حرّ، وقال الآخر: إن لم يدخل فلانٌ الدَّارَ غدا ، فعبدُهُ حرّ، فمضى، ولم يدر أنه دَخَلَ أو لا، لا يعتق شيء من العبدين ؛ لأنَّ المقضي عليه بالعتق والمقضي له مجهولان، ففحشت الجهالة (1)
[١] قوله : قلنا ؛ جواب من قبل أبي حنيفة له عن دَلـيـل مـحـمـد لله، وإثبات لمذهبه، وحاصله : إن أحد الشريكين في الصورة المذكورة حانت بيقين، وهو الذي وجد شرطه فعـتـق نـصـف العبد، وسقوط نصف السعاية متيقن ، فمع ذلك كيف يحكمُ بوجوب السعاية على العبد في جميع قيمته.
وأما كون المقضي عليه مجهولاً فغير مضر، فإنّ الجهالة ترتفع بشيوع النصف الذي عتق، وبتوزيع نصف السعاية عليهما، ونظيره ما إذا أعتق رجل أحد عبديه لا بعينه ، ومات قبل البيان، أو أعتق عبديه بعينه ونسيه ومات قبل التذكر ؛ فإنّه يعتق من كلّ منهما نصفه، وتجب السعاية في نصفه على كلّ واحدٍ منهما. كذا في «البناية»(۲). [٢] قوله : فينصف ؛ أي إذا كان كلّ منهما مدعياً لسقوط حصة الآخر، وسقوطُ النصف متيقن ينصف ذلك النصف الواجب بينهما ؛ دفعاً للزوم الترجيح بلا مرجّح ، ورفعاً للمنازعة. [٣]قوله : في عبدين ؛ يعني إذا كان لكل منهما عبد على حدة، فقال أحدهما : إن دخل فلان الدار غـداً فـعـبدي حر، أو قال الآخر: إن لم يدخل فلان الدار غداً فعبدي حرّ، ومضى الغد ولم يعلم الدخولُ وعدمه، فلا يعتق واحد منهما. [٤] قوله : ففحشت ؛ من الفحش - بضمّ الفاء وسكون الحاء المهملة - بمعنى : التجاوز عن الحد ؛ أي كثرت الجهالة في هذه الصورة حيث لم يعلم المقضي له ولا المقضي عليه، وفي صورة العبد الواحد المقضي به، وهو عتق نصف العبد، والمقضي له ، وهو العبد الواحد معلوم، فلم تتفاحش الجهالة.
Book of Emancipation / Chapter on Partial Emancipation
And there is no emancipation regarding two slaves.
We said: Half of the earnings (sa'y) is definitely dropped, and each of the two partners says to his companion: 'The remaining half is my share, and the dropped half is your share,' so it is divided equally between them. (And there is no emancipation regarding two slaves): That is, if a man said: 'If so-and-so enters the house tomorrow, his slave is free,' and another said: 'If so-and-so does not enter the house tomorrow, his slave is free,' and the day passed and it is not known whether he entered or not, nothing of the two slaves is emancipated, because the one against whom emancipation is decreed and the one for whom it is decreed are both unknown, so the ignorance is excessive.
[1] His statement: 'We said' is a response from Abu Hanifa to Muhammad's evidence, and an affirmation of his own madhhab. Its summary: One of the two partners in the mentioned scenario definitely became liable (hanith), namely the one whose condition was fulfilled, so half of the slave is emancipated, and the dropping of half of the earnings is certain. So despite that, how can it be ruled that the full value of the slave is due as earnings? As for the fact that the one against whom it is decreed is unknown, that is not harmful, because the ignorance is removed by the dispersal of the half that was emancipated and by distributing half of the earnings between them. An analogy is the case where a man emancipates one of his two slaves without specification and dies before clarifying, or emancipates both of his slaves specifically but forgets and dies before remembering: then half of each of them is emancipated, and earnings are due for half of each. This is recorded in al-Binaya. [2] His statement: 'so it is divided equally' means that when each of them claims the dropping of the other's share, and the dropping of half is certain, that obligatory half is divided equally between them to avoid preferring one without a preference and to remove dispute. [3] His statement: 'regarding two slaves' means that if each of them owns a slave separately, and one said: 'If so-and-so enters the house tomorrow, my slave is free,' and the other said: 'If so-and-so does not enter the house tomorrow, my slave is free,' and the next day passes without knowledge of entry or non-entry, then neither of the two slaves is emancipated. [4] His statement: 'so it is excessive' (fahishat) from al-fuhsh (with damm on the fa and sukun on the important ha) meaning exceeding the limit; that is, the ignorance is abundant in this scenario where neither the one for whom nor the one against whom the decree is made is known. In contrast, in the scenario of a single slave, the one against whom the decree is made (the emancipation of half the slave) and the one for whom it is decreed (the single slave) are known, so the ignorance is not excessive.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on two slaves subject to contradictory conditional manumissions. The matn states the ruling: if two men each make a conditional statement about their own slaves—one saying 'if X enters the house, my slave is free' and the other saying 'if X does not enter, my slave is free'—and the day passes without knowing whether X entered or not, then neither slave is freed. The reason given is that both the person against whom emancipation is decreed and the person for whom it is decreed are unknown, leading to excessive ignorance (tafahush al-jahalah). The commentary then presents Abu Hanifa's argument against Muhammad al-Shaybani. Abu Hanifa argues that in the scenario, one of the partners definitely became liable (hanith) because one condition must have been fulfilled. Since half of the slave's earnings (sa'y) is definitely dropped, it is inconsistent to rule that the slave owes earnings for the entire value. Furthermore, the ignorance of which partner is liable is removed by dividing the half equally between them, as each claims the other's share is dropped. The commentary provides an analogy: if a man emancipates one of two unspecified slaves and dies without clarifying, or emancipates both but forgets and dies, then half of each slave is freed and each owes earnings for half. This shows that when the specific recipient is unknown, the half is distributed. Thus, Abu Hanifa's position is that half of each slave is freed and the earnings are divided equally. The opposing view (Muhammad's) is implied but not detailed; the text focuses on refuting it. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa, as he is the one who provides the analogy and rationale.

Key Terms
جهالة متفاحشة (jahalah mutafahishah): Excessive ignorance that prevents legal certainty; here, where both the person entitled to the emancipation and the person against whom it is decreed are unknown.
سعاية (sa'y): The earnings acquired through the slave's work; when a part-slave is freed, he must work to earn money to compensate his owner for the unfreed portion.
عتق البعض (itq al-ba'd): The legal situation where a slave is partially freed (e.g., half), leading to rules of compensation via sa'y.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where two slaves are subject to contradictory conditions (one says 'if X enters, my slave is free', the other says 'if X does not enter, my slave is free') and the outcome is unknown, what is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa as presented in this passage?
A) Both slaves are fully freed and no compensation is due.
B) Neither slave is freed due to excessive ignorance.
C) Half of each slave is freed and the earnings (sa'y) are divided equally between the owners.
D) Only the slave whose condition might have been fulfilled is freed, and the other remains enslaved.

Key Takeaway
Certainty that one of two contradictory conditions occurred justifies partial emancipation and split earnings, resolving the ignorance of which condition actually occurred.

Father Owning Son Jointly: Emancipation and Liability
Ruling on father's emancipation without liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a father owning his son jointly with another, and identify the four scenarios in which the father's share is emancipated without liability.

Core Idea
A father's share in his son is automatically emancipated upon ownership, and he owes no compensation to his co-owner.

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling from al-Hidayah concerning a father who owns his son along with an outsider. The matn presents multiple acquisition scenarios—purchase, gift, bequest, and conditional purchase—all leading to the same result: the father's share in the son becomes free, and he is not liable to the co-owner. The commentary expands each scenario and explains the underlying principle of mahram ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal actor and the nature of ownership
Recognize that the passage deals with a father who acquires a share in his son, along with another person, through various means of acquisition (purchase, gift, bequest, conditional purchase).
The acquisition can be from a previous master or through a conditional sale.
2
Recognize the mahram relationship
Understand that the father-son relationship is a mahram (unmarriageable kin) relationship, which automatically triggers emancipation when ownership is established.
The same rule applies to any mahram relative (brother, uncle, etc.).
3
Distinguish the four scenarios
List the four ways the father can acquire a share: (1) buying the son together with another, (2) receiving as gift or bequest, (3) buying half of the son from the master, (4) making a conditional promise and then buying.
Each scenario leads to the same legal consequence.
4
Apply the ruling to each scenario
Confirm that in each scenario, the father's share is emancipated (عتق حصته) and that he is not liable for compensation (لم يضمن) to the partner, regardless of wealth or knowledge.
The commentary explicitly states non-liability is absolute.
5
Generalize the principle
Note that the ruling extends to any mahram relative co-owned with an outsider, and the cause of ownership can be any valid means (purchase, gift, bequest, inheritance, etc.).
This is a core Hanafi principle: ownership of mahram causes immediate emancipation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ملك ابنه مع آخر بشراء، أو هبة، أو وصيَّة، أو اشترى نصف ابنه من سيده ، أو علق بشراء نصفه ثُمَّ اشتراه مع آخر عتق حصته ، ولم يَضمن الأب (ومن ملك ابنه مع آخر بشراء أو هبة، أو وصيّة، أو اشترى نصف ابنه من سيده (۱) ، أو علق (۲) بشراء نصفه ثُمَّ اشتراه مع آخر عتق حصته ، ولم يَضْمَنِ (1) الأب" المقضي عليه، وفي صورة العبد الواحد المقضي به، وهو عتق نصف العبد، والمقضي له ، وهو العبد الواحد معلوم، فلم تتفاحش الجهالة.
[١] قوله : ومن ملك ابنه...الخ ؛ صورته : أن يشتري الرجلان عبداً واحداً من مولاه، والعبد ابن لأحدهما ، أو يهبهما مولاه أو يوصي لهما مولاه عند موته فيملكانه بالوصيّة، وكذا الحكم في ملكِ كلّ ذي رحم محرم مع آخر أجنبي، بسبب من أسباب الملك.
[٢] قوله : أو اشترى نصف ابنه ؛ بأن كان ابنه عبداً لإنسان فاشترى أبوه نصف ابنه من مولاه، وصار هو مع مولاه شريكين في ذلك العبد. (۳) قوله : أو علق ... الخ ; صورته أن يقول : أنت حر إن اشتريت نصفك ، ثمّ يشتريه من مولاه مع آخر، فحينئذ تعتق حصته لوجود الشرط المعلّق عليه. [٤]قوله : عتق حصته ؛ أي حصة الحالف أو المشتري والمالك، أما العتق في صور الحلف فلما أنه لما علق عتقه بشراء النصف ثمّ شراه وجد الشرط وعتق عليه ذلك القدر، وأما في صورة ملك ذي رحم محرم منه مع آخر بسبب من أسباب الملك، فلما مرّ من أن يملك ذي رحم محرم موجب لعتقه عليه ، فإذا تملك النصفُ عتق عليه ذلك القدر [0]قوله : ولم يضمن الأب ؛ أي لا يجب الضمان على الأب إذا أعتق عليه نصفُ ابنه لشريكه موسراً كان الأب أو معسراً، سواء علم الشريك الأجنبي حاله أو لم يعلم. [٦]قوله : الأب ؛ هو فاعل لقوله : «لم يضمن»، إن جعل صيغة مضارع معروف
And whoever owns his son along with another person through purchase, gift, or bequest, or buys half of his son from his master, or attaches (a condition) to the purchase of his half and then buys him together with another, his share is emancipated and the father is not liable. (And whoever owns his son along with another through purchase, gift, or bequest, or buys half of his son from his master, or attaches a condition to the purchase of his half and then buys him together with another, his share is emancipated and the father is not liable.)
[1] His statement: 'And whoever owns his son...' etc. Its form: that two men buy one slave from his owner, and the slave is the son of one of them, or their master gives him to them as a gift or bequeaths him to them at death so they own him through bequest. Similarly, the ruling applies to owning any mahram relative along with another outsider through any cause of ownership.
[2] His statement: 'or buys half of his son' means that his son was a slave belonging to someone, so the father bought half of his son from his master, and he becomes a partner with his master in that slave.
[3] His statement: 'or attaches...' etc. Its form: that he says, 'You are free if I buy half of you,' then he buys him from his master along with another; then his share is emancipated due to the existence of the condition attached.
[4] His statement: 'his share is emancipated' means the share of the swearer or buyer and owner. As for emancipation in the case of swearing, because he attached emancipation to buying half, then he bought, so the condition is found and that portion becomes free upon him. As for the case of owning a mahram relative along with another through any cause of ownership, it is as previously mentioned: that owning a mahram relative necessitates his emancipation upon him, so when he owns half, that portion becomes free upon him.
[5] His statement: 'and the father is not liable' means that the father is not obliged to guarantee (compensate) his partner for the half that is freed on his behalf, whether the father is wealthy or poor, and whether the outside partner knew his situation or not.
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement regarding a father who owns his son jointly with another person. The matn lists several scenarios: (1) Two people buy a slave who is the son of one of them; (2) Two people are given the slave as a gift or bequest; (3) The father buys half of his son from the son's master; (4) The father makes a conditional statement ('You are free if I buy half of you') and then buys the slave along with another. In all these cases, the father's share in the son becomes free (emancipated), and the father is not liable (does not have to pay compensation) to the co-owner for the value of the freed share. The commentary explains that the same ruling applies to any mahram relative (i.e., a relative whom one cannot marry, such as father-son, brother, etc.) when co-owned with an outsider. The reason the father's share frees is that owning a mahram relative triggers immediate emancipation for that share. The father's non-liability is irrespective of his wealth or the partner's knowledge. This ruling is based on the principle that emancipation of a mahram is effective even if it causes loss to the partner, and the father is not required to guarantee the partner's share because the emancipation occurred by the operation of law, not by the father's deliberate act.

Key Terms
ذي رحم محرم (dhi rahim muharram): A relative within the prohibited degrees of marriage (e.g., father, son, brother, uncle). Owning such a relative leads to automatic emancipation.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate a co-owner for the value of the freed share. The father is not liable (la yadman).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a father jointly owns his son with an outsider through purchase, what is the ruling regarding the father's liability?
A) The father must compensate the outsider for the value of the freed half.
B) The father is not liable regardless of his wealth or the outsider's knowledge.
C) The father is liable only if he is wealthy.
D) The father is liable only if the outsider did not know the slave was his son.

Key Takeaway
The father's share in his son frees automatically upon any acquisition of co-ownership, and he bears no liability to the partner.

Automatic Emancipation of a Father's Share Without Liability
Commentary on matn ruling that father's share is freed without liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a father’s share in his jointly-owned slave-son is automatically emancipated without liability, and explain the rationale (talil) that no compensation is owed because there is no intentional corruption (ifsad).

Core Idea
Owning a mahram relative frees him automatically; no compensation is due if the acquisition was involuntary or without choice.

This lesson examines a passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya that expands on a matn from Sharh al-Wiqayah. The commentator explains five scenarios where a father acquires his slave-son jointly with another person. In each case, the father's share is freed and he owes no compensation to his partner. The key principle is that liability only arises from intentional emancipation (ifsad), which is absent here because the emancipation is a direct legal consequence of owning a mahram relative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five acquisition scenarios
The text lists purchase, gift, will, buying half from the master, and conditional purchase (oath). Note that the same ruling applies to ownership of any mahram relative with an unrelated partner.
The scenarios cover both immediate acquisition (purchase, gift, will) and gradual acquisition (buying half, conditional purchase).
2
Establish the principle of automatic emancipation
Ownership of a mahram relative triggers immediate emancipation of that share, regardless of the cause of ownership. This is a well-known Hanafi rule.
3
Trace the rationale (talil) for no liability
Compensation (daman) is only due if the father intentionally caused the partner's share to be freed (ifsad). Here, the father did not choose to free the partner's share; the emancipation was a legal consequence of his own ownership, not a deliberate act of freeing the entire slave.
The passage states: 'the rationale in all of this… is that this compensation is obligatory upon the one who causes emancipation due to the existence of corruption (ifsad) from him, and in these mentioned scenarios, there is no corruption.'
4
Apply the analogy to inheritance
The author likens the disputed purchase/gift/will cases to the agreed-upon inheritance case. In inheritance, the father acquires his share involuntarily, so clearly no ifsad exists. The analogy argues that purchase, gift, and will are also involuntary in the sense that the father is not seeking to free the partner's share.
The inheritance case is agreed upon (ittifaq) by Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Source Text

عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية
ومن ملك ابنه مع آخر بشراء، أو هبة، أو وصيَّة، أو اشترى نصف ابنه من سيده، أو علق بشراء نصفه ثُمَّ اشتراه مع آخر عتق حصته، ولم يَضمن الأب.
قوله : ومن ملك ابنه...الخ ؛ صورته : أن يشتري الرجلان عبداً واحداً من مولاه، والعبد ابن لأحدهما، أو يهبهما مولاه أو يوصي لهما مولاه عند موته فيملكانه بالوصيّة، وكذا الحكم في ملكِ كلّ ذي رحم محرم مع آخر أجنبي، بسبب من أسباب الملك.
قوله : أو اشترى نصف ابنه ؛ بأن كان ابنه عبداً لإنسان فاشترى أبوه نصف ابنه من مولاه، وصار هو مع مولاه شريكين في ذلك العبد.
قوله : أو علق ... الخ ؛ صورته أن يقول : أنت حر إن اشتريت نصفك ، ثمّ يشتريه من مولاه مع آخر، فحينئذ تعتق حصته لوجود الشرط المعلّق عليه.
قوله : عتق حصته ؛ أي حصة الحالف أو المشتري والمالك، أما العتق في صور الحلف فلما أنه لما علق عتقه بشراء النصف ثمّ شراه وجد الشرط وعتق عليه ذلك القدر، وأما في صورة ملك ذي رحم محرم منه مع آخر بسبب من أسباب الملك، فلما مرّ من أن يملك ذي رحم محرم موجب لعتقه عليه ، فإذا تملك النصفُ عتق عليه ذلك القدر.
قوله : ولم يضمن الأب ؛ أي لا يجب الضمان على الأب إذا أعتق عليه نصفُ ابنه لشريكه موسراً كان الأب أو معسراً، سواء علم الشريك الأجنبي حاله أو لم يعلم.
قوله : الأب ؛ هو فاعل لقوله : «لم يضمن»، إن جعل صيغة مضارع معروف من التضمين فهو مفعوله، وفاعله الضمير الراجع إلى الشريك، والظاهر هو الأول ، وكذا الحكم في صورة الحلف بالعتق ، حيث لا يجب الضمان على الحالف.
عَلِمَ الشريك حاله أو لا : أي عَلِمَ الشَّرِيكُ أنه ابن لشريكه ، أو لم يعلم، كما لو ورثاه : أي لا يضمن الأب نصيب الشريك في الصور المذكورة، كما لا يضمن الأب إذا ورثَ هو وشريكه ابنَه ، وصورته : ماتت امرأة، ولها عبد، هو ابن زوجها، فتركت الزوج، والأخ ، فوَرِثَ الأب نصف ابنه ، فعتق عليه، لا يضمن حصة أخيها اتفاقاً ، لأنَّ الإرث ضروري الثبوت، ولا اختيار للأب في ثبوته.
قوله : كما لو ورثاه ؛ يعني إذا ورث الأب وشريك آخـر عـبداً، وهو ابـن أحدهما ، فعـتـق علـى الأب سهمه ؛ لوجودِ تملك ذي رحم محرم لا يجب عليه أداء الضمان إلى شريكه ، علم الشريك حاله أو لم يعلم ، والوجه في ذلك كلّـه علـى مـا سيأتي أن هذا الضمان إنّما يجب عليه المعتق ؛ لوجود الإفساد منه، وفي هذه الصور المذكورة لم يوجد الإفساد، فلا يجب عليه الضمان.
قوله : في الصور المذكورة ؛ أي ما إذا ملك الأب ابنه مع آخر بشراء أو هبة أو وصيّة أو نحوها، أو اشترى نصفه من سيّده ، أو كان علّق عتقه بشراء نصفه فاشتراه مع آخر.
قوله : وصورته...الخ ؛ يعني إذا ماتت امرأة وتركت من الورثة زوجها وأخاها، ولها عبد هو ابن زوجها من الزوجة الأخرى ، فحينئذ يرثُ زوجها نصف ابنه، ويرث نصفه الباقي أخ المتوفاة ، لما تقرّر أنّ للزوج عند عدم أولادها النصف، وإن الأخ من العصبات يحرز ما أبقته أصحاب الفرائض.
قوله : اتفاقاً ؛ أي بين أبي حنيفة وبين صاحبيه ، وبه يعلم توجيه التشبيه الواقع في قول المصنف كما لو ورثاه ، وهو أنه لما كانت هذه المسألة اتفاقية شبه بقية المسائل الاختلافية بهذه المسألة.
قوله : لأنّ الإرث ضروري ؛ فإنّ أسباب الملكِ على قسمين :
And whoever owns his son with another by purchase, or gift, or will, or buys half of his son from his master, or he stipulates [a condition] of buying half of him then buys him with another — his share is freed, and the father is not liable.
His statement: 'And whoever owns his son... etc.': Its form: that two men buy one slave from his master, and the slave is the son of one of them, or their master gifts him to them, or their master bequeaths him to them at his death so they own him by will. And similarly the ruling for the ownership of every close relative (dhi rahm mahram) with another unrelated person, by any cause of ownership.
His statement: 'Or buys half of his son': that his son was a slave to a person, so the father buys half of his son from his master, and he becomes a partner with his master in that slave.
His statement: 'Or stipulates... etc.': Its form: that he says: 'You are free if I buy half of you,' then he buys him from his master with another, then his share is freed due to the existence of the condition on which it was suspended.
His statement: 'His share is freed': i.e., the share of the one who made the oath or the buyer and owner. As for emancipation in the case of the oath, because when he suspended his emancipation on buying half, then he bought it, the condition occurred and that portion is freed for him. And as for the case of ownership of a close relative (dhi rahm mahram) with another by a cause of ownership, because of what has passed: that owning a close relative necessitates emancipation for him. So if he owns half, that portion is freed for him.
His statement: 'And the father is not liable': i.e., liability is not obligatory upon the father if half of his son is freed for his partner, whether the father is wealthy or poor, whether the unrelated partner knew his situation or not.
His statement: 'The father': It is the subject of his statement 'is not liable'; if he makes it a transitive verb form of liability, then it is the object, and its subject is the pronoun referring to the partner. The apparent view is the first. And similarly the ruling in the case of the oath of emancipation, where liability is not obligatory upon the one who made the oath.
'Whether the partner knew his situation or not': i.e., whether the partner knew that he is the son of his partner or did not know, as if they inherited him. i.e., the father is not liable for the partner's share in the mentioned scenarios, just as the father is not liable when he and his partner inherit his son. Its form: a woman died, and she had a slave who is the son of her husband, so she left the husband and the brother; so the father inherits half of his son, and it is freed for him; he is not liable for the share of her brother by agreement, because inheritance is necessary in its establishment, and the father has no choice in its establishment.
His statement: 'As if they inherited him': meaning if the father and another partner inherit a slave who is the son of one of them, then the father's share is freed for him due to ownership of a close relative; he is not obligated to pay compensation to his partner, whether the partner knew his situation or not. The rationale in all of this, as will come, is that this compensation is obligatory upon the one who causes emancipation due to the existence of corruption (ifsad) from him, and in these mentioned scenarios, there is no corruption, so compensation is not obligatory upon him.
His statement: 'In the mentioned scenarios': i.e., when the father owns his son with another by purchase, gift, will, or similar, or buys half of him from his master, or had suspended his emancipation on buying half and then bought him with another.
His statement: 'Its form... etc.': meaning if a woman died and left among her heirs her husband and her brother, and she has a slave who is the son of her husband from another wife, then her husband inherits half of his son, and the remaining half is inherited by the brother of the deceased, because it is established that the husband gets half when there are no children, and the brother from the 'asaba (agnates) takes what is left by the holders of prescribed shares.
His statement: 'By agreement': i.e., between Abu Hanifa and his two companions. And by this is known the justification of the similitude in the author's statement 'as if they inherited him': that since this issue is agreed upon, he likened the other disputed issues to this issue.
His statement: 'Because inheritance is necessary': for the causes of ownership are of two kinds:
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expansion of the matn from Sharh al-Wiqayah, explaining the ruling that when a father owns his slave-son jointly with another person through purchase, gift, will, conditional purchase, or inheritance, the father's share is automatically emancipated and he is not liable to compensate the co-owner for his share. The commentator begins by defining the scenarios: the father and another person jointly acquire a slave who is the father's son, whether by purchase, gift, or will. The same ruling applies to ownership of any close relative (mahram) with an unrelated person. The next scenario is when the father buys half of his son from the current master, becoming partners. The third scenario involves a conditional emancipation: the father says 'You are free if I buy half of you,' then buys the slave with another; upon purchase, his share is freed because the condition is met. The commentator then explains that 'his share is freed' refers to the share of the oath-maker or buyer. The emancipation occurs either because the condition is fulfilled or because owning a mahram relative automatically frees that portion. The key ruling is that the father is not liable for the partner's share, regardless of his wealth or the partner's knowledge. The grammatical analysis confirms 'al-ab' (the father) is the subject of 'lam yadman' (is not liable). The commentator then draws an analogy to inheritance: just as a father who inherits his slave-son jointly with another is not liable—because inheritance is involuntary and there is no corruption (ifsad) from the father—so too in these scenarios, the father does not cause corruption (he did not intentionally free the partner's share), hence no liability. The commentator specifies that the inheritance case is agreed upon by Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and the author likened the disputed purchase cases to this agreed case to argue for non-liability. Finally, the commentator begins a discussion on the causes of ownership, dividing them into two categories, likely to further justify the ruling.

Key Terms
ملک (milk): Ownership; the legal acquisition and possession of property, here specifically of a slave.
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave; the act of freeing a slave, which may be automatic upon ownership of a close relative.
ضمان (daman): Liability or compensation; the obligation to pay the value of a share to a co-owner when a slave is freed.
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahm mahram): A close relative within the prohibited degrees of marriage; owning such a person as a slave automatically results in their emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following scenarios is a father NOT liable for the share of his slave-son's partner when his share is freed, according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) The father buys his son jointly with another person.
B) The father is gifted his son jointly with another person.
C) The father inherits his son jointly with another person.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
A father who owns his slave-son jointly with another is automatically freed from his share and owes no compensation to the partner, because the emancipation is involuntary and there is no element of corruption (ifsad).

Liability in Partial Emancipation of a Son-Slave
Explains ruling on compensation when father jointly owns his son-slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on compensation when a father jointly owns his son as a slave, and distinguish between the inheritance and purchase scenarios.

Core Idea
Inheritance: no compensation. Voluntary purchase: Abu Hanifa says no liability; Sahibayn say liability if wealthy.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah unpacks the nuance of partial emancipation when a father co-owns his son. The ruling hinges on how the father acquired his share—whether through compulsory inheritance or a voluntary transaction like purchase. Understanding this distinction is critical for correctly applying Hanafi law in historical slavery cases and for grasping Hanafi methodology regarding consent and liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of acquisition
Distinguish between inheritance (compulsory) and voluntary acquisition (purchase, gift, etc.). The father's consent varies between them.
2
Trace the agreed ruling for inheritance
Both Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree: no compensation (daman) is owed by the father because inheritance is compulsory and there is no 'ifsad' (corruption) of the partner's interest.
The text emphasizes that inheritance is necessary (daruri) and cannot be dropped.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute for purchase
In voluntary acquisition, Abu Hanifa holds no liability; the Sahibayn hold liability if the father is wealthy (half value) or else the slave works (sa'ayah).
The father's participation in the purchase is the key factor.
4
Analyze the underlying reasoning (illa)
Purchase is the cause of ownership; ownership of a close relative is the cause of emancipation. Abu Hanifa: consent is implied, so no liability. Sahibayn: purchase itself triggers emancipation, so liability follows unless the father is poor.
This is an application of 'illa al-'illa' (cause of the cause).
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: according to Sahibayn, a wealthy father must pay his partner half the slave's value; according to Abu Hanifa, he never pays.
The text does not give a tarjih, but the later school often prefers Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتاق باب عتق البعض
عَلِمَ الشريك حاله أو لا كما لو ورثاه
أي عَلِمَ الشَّرِيكُ أنه ابن لشريكه ، أو لم يعلم، كما لو ورثاه ) : أي لا يضمن الأب نصيب الشريك في الصور المذكورة، كما لا يضمن الأب إذا ورثَ هو وشريكه ابنَه ، وصورتُهُ": ماتت امرأة، ولها عبد، هو ابن زوجها، فتركت الزوج، والأخ ، فوَرِثَ الأب نصف ابنه ، فعتق عليه، لا يضمن حصة أخيها اتفاقاً ، لأنَّ الإرث ضروري الثبوت، ولا اختيار للأب في ثبوته
[١]قوله : كما لو ورثاه ؛ يعني إذا ورث الأب وشريك آخـر عـبداً، وهو ابـن أحدهما ، فعـتـق علـى الأب سهمه ؛ لوجودِ تملك ذي رحم محرم لا يجب عليه أداء الضمان إلى شريكه ، علم الشريك حاله أو لم يعلم ، والوجه في ذلك كلّـه علـى مـا سيأتي أن هذا الضمان إنّما يجب عليه المعتق ؛ لوجود الإفساد منه، وفي هذه الصور المذكورة لم يوجد الإفساد، فلا يجب عليه الضمان.
[٢]قوله : في الصور المذكورة ؛ أي ما إذا ملك الأب ابنه مع آخر بشراء أو هبة أو وصيّة أو نحوها، أو اشترى نصفه من سيّده ، أو كان علّق عتقه بشراء نصفه فاشتراه مع آخر.
[٣] قوله : وصورته...الخ ؛ يعني إذا ماتت امرأة وتركت من الورثة زوجها وأخاها، ولها عبد هو ابن زوجها من الزوجة الأخرى ، فحينئذ يرثُ زوجها نصف ابنه، ويرث نصفه الباقي أخ المتوفاة ، لما تقرّر أنّ للزوج عند عدم أولادها النصف، وإن الأخ من العصبات يحرز ما أبقته أصحاب الفرائض.
[٤] قوله : اتفاقاً ؛ أي بين أبي حنيفة وبين صاحبيه ، وبه يعلم توجيه التشبيه الواقع في قول المصنف الله كما لو ورثاه ، وهو أن لما كانت هذه المسألة اتفاقية شبه بقية المسائل الاختلافية بهذه المسألة.
[0]قوله : لأنّ الإرث ضروري ؛ فإنّ أسباب الملكِ على قسمين :
١ . اختيارية كالبيع والشراء وغيرهما.
٢ . وضرورية كالإرث. ولهذا صرحوا بأن الإرث لا يسقط بالإسقاط ، بل هو جبري، حتى لو قال وارث بعد موت مورثه: لا آخذ حصتي من التركة، ثم ادعاها صحت دعواه، صرح به في تنقيح الفتاوي الحامدية وغيره، ولما كان الإرث مما لا اختيار فيه للأب، فعتق سهمه عليه لا يكون إفساداً منه على شريكه ، حتى يجب ضمانه عليه.
وأعتقه الآخر ، أو سعى له ، وقالا : في غير الإرث ضَمِنَ نصف قيمته غنياً، وسعى له فقيراً
( وأعتقه الآخر، أو سعى له ) : أي لما لم يكن للشريك ولاية التضمين بقي له أحد الأمرين : إما الإعتاق، أو السعاية. (وقالا : في غيرِ الإرثِ ضَمِنَ نصف قيمتِهِ غنياً ، وسعى له فقيراً) ؛ لأنَّ شراءَ القريب إعتاق" فإن كان موسراً يَجِبُ الضمان ، وإن كان معسراً سعى العبد. وأبو حنيفة له يقول : إنَّه رضي بإفسادِ نصيبه فلا يَضمَنْهُ، كما إذا أذن بإعتاق نصيبه حيث شاركَهُ في علة العتق ، وهو الشراء
[١] قوله : سعى له ؛ أي سعى العبد لذلك الشريك بقدر حصته. (٢) قوله : بقي له أحد الأمرين ؛ سواء كان المعتق موسراً أو معسراً.
[٣]قوله : إعتاق ؛ وكذلك الحلف بالعتق إعتاق عند وجود الشرط فيجري فيه حكم إعتاق أحد الشريكين نصيبه، وهو التضمين حال كون المعتق غنياً، واستسعاء العبد حال كونه فقيراً.
[٤] قوله : فلا يضمنه ؛ الضمير البارز المتصل إلى الأب ، إن جعل ضمير يضمن، وهو من التضمين إلى الشريك ، وإلى الشريك إن كان ضميره يضمن، وهو من الضمان إلى الأب.
(0) قوله : أذن ؛ أي أذن أحد الشريكين للآخر بإعتاق نصيبه صريحاً، بأن قال له : أعتق نصيبك فأعتقه فيه، لا يجب الضمان ؛ لعدم وجود الإفساد. [٦] قوله : حيث شاركه في علّة العتق ؛ رضى بالعتق لا محالة، والمراد بالعلة علة العلة ؛ لأنّ الشراء علّة التملك، والتملك في القريب علّة العتق، والحكم يضاف إلى علة العلة إذا لم تصلح العلة للإضافة إليها ، وهاهنا كذلك ؛ لأن التملك حكم شرعي
Book of Emancipation, Chapter on Partial Emancipation. The partner knows his condition or not, like when both inherit him. Explanation: i.e., the partner knows that he is the son of his partner, or does not know, like when both inherit him: meaning the father does not guarantee the partner's share in the mentioned cases, just as the father does not guarantee when he and his partner inherit his son. Its form: A woman dies, and she has a slave who is the son of her husband, so she leaves the husband and the brother. The father inherits half of his son, and he is freed upon him; he does not guarantee the sister's share [of the brother] by agreement, because inheritance is compulsory in establishment, and the father has no choice in its establishment. [1] His statement: 'like when both inherit him': meaning when the father and another partner inherit a slave who is the son of one of them, then the father's share is freed upon him due to ownership of a close relative (mahram). He must not pay compensation (daman) to his partner, whether the partner knows his condition or not. The reasoning in all of this, as will come, is that this compensation is only obligatory on the manumitter due to the existence of corruption (ifsad) from him. In these mentioned cases, no corruption existed, so compensation is not obligatory upon him. [2] His statement: 'in the mentioned cases': i.e., when the father owns his son jointly with another by purchase, gift, bequest, or the like, or buys half of him from his master, or had suspended his emancipation on buying half of him and then bought him with another. [3] His statement: 'its form...': meaning when a woman dies and leaves among the heirs her husband and her brother, and she has a slave who is the son of her husband from his other wife. Then her husband inherits half of his son, and the deceased woman's brother inherits the remaining half, as it is established that the husband gets half when there are no children of hers, and the brother as an 'asabah (residuary) takes what remains of the prescribed shares. [4] His statement: 'by agreement': i.e., between Abu Hanifa and his two companions. By this the direction of the comparison in the author's statement 'like when both inherit him' is known, i.e., since this case is agreed upon, he compared the other disputed cases to this case. [0] His statement: 'because inheritance is compulsory': for the causes of ownership are of two types: 1. Voluntary, like sale, purchase, etc. 2. Compulsory, like inheritance. For this reason they stated that inheritance cannot be dropped by dropping (renunciation); it is compulsory. Even if an heir after the death of his testator says: I will not take my share from the estate, then claims it, his claim is valid, as stated in Tanqih al-Fatawa al-Hamidiyya and others. And since inheritance is something over which the father has no choice, the freeing of his share upon him is not corruption against his partner, such that compensation be obligatory upon him. And the other frees him, or he seeks earning, and they said: In non-inheritance, he guarantees half his value if wealthy, and the slave seeks earning for him if poor. (And the other frees him, or he seeks earning): meaning since the partner has no authority to seek compensation, one of two things remains for him: either emancipation or seeking earning (sa'ayah). (And they said: In non-inheritance, he guarantees half his value if wealthy, and the slave seeks earning for him if poor): because the purchase of a close relative is emancipation. If he is wealthy, compensation is obligatory; if he is poor, the slave seeks earning. And Abu Hanifa says: He agreed to the corruption of his share, so he does not guarantee it, just as if he permitted the freeing of his share where he participated in the cause of emancipation, which is purchase. [1] His statement: 'he seeks earning': meaning the slave seeks earning for that partner to the extent of his share. (2) His statement: 'one of two things remains for him': whether the manumitter is wealthy or poor. [3] His statement: 'emancipation': likewise, swearing by emancipation is emancipation when the condition exists; thus the ruling of one partner freeing his share applies, which is compensation when the manumitter is wealthy, and the slave's earning when he is poor. [4] His statement: 'so he does not guarantee it': the overt attached pronoun refers to the father if the pronoun of 'yadman' is from tadmeen (compensation) and the object is the partner; and to the partner if its pronoun is from daman (guarantee) and the object is the father. (0) His statement: 'he permitted': i.e., one of the two partners explicitly permitted the other to free his share, by saying to him: Free your share, so he freed it; compensation is not obligatory due to the absence of corruption. [6] His statement: 'he participated in the cause of emancipation': he agreed to emancipation necessarily. By 'cause' is meant the cause of the cause; for purchase is the cause of ownership, and ownership of a close relative is the cause of emancipation. The ruling is attributed to the cause of the cause when the cause is not suitable for attribution to it, and here it is so, because ownership is a legal ruling.
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah (Kitab al-Itaq, Bab Itq al-Ba'd) explains the rulings when a father and another person jointly own a slave who is the father's son. The key distinction is whether the father's share of the slave was acquired through inheritance (compulsory) or through a voluntary transaction (purchase, gift, etc.). The passage begins with the matn: 'The partner knows his condition or not, like when both inherit him.' The commentator explains that if the father inherits his son jointly with another heir (e.g., the brother of the deceased wife), the father's share becomes free upon him, and he owes no compensation (daman) to his partner. This is agreed upon by Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Sahibayn) because inheritance is compulsory (daruri), and the father had no choice in acquiring ownership. The 'ifsad' (corruption) that triggers liability is absent. The commentator then illustrates with an example: a woman dies leaving her husband and brother; the husband inherits half of his son (a slave), and the brother inherits the other half. The father's half becomes free, and he does not pay the brother anything. The reason, elaborated in the footnote, is that the causes of ownership are of two types: voluntary (like purchase) and compulsory (like inheritance). Compulsory ownership does not involve the owner's choice, so no liability arises. Then the matn continues: 'And the other frees him, or he seeks earning, and they said: In non-inheritance, he guarantees half his value if wealthy, and the slave seeks earning for him if poor.' This addresses the scenario where the father acquires his share through purchase (a voluntary act). Here, the Sahibayn hold that if the father is wealthy, he must pay his partner half the slave's value; if poor, the slave works to earn the partner's share. Abu Hanifa, however, holds that the father, by voluntarily entering into the purchase, has agreed to the emancipation of his share and therefore owes nothing. His reasoning is analogous to explicit permission: if a partner authorizes the other to free his share, no compensation is due. The commentator clarifies that in the purchase case, the father participates in the 'cause of emancipation' (shira' al-qarib itaq), so his consent is implied. The practical difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn is whether the father must pay when he voluntarily acquires his son's share. The passage also notes that if the partner chooses to free his share rather than seek earning, he may do so; but if he seeks earning, the slave must work for him. The discussion of the cause of emancipation (illa) is refined: purchase is the 'cause of the cause' (cause of ownership), and ownership of a close relative is the cause of emancipation. The ruling attaches to the cause of the cause when the immediate cause is not suitable for direct attribution.

Key Terms
الضمان (al-daman): Compensation or liability owed by a co-owner to his partner when the co-owner's share of a slave is freed by operation of law (e.g., by inheritance or purchase by a parent).
السعاية (al-sa'ayah): The act of a slave working to earn money to compensate the partner for his freed share, used when the father/owner is poor.
علة العتق (illat al-'itq): The effective cause of emancipation; in this context, purchase of a close relative (like a son) is considered a cause of emancipation (shira' al-qarib itaq).
علة العلة (illat al-'illa): The cause of the cause; here purchase is the cause of ownership, and ownership of a close relative is the cause of emancipation, so the ruling attaches to the higher cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a father and another heir inherit a slave who is the father's son, what is the ruling according to all Hanafi imams?
A) The father must pay half the value to the other heir.
B) The father owes no compensation to the other heir.
C) The slave must work to compensate the other heir.
D) The father may choose to free the slave or pay compensation.

Key Takeaway
A father who acquires his son-slave through inheritance owes no compensation; in voluntary purchase, Abu Hanifa holds no liability while Sahibayn require payment if the father is wealthy.

Guarantee for Manumission of a Relative's Share: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on guarantee for manumission of a relative's share

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on guarantee (daman) when a partner manumits his share of a jointly owned slave who is his relative, and evaluate the causal reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
Participation in the cause is deemed consent: Abu Hanifa's key principle vs. Sahibayn's compensation rule.

When two people jointly own a slave who turns out to be a relative of one partner, and that partner manumits his share, does the silent partner deserve compensation? Abu Hanifa says no, because the silent partner participated in the purchase (the cause of manumission). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say yes, the manumitter must pay half the value if rich, or the slave works if poor. This lesson dissects their proofs and the practical outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their positions
Abu Hanifa holds no guarantee; Sahibayn hold guarantee (daman) if wealthy, sa'aya (slave works) if poor. Both agree that inheritance cases are different (guarantee always due).
2
Trace the proofs: Sahibayn's dalil
They argue that buying a relative is manumission (since ownership of a relative triggers immediate freedom). Therefore, the manumitter has spoiled the partner's share and must compensate.
This is based on the rule that owning a relative frees him automatically.
3
Trace the proofs: Abu Hanifa's dalil
He argues that the silent partner, by participating in the purchase (the cause of the cause), is deemed to have consented to the manumission. Hence, no compensation is due.
Principle: 'The ruling is attached to the cause of the cause when the immediate cause is not suitable.'
4
Examine the exception: inheritance
In inheritance, the silent partner did not participate in the cause (inheritance is involuntary), so Abu Hanifa joins the Sahibayn in requiring guarantee. This shows the decisive role of 'participation in the cause'.
Inheritance is unrelated to purchase; no 'cause of manumission' shared.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative position)
In non-inheritance cases, the fatwa is according to the Sahibayn: guarantee or sa'aya is due. Abu Hanifa's view is considered weak for practice, though recorded in the school.
Tarjih by later authorities like Al-Marghinani.

The gist of the rebuttal: Ignorance is not an excuse in legal matters, and participation in the cause of manumission suffices to deem him content with spoiling his share.
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the guarantee (daman) when one of two partners manumits his share of a jointly owned slave, specifically when the manumitter bought his share (the slave is his relative) and the other partner did nothing. The two imams (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that if the acquisition is not through inheritance (e.g., purchase), the manumitter must guarantee half the value to the other partner if he is wealthy; if poor, the slave must work (sa'aya) to compensate. Their basis: buying a relative constitutes manumission (since owning a relative triggers immediate freedom). Abu Hanifa disagrees: he argues that the silent partner, by participating in the cause of manumission (namely, the purchase which led to ownership and then manumission), is deemed to have consented to the spoiling of his share, so no guarantee is owed—just as if he had explicitly permitted manumission. The commentary then distinguishes voluntary acquisitions (like purchase) from involuntary ones (like inheritance). Inheritance is compulsory and not subject to consent; therefore, if the share came through inheritance, the silent partner did not consent, and guarantee is obligatory. The passage also clarifies that ignorance is no excuse: even if the partner was unaware that his purchase would lead to manumission, he is still considered to have consented by participating in the causal chain. This is a core issue in Hanafi fiqh regarding partial manumission and the concept of 'consent by participation in the cause.'

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Financial guarantee/compensation paid by a partner who manumits his share to the other partner for the loss of his share.
سعاية (sa'aya): The slave's earning/working for the partner to compensate for the partner's share when the manumitter is poor.
عتق البعض (itq al-ba'd): Manumission of a share in a jointly owned slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Hanafi school's mufta bihi is that a partner manumitting his share of a relative-slave must compensate the other partner (daman if rich, sa'aya if poor), except in inheritance where Abū Ḥanīfa agrees guarantee is due.

Guarantee When a Stranger Buys Half Then the Father Buys the Rest
Intra-Hanafi dispute on guarantee and sa'aya when the father buys after a stranger.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on compensation when a stranger's purchase precedes a relative's purchase leading to manumission, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
When a stranger buys first, the father's subsequent purchase spoils the stranger's share — compensation is due.

In this lesson, we examine a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: a stranger buys half of a slave, then the slave's father buys the remaining half, thereby manumitting the slave. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the father must compensate the stranger for spoiling his share, either by payment (if wealthy) or by the slave's work (if poor). Abu Hanifa's view is impliedly opposed, likely holding no compensation. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the principle that consent matters in liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the legal issue
The scenario: a stranger buys half of a slave; then the slave's father buys the other half, causing the slave to become free. The issue: must the father compensate the stranger? The answer depends on whether the stranger consented to the manumission.
The father's purchase is a cause of manumission because the slave is his relative.
2
State the Sahibayn's position and reasoning
The Sahibayn say the father must either pay the value of the stranger's share (guarantee) or the slave works for the stranger (sa'aya), and the stranger has the choice. Reasoning: the stranger did not consent to the spoilage of his share, as his purchase preceded the father's.
If the father is wealthy, the stranger can demand guarantee; if poor, only sa'aya is due.
3
Identify Abu Hanifa's implied position
The text records a disagreement ('they disagreed in it'). Abu Hanifa likely held no compensation, perhaps by analogy to the case where both buy together — there, the stranger participates in the cause of manumission and consents. Here, the stranger's purchase is separate, so the analogy fails.
Abu Hanifa's view is not explicitly stated but inferred from the dispute.
4
Analyze the basis of the disagreement
The key difference is consent. In the joint-purchase case, both buyers intend manumission together. Here, the stranger bought first without intending the father's subsequent manumission; thus, the father's act spoils the stranger's share without consent.
This illustrates the principle that spoiling another's property without consent incurs liability.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The Sahibayn's view is considered stronger because the stranger's lack of consent is clear. Thus, in practice, the father must compensate the stranger.
The text implies the Sahibayn's view is correct by detailing the reasoning for the partner's choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

In this scenario, the partner did not consent to spoiling his share, so he has the choice, and in their view, no work is required for him because the manumitter is wealthy. Commentary
This passage presents a second scenario: a stranger buys half of a slave, then the slave's father (who is the slave's relative, so his purchase triggers manumission) buys the remaining half. Here, the father must guarantee the stranger's share (if wealthy) or the slave works for the stranger (if the father is poor). The Sahibayn hold that since the stranger's purchase preceded the father's, the stranger did not consent to the spoilage of his share, so he has a choice. Abu Hanifa's view is impliedly opposed (hence 'they disagreed in it'), likely that the father owes nothing because the stranger participated in the cause? The commentary explains the reasoning: the stranger's prior purchase means he did not intend to participate in the father's manumission cause, unlike the earlier case where both bought together. Thus, the father is liable for spoiling the stranger's share.

Key Terms
تخيير (takhyeer): The partner's choice to demand either guarantee (if father wealthy) or sa'aya (if father poor).
ضمان (daman): Guarantee; the father's obligation to pay the value of the stranger's share.
سعاية (sa'aya): The slave's work to earn the value of the stranger's share; required when the father is poor.

Key Takeaway
When a stranger's purchase precedes a relative's purchase, the relative must compensate the stranger because the stranger did not consent to the manumission.

Ruling on Tadbir and Manumission by Different Partners
Statement of a ruling when one partner makes a slave mudabbar and the other manumits him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the guarantee chain when one partner makes a slave mudabbar and another manumits him, and identify the condition of wealth.

Core Idea
When one partner makes a mudabbar and another manumits, the mudabbar guarantees the silent partner, and the manumitter guarantees the mudabbar.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi fiqh where three partners share a slave. One partner makes his share mudabbar, another manumits his share immediately, and the third does nothing. The ruling establishes a cascade of guarantees: the mudabbar must compensate the silent partner, and the manumitter must compensate the mudabbar. Understanding this ruling is important because it illustrates how Hanafi law protects property rights when co-owners' actions conflict.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their actions
List the three partners: one makes tadbir (conditional manumission upon death), one manumits immediately, and one remains silent. Note that each owns a share of the slave.
The shares are equal (each one-third).
2
Determine the spoil caused by each action
The tadbir spoils the silent partner's share's value, and the manumission spoils the mudabbar's share.
Spoiling here means the property is lost or diminished.
3
Apply the condition of wealth
The ruling only applies if both the mudabbar and the manumitter are wealthy (musaran). If they are poor, the ruling may differ.
Wealth is a condition for the guarantee to be enforceable.
4
Understand the guarantee chain
The mudabbar guarantees the silent partner's share, and the manumitter guarantees the mudabbar's share. The silent partner does not guarantee anyone.
This cascade ensures compensation flows from the one whose action caused the spoil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو دبره أحد الشركاء، وأعتقه الآخر، وهما موسران ضَمَّنَ الساكت
[٤] قوله : ولو دبره ... الخ ؛ حاصل هذه الصورة أنه إذا كان العبد بين ثلاثة نفر قدير أحدهم سهمه، وهو ثلث العبد، وهو موسر، ثم أعتق الآخر سهمه، وبقي الثالث لم يدبر ولم يعتق، فإن أراد الساكت والمدبّر الضمان يضمن المدبر للساكت، ويضمن المعتق المدبر.
[0] قوله : ضمن ; ماض معروف من التضمين فاعله : الساكت، ومفعوله :
And if one of the partners makes him a mudabbar (i.e., declares him free upon the owner's death), and the other manumits him, and both are wealthy, then the silent (partner) is made to guarantee. [4] His saying: And if he makes him a mudabbar... etc. The summary of this scenario: If the slave is between three persons: one of them makes his share mudabbar, which is one-third of the slave, and he is wealthy, then the other manumits his share, and the third remains, having neither made mudabbar nor manumitted, then if the silent partner and the mudabbar wish guarantee, the mudabbar guarantees to the silent partner, and the manumitter guarantees to the mudabbar. [0] His saying: He is made to guarantee; it is a past tense known from 'tadmin', its subject is the silent partner, and its object is...
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling involving three partners sharing a slave. One partner makes his share mudabbar (the slave will be free upon that partner's death), another manumits his share immediately, and the third does nothing. All three are wealthy. The ruling: the silent partner (the one who did nothing) is made liable for guarantee. The mudabbar must guarantee the silent partner's share (because his tadbir spoiled the silent partner's share), and the manumitter must guarantee the mudabbar's share (because his immediate manumission spoiled the mudabbar's share). This is a cascade of guarantees to ensure each partner whose share was spoiled is compensated. The passage is a straightforward statement of the Hanafi position on this multi-party scenario.

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbir): A declaration by an owner that a slave will be free upon the owner's death, making the slave a mudabbar.
ساكت (sakit): The silent partner who neither made tadbir nor manumitted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario with three partners (one makes mudabbar, one manumits, one does nothing), who is the silent partner guaranteed by?
A) The manumitter guarantees the silent partner.
B) The mudabbar guarantees the silent partner.
C) The silent partner guarantees himself.
D) No guarantee is owed.

Key Takeaway
In a multi-partner ownership of a slave, if one partner makes tadbir and another manumits, the mudabbar must guarantee the silent partner, and the manumitter must guarantee the mudabbar, provided both are wealthy.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Guarantee in Partial Emancipation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on guarantee in partial emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi positions on guarantee (daman) and earning (saʿyah) when a co-owner impairs another’s share through emancipation or tadbir, and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Tadbir is divisible; a silent co-owner claims guarantee only from the mudabbar, not the emancipator.

This lesson examines two scenarios of partial emancipation where a co-owner’s actions impair another’s share. The core dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn centers on whether tadbir (deferred emancipation) is divisible and how this affects the rights of other co-owners. Understanding this will help you apply the correct compensation rules in co-ownership emancipation cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two factual scenarios
First scenario: a stranger buys half a slave, then the father buys the remaining half. Second scenario: three co-owners, one makes the slave mudabbar, one emancipates, one remains silent.
The passage also addresses ignorance as a non-excuse.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling for each scenario
First scenario: the father must either guarantee or the slave earns (saʿyah); the partner has a choice. Second scenario: the mudabbar guarantees the silent partner, and the emancipator guarantees the mudabbar; the silent partner may only claim from the mudabbar.
Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that tadbir is divisible (mutajazzi).
3
State the Sahibayn's objection
In the first scenario, they hold that no earning is required if the emancipator is wealthy; only guarantee is due. In the second scenario, they consider tadbir indivisible, leading to a different distribution of liabilities (not detailed in the text).
Their position is based on tadbir not being divisible.
4
Understand the concept of tadbir divisibility
Abu Hanifa argues that tadbir is a branch of emancipation, which is divisible, so it applies only to the mudabbar's share but impairs others’ shares (prevents sale/gift). The Sahibayn see tadbir as indivisible, treating it like a single act covering the whole slave.
This difference affects which guarantees are available.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical impact
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's. Practically, under his view, a silent co-owner retains options (e.g., can emancipate his own share or seek guarantee from the mudabbar). Under the Sahibayn, the rights would be more restricted.
Ignorance of the law does not change this classification.
