Book of Emancipation, Chapter on Partial Emancipation. And if he bought half, then the father [bought] the remainder while wealthy, he guarantees for him, or earnings, and they differed regarding it. And if one of the partners made him a mudabbar, and the other emancipated him, and both are wealthy, the silent one is made liable. And if ignorant, ignorance is not an excuse. (And if he bought half, then the father the remainder while wealthy, he guarantees for him, or earns, and they differed regarding it). In this situation the partner did not consent to the impairment of his share, so he has a choice. According to them both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), earning is not obligatory because the emancipator is wealthy. (And if one of the partners made him a mudabbar, and the other emancipated him, and both are wealthy, the silent one is liable). It is established after directly performing its cause without choice, unlike inheritance, for there is no emancipation there and therefore one does not exit from kaffarah by it. Thus in al-'Inayah and al-Binayah. [1] His saying: 'And if ignorant...' is a rebuttal to what is said: that making him consenting to the impairment of his share, if it is valid, is only valid when the partner knows; as for if he is ignorant, it is not valid to make him consenting. The summary of the rebuttal: ignorance is not an excuse in legal matters, and participation in the cause of emancipation is sufficient to make him consenting to the impairment of his share. [2] His saying: 'And if he bought half...' means if a stranger started with purchase, so he bought half of the slave from his master, then the father of the slave bought the remaining half, so the slave became free by ownership, then the father must guarantee to his partner, or earning is obligatory, and the partner has a choice if the father is wealthy, and if he is poor, the right is only in earning, because the partner did not consent in this situation to the impairment of his share, for his purchase was prior to the father's purchase. [3] His saying: 'Because the emancipator is wealthy' – according to them both there is no earning while the emancipator is wealthy. [4] His saying: 'And if he made him a mudabbar...' The summary of this situation: if the slave is among three persons, one of them made his share a mudabbar, which is one-third of the slave, and he is wealthy, then another emancipated his share, and the third remained neither having made him mudabbar nor emancipated, and if the silent one and the mudabbar want guarantee, the mudabbar guarantees to the silent one, and the emancipator guarantees to the mudabbar. [0] His saying: 'guaranteed' is a past tense verb from taḍmīn, its subject: the silent one, and its object: his mudabbar, not his emancipator, and the mudabbar [guarantees] his emancipator for his one-third as mudabbar, not for what he guaranteed for him. This is according to Abu Hanifa, because tadbir is divisible according to him like emancipation, so it is confined to his share, but it impaired the share of his two partners, so one of them chose emancipation of his share, so his right became fixed in it, and he no longer had a choice of another matter like guarantee and others. Then for the silent one two causes of guarantee apply: guarantee of tadbir and guarantee of emancipation. But [1] his mudabbar is on the pattern of an active participle from tadbir. His saying: his mudabbar; this is if the mudabbar is wealthy, and if he is insolvent, the silent one may seek earnings not guarantee, and similarly if the emancipator is insolvent, the mudabbar may seek earnings not guarantee from the emancipator. Thus in al-Bahr. [2] His saying: 'and the mudabbar' – with kasra on the stressed ba – meaning the one who made him a mudabbar guarantees the share of the emancipator. [3] His saying: 'he chose emancipation of his share...' Explanation: tadbir according to Abu Hanifa is divisible, not according to them both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad); because it is a branch of emancipation, so its ruling is its ruling. Therefore according to him (Abu Hanifa) tadbir is confined to the share of the mudabbar, but it impairs the share of the others, since its sale and gift are prevented. So each of the other two partners may make his share a mudabbar, or emancipate, or make a kitaba, or take guarantee of his share from the mudabbar, or seek earnings from the slave, or leave him as is. That is because each of them remains in his ownership, though impaired, by the impairment of his partner; i.e., the one who made his share a mudabbar, as he blocked both ways of benefit. So if one of them chooses emancipation of his share, his right becomes fixed in it, and his other choices fall. As for the silent one – who neither made mudabbar nor emancipated – two causes of guarantee apply to him: guarantee of the tadbir of the first partner for his share, and guarantee of the emancipation of the second for his share; because each of them impaired his share: the first by his tadbir, and the second by his emancipation. However, he may take the guarantee from the mudabbar, not from the emancipator, because the guarantee of tadbir is a guarantee of exchange, unlike the guarantee of emancipation. [4] His saying: 'but...' Explanation of restricting the guarantee to the one who made mudabbar after the emancipation had [occurred].
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on al-Wiqayah, addressing two scenarios in partial emancipation (ʿitq al-baʿḍ). The first scenario: a stranger buys half of a slave, then the slave's father buys the other half. Abu Hanifa (r.a.) holds that the father must either guarantee the stranger's share or the slave earns his freedom by working (saʿyah), and the stranger has a choice if the father is wealthy. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) disagree: they say that since the emancipator (the father) is wealthy, no earning is required, and only guarantee is due. The commentator explains that the partner (stranger) did not consent to the impairment of his share because his purchase preceded the father's, so he retains more rights. The second scenario involves three co-owners: one makes the common slave a mudabbar (tadbir), another emancipates his share, and the third remains silent. If both the mudabbar and the silent one seek guarantee, the mudabbar guarantees the silent one, and the emancipator guarantees the mudabbar. This is Abu Hanifa's position, because he considers tadbir divisible (mutajazzi), unlike the Sahibayn who view it as indivisible. The commentator details that the silent one has two guarantee claims: one against the mudabbar (for impairing his share by tadbir) and one against the emancipator, but he can only collect from the mudabbar because tadbir guarantee is like exchange (daman muʿāwaḍa), whereas emancipation guarantee is not. The discussion highlights the Hanafi principle that participation in the cause of emancipation (sharing in the ʿillah of ʿitq) makes one deemed consenting to the impairment of another's share, and ignorance of the law is no excuse.

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbir): A contract where a master declares that his slave will be free upon the master's death.
ضمان (daman): Financial compensation or guarantee paid by a co-owner who impairs another's share through emancipation or tadbir.
سعاية (saʿyah): The slave's earning of his freedom by working for the co-owner whose share was impaired, until he pays off the value.
متجزئ (mutajazzi): Divisible; capable of applying to a portion of a slave rather than the whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if one of three co-owners makes a slave mudabbar and another emancipates his share, whom can the silent co-owner claim guarantee from?
A) Only the mudabbar
B) Only the emancipator
C) Both the mudabbar and the emancipator
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
In partial emancipation, the divisibility of tadbir determines the guarantee obligations among co-owners, with the mufta bihi giving the silent partner a claim only against the mudabbar.
